201 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brodsky v. Murphy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 518; 255 N.E.2d 700; 307 N.Y.S.2d 435; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 908


September 25, 1969, Argued  
December 11, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent members of the state tax commission refused to direct the payment of interest on the refund of the mortgage taxes required in an earlier proceeding. The special term held that petitioner taxpayers had no right to interest absent a provision therefor in the mortgage tax refund statute. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) affirmed on a procedural ground, and the taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: In an earlier proceeding, it was determined that the mortgage taxes were erroneously imposed on a supplemental mortgage that was entitled to an exemption under N.Y. Tax Law § 263. The court affirmed. The court discussed the rule that interest was required where the tax imposed was void and had no support except an unconstitutional statute. The court held that this rule did not apply to taxes erroneously collected under an otherwise valid statute. In such cases, there could have been many countervailing circumstances for the erroneous collection and payment, not always the responsibility of the tax collector. Thus, the court held that where the tax was erroneously imposed under a valid statute, interest was only authorized if explicitly provided for by statute. Because § 263 was silent on whether interest should or could be paid on tax refunds, interest was not authorized in this case. The court did not reach the procedural issue addressed by the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order, holding that the taxpayers were not entitled to interest on the refund of mortgage taxes.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- interest on refund of taxes -- taxpayer is not entitled to interest on refund of taxes improperly paid unless either statute directs payment of interest or taxes were collected under void statute; here, since taxes were collected under valid statute erroneously interpreted, appellants are not entitled to interest from time of payment.

A taxpayer, entitled to a refund of taxes improperly paid and collected by the State, is not entitled to interest on the refund from the time of payment of the taxes unless either the statute authorizing the refund or other tax statute directs the payment of interest thereon or the taxes were collected under an unconstitutional statute void ab initio. In the present case, since the taxes were collected under a valid tax statute erroneously interpreted, appellants are not entitled to interest from the time of payment but only from the time of judgment ( CPLR 5003).

 

202 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Klein v. Krueger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 497; 255 N.E.2d 552; 307 N.Y.S.2d 207; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 923


September 29, 1969, Argued  
December 10, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant warden challenged a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's judgment dismissing a writ of habeas corpus brought by respondent relator and granted the application to the extent that bail was fixed in the amount of $ 125,000.


OVERVIEW: Relator sought a writ of habeas corpus. The trial court dismissed the writ, and relator appealed. The appellate court reversed and granted the application to the extent that bail was fixed in the amount of $ 125,000, and the warden appealed. The court held that the appellate court did not err in fixing bail instead of upholding the denial of bail. The critical support for the denial of bail was the danger to potential witnesses. Considering the conspiratorial nature of the alleged crime and its perpetrators, it was not rational to believe that the witnesses would be safer if the 60-year old relator, long-time resident in his community, and a grandfather, steadily employed, were kept in prison. The court held that in consideration of the fact that relator was in a position to put up high bail and of the nature of the crimes, relator's background, and of other information, the appellate court did not err in fixing the amount of bail at $ 125,000. The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment reversing the dismissal of relator's writ of habeas corpus and fixing bail.


HEADNOTES

Bail -- denial of bail -- in habeas corpus proceeding, court may review denial of bail by criminal court if constitutional standards inhibiting excessive bail or arbitrary denial of bail are violated; here, Appellate Division properly exercised discretion in habeas corpus to admit relator to bail in sum of $ 125,000 on finding that reasons for denial of bail were inadequate.

In a habeas corpus proceeding, the court may review the action of the criminal court at nisi prius in denying bail or fixing the amount of bail if it appears that the constitutional or statutory standards inhibiting excessive bail or the arbitrary refusal of bail are violated. The nisi prius criminal court (County Court) here denied bail to relator, held on an indictment for robbery, kidnapping and other related crimes arising out of a serious and involved offense, the court stating that it was taking into consideration the danger of defendant's flight. Special Term, in denying the writ, took into consideration the danger to potential witnesses. Since it is not rational to believe that witnesses would be safer if this 60-year-old relator, a grandfather steadily employed, were kept in prison, the Appellate Division properly fixed bail, although because of other elements in the case and in the exercise of discretion, in the amount of $ 125,000.

 

203 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCaleb


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 394; 255 N.E.2d 136; 306 N.Y.S.2d 889; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 963


October 9, 1969, Argued  
November 26, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed and case remitted to the Criminal Court of the City of New York for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted in separate cases of unauthorized use of a vehicle in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 165.05(1). The Appellate Term of the Supreme Court for the Second and Eleventh Judicial District in the Second Judicial Department (New York) reversed, and the state sought review.


OVERVIEW: Each defendant was found in vehicles which had been taken and moved without the owners' permission. One defendant was found in the rear seat with the engine off, and the other defendant was found asleep in the passenger seat with the motor running. The state claimed that defendants' conduct constituted unauthorized use of a vehicle as there was a presumption one who used a vehicle without the owner's consent knew he did not have consent. Under N.Y. Penal Law § 165.05 a person was guilty of unauthorized use of a vehicle if knowing he did not have consent of the owner took operated, exercised control over, rode in or otherwise used a vehicle. Defendants alleged that § 165.05 should have been narrowly construed and only referred to vehicles in motion. The court reversed and remanded for new trials finding there was a fair, natural, and rational connection between the fact that the cars were used without the owners' permission and the presumption that those in a car were aware they did not have permission.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision that reversed defendants' convictions and remanded the cases for new trials.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- unauthorized use of vehicle -- convictions for unauthorized use of vehicle (Penal Law, § 165.05, subd. 1) improperly reversed on law -- statute makes occupation of another's motor vehicle, without consent, criminal without regard to whether vehicle is in motion -- statutory presumption constitutionally valid.

1. Convictions of defendants for class A misdemeanor of unauthorized use of a vehicle (Penal Law, § 165.05, subd. 1) were improperly reversed on the law. New trials are ordered.

2. This statute makes criminal the occupation of another's motor vehicle, without consent, without regard to whether the vehicle is in motion.

3. The presumption contained in the statute presents a rational connection between the fact proven -- nonconsent -- and the fact presumed -- knowledge of nonconsent -- and is constitutionally valid.

4. Although the Appellate Term's reversal and dismissal in each case "on the law" was error, its reversal "on the * * * facts" is not reviewable by the court. Such reversal on the facts, however, does not require the dismissal to stand but results in the ordering of a new trial.

Plaza Management Co. v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 630; 254 N.E.2d 227; 306 N.Y.S.2d 11; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1003


October 3, 1969, Argued  
October 30, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which modified orders of a lower court in the owners' declaratory judgment action against respondent city rent agency by directing the entry, in each action, of a judgment declaring that an amendment in New York City, N.Y., Local Law 41 (1967) was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: New York City, N.Y., Local Law 41 (1967) provided that only in the case of property sold after a certain date could the purchase price paid be used in determining the net return therefrom. The owner's properties did not qualify. On appeal, the court held that (1) the dissent's position was virtually identical to one rejected in previous precedent, and in response to its contention that the use of special equalization ratios was somehow inconsistent with the use of property's assessed value to determine a fair return, it sufficed to point out that the equalization ratio, a borough-wide average, did not purport to measure or reflect the value of any particular property; (2) the court could not conclude, merely on the basis of the special equalization ratio, that one owner's property was actually worth its equalized assessed value or that its return would only amount to 4.7 percent; and (3) although the special equalization allowed the city to impose a higher tax rate than was otherwise constitutionally permissible, real estate taxes were part of the operating expenses of a building, passed directly on to tenants under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y51-5.0.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower appellate court.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- in actions by owners of properties purchased prior to February 1, 1961, for judgments declaring unconstitutional City Rent and Rehabilitation Law, as amended by Local Law No. 41 of City of New York of 1967, which provided that, upon application for fair return rent adjustment, purchase price paid for property, rather than its assessed valuation, might be used as valuation base in determining net return therefrom, if, among other things, sale occurred between February 1, 1961 and time of filing application, Appellate Division declared that amendment embraced in said local law was constitutional -- order of Appellate Division affirmed -- Court of Appeals agrees with opinion at Appellate Division -- as to argument, not treated below but made in Court of Appeals dissent, that city's use of special equalization ratios to compute taxes was inconsistent with use of unequalized assessed value to determine fair return, position taken appears to be virtually identical to contention considered and rejected by Court of Appeals in Matter of Hartley Holding Corp. v. Gabel (13 N Y 2d 306). 

 

206 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pagnotta


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 333; 253 N.E.2d 202; 305 N.Y.S.2d 484; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1013; 48 A.L.R.3d 1264


September 25, 1969, Argued  
October 29, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which upheld his conviction for loitering with intent to use or possess narcotics, in violation of former N.Y. Penal Law § 1533(5).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for unlawful loitering, contending that his guilt was not established beyond a reasonable doubt and that the statute he was convicted of violating was unconstitutional because it penalized noncriminal behavior. The court rejected both arguments, and affirmed defendant's conviction. Defendant, a known narcotics addict, was charged with having loitered for the purpose of using or possessing narcotics after he was seen holding, and then dropping upon police arrival, a bottlecap containing a liquid while seated inside a building's stairwell. The court held that this evidence was sufficient to permit the inference that defendant's purpose in loitering was to use narcotics, irrespective of what was actually inside the bottle cap. It also held that the statute was not impermissibly vague and did not punish noncriminal behavior.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding defendant's conviction was affirmed. There was sufficient evidence to convict him, given the totality of the circumstances, and the statute under which he was convicted was not unconstitutional because it regulated not mere loitering but loitering with an illegal purpose.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering for purpose of using narcotic drug -- defendant's conviction of loitering for purpose of possessing or using narcotic drug in violation of subdivision 5 of section 1533 of former Penal Law was properly affirmed -- unnecessary for People to prove that liquid spilled by defendant when he saw police officer was heroin -- prosecutor's consent to dismissal of charges against defendant's companion who had empty glassine envelopes does not require that possession of drug be proven -- circumstances of gathering, possession of narcotic implements by defendant's companion and expert testimony of police officer as to use of "cookers" by addicts support inference that defendant and companions had gathered to take narcotic drugs -- subdivision 5 of section 1533 of former Penal Law is constitutional.

1. Defendant was convicted of loitering for the purpose of possessing or using a narcotic drug in violation of subdivision 5 of section 1533 of the former Penal Law. An officer came upon defendant and two others on a stair landing in a building. Defendant had a bottle cap containing a liquid in his hand and one of the others had a hypodermic needle and an eyedropper. A third had glassine envelopes which were empty. When defendant saw the officer he dropped the bottle cap, spilling the liquid, none of which was recovered. The officer testified that bottle caps are used as "cookers" in which heroin and water are mixed, and the mixture is then drawn up into an eyedropper and injected into the vein by means of a hypodermic needle. Defendant's conviction was properly affirmed.

2. It was unnecessary for the People to prove that the unrecovered liquid was in fact heroin so long as it was shown that defendant and his friends had congregated for the purpose of using or possessing drugs.

3. The District Attorney's consent to the dismissal of charges against the possessor of the glassine envelopes does not mandate that possession be proven nor may it be raised as some sort of collateral estoppel in favor of defendant herein.

4. While the People were unable to actually prove that the liquid in the "cooker" was a narcotic drug, the circumstances of the gathering, the possession of narcotic implements by one of defendant's companions and the expert testimony of the police officer as to the use of "cookers" by narcotic addicts support the inference that defendant and his companions had gathered to take narcotic drugs. While the evidence is circumstantial, the facts support no other inference but guilt.

5. Subdivision 5 of section 1533 of the former Penal Law provides that a person who "loiters about any stairway * * * or any passageway of a building for the purpose of unlawfully using or possessing any narcotic drug" is guilty of a misdemeanor. The statute is not unconstitutionally vague and is a reasonable restriction upon the individual for the public good. The statute sought to prevent idle persons engaged in the unlawful traffic in narcotics from gathering together for that purpose in places frequented by other citizens, thereby endangering public health, morals and tranquility.

 

207 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Abate v. Mundt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 309; 253 N.E.2d 189; 305 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1677


September 23, 1969, Argued  
October 8, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term that was in favor of appellee county board of supervisors in an action involving voter re-districting.


OVERVIEW: The board was order to devise a plan the would satisfy the requirements of the Equal Protection Clause and to submit the plan to the voters of the county for their approval. Pursuant to that order, three plans were devised and submitted to the voters but were rejected at the polls. A taxpayer then filed an action to compel the board to reapportion in accordance with constitutional requirements. The board submitted a plan, but that plan was rejected at Special Term. The board then submitted another plan, that the lower court approved. The taxpayer appealed and the court affirmed. The court found that the population variance contained in the plan was within permissible limits and the practical and historical justification for the variance was sufficient because the plan did not ignore population equality, but achieved substantial equality within the context of a long established town government framework, thus accommodating both constitutional and practical considerations. Also, the court held that the plan was constitutional because the taxpayer failed to show that the plan's multimember districts constituted an invidious discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that was in favor of the board.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- plan of apportionment for County Legislature created 5 districts corresponding to 5 constituent towns; smallest receives one representative; population of remaining 4 is divided by that of smallest and receives number of representatives resulting from division plus one for major fraction; there is variation of 12% in number of people per legislator; plan is bona fide application of "one man-one vote" principle on local level and deviation is not unconstitutional -- largest district has 6 representatives; voter in such district votes for 6 representatives; his vote is equal in weight to that of any other citizen -- Special Term properly modified plan so as to require that, if Town Supervisor wished to be County Legislator, he must be elected separately (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10).

1. A plan of apportionment of a county provides for a County Legislature composed, on the basis of population, of 18 members chosen from 5 districts which correspond to the county's 5 constituent towns. The smallest district receives one representative. The population of the 4 remaining districts is divided by that of the smallest district and receives the number of representatives equal to the whole number resulting from the division plus any major fraction. There is a variation of 12% in the number of people per legislator. Deviations from strict population equality may be broader in scope when dealing with local, intra-State legislative bodies. Accordingly the population variance here is within permissible limits in view of the objective of retaining the outlines of the long-established town government. The plan represents a balanced, bona fide application of the "one man-one vote" principle to the needs and circumstances of local government and is not unconstitutionally defective.

2. The largest district has 6 representatives while the smallest has one. Since each voter in the largest district votes for 6 representatives to represent his interests, his vote is equal in weight to that of any other citizen in the county. No showing has been made that the plan operates to minimize or cancel out the voting strength of a racial or political element of the voting population.

3. The plan provided that a person elected to the office of Town Supervisor would automatically be entitled to a seat in the County Legislature. Special Term properly modified this provision so as to require that, if a Town Supervisor also wished to be a County Legislator, he must be elected separately. Section 10 of the Municipal Home Rule Law provides that "a plan of apportionment adopted by a County * * * may provide that * * * supervisors of towns * * * shall be eligible to be elected as members of the county legislative body". The use of the term "elected" indicates a legislative intent that a Town Supervisor could serve as a County Legislator only if he prevailed in an election for the office of County Legislator.

 

208 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nuernberger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 179; 250 N.E.2d 352; 303 N.Y.S.2d 74; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1110


June 3, 1969, Argued  
July 2, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v. Nuernberger: Judgment modified in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.

In People ex rel. Nuernberger v. McKendrick: Appeal dismissed as moot, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) denying his application for a writ of habeas corpus after he was convicted of assault in the second degree and of impairing the morals of a minor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of assault in the second degree and of impairing the morals of a minor. The crimes were committed on petitioner's 11-year-old daughter. The assault as a felony depended upon an intent to commit a crime, incest. The lower court denied petitioner's application for habeas corpus relief. On appeal, the court found that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the conviction for assault and for impairing the morals of a minor. However, the court found that there was a jurisdictional defect in that the assault charge should have been tried in the Family Court. The court explained that the Family Court had exclusive original jurisdiction over any proceeding concerning acts that would constitute an assault between a parent and a child.


OUTCOME: The court reversed petitioner's conviction for assault and remanded the case for a new trial. The court affirmed petitioner's conviction for impairing the morals of a minor. The court dismissed petitioner's appeal from the denial of habeas corpus relief as moot.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Family Court -- assault -- Family Court had exclusive original jurisdiction over assault as felony which depended on intent to commit incest -- Family Court did not have exclusive original jurisdiction of charge of endangering morals of minor.

1. Defendant was convicted in the County Court both of assault and of impairing the morals of a minor. The assault as a felony depended on an intent to commit a crime -- incest. The Family Court "has exclusive original jurisdiction" over "any proceeding" concerning "acts which would constitute * * * an assault * * * between parent and child" (Family Ct. Act, § 812; cf. N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 13, subd. b). If there is to be a prosecution for an assault within the enumerated family group of any degree or for any purpose, the prosecution must begin in the Family Court. The judgment of conviction for assault should be reversed and that charge should be transferred to the Family Court.

2. Since the offense of endangering the morals of a minor does not itself come within the statutory definition of either assault or disorderly conduct and was essentially a different crime from either, the Family Court did not have exclusive original jurisdiction of that charge.

210 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Baldwin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 66; 250 N.E.2d 62; 302 N.Y.S.2d 571; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1150


May 28, 1969, Argued  
July 1, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and case remitted to the Criminal Court of the City of New York for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Appellate Term, Second Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment from the trial court that convicted defendant for illegal possession of narcotics. Prior to his conviction, the trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Without a search or arrest warrant, a police officer arrested defendant on a burglary charge. When he was placed under arrest, defendant dropped a white envelope with his name and address on it. The officer retrieved and opened the envelope and found two pills, which defendant stated contained amphetamine or dolophine. The officer charged defendant with possession of narcotics. Defendant brought a motion to suppress the evidence, which the trial court denied. Defendant was convicted for possession of narcotics. On appeal, defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred in denying the motion to suppress. The court reversed the conviction and held that: (1) absent a valid abandonment of the property, a warrantless search not authorized by consent was reasonable only if conducted as incident to a lawful arrest; (2) even if the facts warranted a finding of an abandonment, there was no abandonment as a matter of law where defendant was unlawfully arrested in the first instance; and (3) the mere dropping of a white envelope with defendant's name and address on it, without more, was not sufficient to establish probable cause to have believed that a crime was being committed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for possession of narcotics and remanded the cause to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- illegal possession of narcotics -- defendant improperly convicted of illegal possession of narcotics -- police officer obtained evidence without search warrant and while defendant was under arrest on unrelated burglary charge, later dismissed for lack of evidence -- evidence should have been suppressed -- no abandonment -- People failed to come forward with evidence to show probable cause.

1. Defendant was improperly convicted of illegal possession of narcotics. While under arrest on an unrelated burglary charge, defendant dropped a white envelope which had his name and address on it. The arresting officer retrieved the envelope and, upon opening it, found two pills, and the officer thereupon charged the defendant with possession of narcotics. The police officer had neither an arrest nor search warrant for the defendant, and the burglary case was apparently dismissed for lack of evidence. The evidence should have been suppressed. In the absence of a valid abandonment of the property, a search, not authorized by consent or a search warrant, is reasonable only if conducted as incident to a lawful arrest.

2. The facts do not disclose the requisite intention to constitute an abandonment. Moreover, there could be no abandonment as a matter of law if the defendant had been unlawfully arrested in the first instance.

3. The People must, in order to make out a prima facie case at the suppression hearing, come forward with some evidence to show probable cause. They may not simply assert that the defendant was under arrest and that the search was conducted pursuant to that arrest.

 

211 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cohn v. Borchard Affiliations


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 237; 250 N.E.2d 690; 303 N.Y.S.2d 633; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1155


May 26, 1969, Argued  
July 1, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed, with costs, and case remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two unrelated matters were consolidated for appeal because they both dealt with the constitutionality of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216, which provided for involuntary dismissal of an action for failure or neglect to prosecute. Appellants in both matters were plaintiffs whose cases were dismissed under Rule 3216 by orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which granted Rule 3216 motions to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 provided for the involuntary dismissal of an action, but only after a defendant served written demand upon plaintiff requiring plaintiff to serve and file a note of issue within 45 days after receipt of such demand. Two unrelated appeals were consolidated because both dealt with the constitutionality of Rule 3216. Appellants in both matters were plaintiffs whose cases were dismissed by the lower court, even though the respondent defendants in both cases had not filed the 45-day demand. The lower court held that the 45-day demand provision was unconstitutional because it interfered with the court's power to dismiss a complaint for lack of prosecution. The court reversed the decisions of the lower courts and held that Rule 3216 constituted a valid exercise of the legislative power.The court found that the procedural device of dismissing complaints or actions for undue delay was a legislative creation and not, as was urged, a product of the court's inherent power.


OUTCOME: The court reversed lower court's decision to dismiss appellant plaintiffs' actions. The court held that statutory provision, which directed that a 45-day demand be served upon a plaintiff before the plaintiff's case could be involuntarily dismissed, was constitutional. The court found that procedural device of dismissing complaints or actions for undue delay was a legislative creation and not a product of the court's inherent power.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and nonsuit -- dismissal for delay -- CPLR 3216 which provides that defendant, before moving to dismiss complaint for failure or neglect to prosecute action, must serve upon plaintiff written demand to serve and file note of issue within 45 days, is constitutional -- no provision in State Constitution prohibiting Legislature from so acting -- procedural device of dismissing complaints for undue delay was legislative creation and not product of court's inherent power.

1. CPLR 3216 provides that a defendant, before moving to dismiss the complaint for failure or neglect to prosecute the action, must serve upon the plaintiff "a written demand * * * requiring [him] * * * to serve and file a note of issue within forty-five days after receipt of such demand." The opportunity which the statute affords to correct a procedural default presents no significant constitutional issue. There is no language in the State Constitution which limits the legislative power to act in this area. On the contrary, section 30 of article VI of the Constitution explicitly grants the Legislature broad power to formulate rules of procedure, restricting the courts to "the adoption of regulations * * * consistent with the general practice and procedure as provided by statute or general rules." It does limit the Legislature's power to that which it "has heretofore exercised". Nevertheless, the language of the Constitution leaves little room for doubt that the power to make rules of procedure lies principally with the Legislature and not with the courts.

2. The particular procedure involved is not such a fundamental part of a court's inherent power that it is beyond the competence of the Legislature to limit or circumscribe its availability. The procedural device of dismissing complaints or actions for undue delay was a legislative creation and not a product of the court's inherent power.

3. To hold CPLR 3216 invalid because it interferes with court calendars would throw into jeopardy numerous other provisions of the broad and comprehensive procedural scheme which constitutes the CPLR.

People v. Capparelli

213 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Donaldson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 38; 250 N.E.2d 46; 302 N.Y.S.2d 549; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1177


June 2, 1969, Argued  
June 12, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v. Donaldson: Order affirmed.

In People v. Harris: Order affirmed.

In People v. Riley: Judgment reversed and case remitted to the Criminal Court of the City of New York for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein and the opinion in People v. Fuller (24 N Y 2d 292).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeals were filed in three separate criminal prosecutions that took place in three separate trial courts (New York), wherein a determination concerning the retroactive applicability of new case law precedent, which related to the disposition of addiction hearings, was sought.


OVERVIEW: In each of the three cases, defendants were convicted upon their plea of guilty to a drug possession violation and a hearing, pursuant to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 208, was held before a judge sitting without a jury. In each case, defendant was certified an addict and committed to the custody of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission. The issue on appeal was whether new case law precedent, which declared that it was a constitutional equal protection violation to deny such defendants an opportunity for a jury trial in criminal narcotics commitment cases, when such opportunity was afforded in civil narcotic commitment cases, applied retroactively? The People argued that the hearings at issue were fairly conducted; that retroactive application of the relevant case law would have imposed severe additional burdens on the courts; and, that law enforcement officials and courts had relied upon a statute which was presumptively constitutional. None of those arguments, however, was sufficient to overcome the considerations militating towards the retroactive application of the applicable case. The court therefore gave the case retroactive effect.


OUTCOME: The judgments in the first and second action were affirmed. The judgment in the third action was reversed and the matter was remitted to the appropriate trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- drug addiction -- decision in People v. Fuller (24 N Y 2d 292), which declared that there was no rational basis for distinction between "criminal" and "civil" commitment for narcotic addicts in grant of jury trial, should be applied retroactively.

The decision in People v. Fuller (24 N Y 2d 292), which held that there was no rational basis for a distinction, as to the opportunity for a jury trial, between "criminal" and "civil" commitment for narcotic addicts and that, therefore, the denial of such an opportunity to the former, while expressly granting it to the latter, violated the constitutional guarantee of the equal protection of the laws, should be applied retroactively.

Carter v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


25 N.Y.2d 7; 250 N.E.2d 30; 302 N.Y.S.2d 525; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1200


May 16, 1969, Argued  
June 5, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Commissioner of Education and Board of Regents, sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 314, modified a judgment by finding that respondent board of education's registration of a proposed high school was not barred.


OVERVIEW: The board of education applied to the Commissioner for a commitment of building aid funds for the construction of a high school in its district. The Commissioner, pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 100.2(h), denied the application. The trial court, in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, affirmed the Commissioner's decision. The appellate division, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 314, modified the judgment and declared that the high school registration was not barred. The Commissioner and the Board of Regents appealed. The court affirmed the order because the Commissioner did not have statutory authorization to bar registration of the board of education's proposed high school.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the modification of the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- registration of high school -- regulation of Commissioner of Education ( 8 NYCRR 100.2[h]) which provides that no public high school shall be recommended for registration if, in judgment of Commissioner of Education, establishment of school would conflict with approved plan for district reorganization has no express or implied statutory authority.

1. A regulation of the Commissioner of Education ( 8 NYCRR 100.2[h]) which provides that "[no] public high school shall be recommended for registration by the Commissioner of Education * * * if, in his judgment, the establishment of such school would conflict with an approved plan for district reorganization" has no express statutory authority and it could not be used as the basis for refusing to register petitioners' high school. No statute in any way sanctions the use of the refusal to register a proposed high school as a method for obtaining compliance with an approved plan for school district reorganization.

2. The regulation of the Commissioner cannot be found to have any implied authorization since any such implication would be contrary to the policy expressed by the Legislature in section 3602 of the Education Law which substituted incentives for compliance for penalties for noncompliance.

Thomas v. United Air Lines, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 714; 249 N.E.2d 755; 301 N.Y.S.2d 973; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1234; 39 A.L.R.3d 187


March 4, 1969, Argued  
May 28, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed, with costs, and case remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, decedents' representatives, sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a lower court judgment limiting recovery against defendant airline in each wrongful death action, as required by the law of the situs of the accident, in compliance with then existing federal law.


OVERVIEW: After the decedents were killed when defendant airline's plane crashed into Lake Michigan within the territorial boundary of the state of Illinois, plaintiffs, decedents' representatives, filed separate wrongful death actions in New York against defendant airline. In two actions, the trial court ruled against defendant's motion for summary judgment in order to dismiss all claims in excess of the $ 30,000 limitation imposed by the Illinois wrongful death statute. In the other two actions, plaintiffs' motion to dismiss the affirmative defenses alleging the same limitation was granted. The appellate court considered plaintiffs' appeals together, reversed the denial of defendant's motions, and granted partial summary judgment dismissing all claims in excess of $ 30,000. The appellate court certified questions for review. The court held that actions for wrongful death, whether or not they were classified as a maritime tort, were not restricted by the Illinois $ 30,000 limitation. The court held that the location of the accident was not the sole factor for applying the wrongful death statute of a particular state and that Illinois did not govern these actions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment in favor of defendant airline and answered certified questions in the negative. The court remitted the actions to the trial court for the employ of a standard choice of law analysis to determine which wrongful death statute governed the actions brought by plaintiffs, decedents' representatives.


HEADNOTES

Conflict of laws -- airplane accident -- plaintiffs' decedents, residents of New Jersey, Connecticut and Iowa, were killed when defendant's airplane on commercial flight from New York crashed into Lake Michigan, within territorial boundary of Illinois -- Appellate Division in error in determining that law of Illinois, with its $ 30,000 limitation, applies to these actions.

Plaintiffs' decedents were killed when defendant's airplane which was on a regularly scheduled nonstop commercial flight originating at New York and terminating in Chicago, Illinois, crashed into Lake Michigan, within the territorial boundary of the State of Illinois. One of the passengers resided in New Jersey, and was employed by a New York concern and worked in New York. Another lived in Connecticut and commuted each day to his place of employment in New York. Two others were resident of Iowa and were returning home from New York. The appeals were taken on certified questions, limiting the court to a determination of whether the Appellate Division properly concluded that the Illinois wrongful death statute, with its $ 30,000 limitation, is applicable to each of these actions. An examination of Federal law, the law of this State, and the law of Illinois requires the conclusion that the Appellate Division was in error in determining that the law of Illinois applies to these four actions.

217 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Original R. Tyson, Inc. v. Tyler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 671; 249 N.E.2d 453; 301 N.Y.S.2d 602; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1684


April 15, 1969, Argued  
May 14, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ticket broker sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which confirmed the suspension of the ticket broker's license by respondent Commissioner of Licenses of the City of New York and dismissed the ticket broker's petition.


OVERVIEW: The ticket broker had a license from the New York City Department of Licenses to sell tickets for the purpose of resale. It sold tickets to a ticket broker in Washington, D.C. for resale. Washington, D.C. did not have a ticket broker licensing law. Pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Dept. Lic. R. 6(f), the Commissioner suspended the ticket broker's license for selling tickets to an unlicensed agency. The ticket broker filed a petition for review. The appellate division confirmed the suspension and dismissed the petition. The ticket broker appealed. The court reversed the order and annulled the Commissioner's determination because the effect of the regulation was limited to New York City.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the confirmation order and annulled the Commissioner's suspension of the ticket broker's license.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- theatre tickets -- Commissioner of Licenses of City of New York not authorized to completely prohibit trade in theatre tickets with agencies located outside City of New York -- regulation of Department of Licenses having such effect, invalid.

The petitioner, licensed by the New York City Department of Licenses to sell, for purposes of resale, tickets of admission to theatres or places of public amusement, had been selling theatre tickets to an established theatre ticket agency in Washington, D. C., at a price lawful under the laws of New York. A regulation of the Department of Licenses prohibiting a licensed broker from consigning or selling tickets to an "unlicensed person" for resale has the effect of proscribing the sale of tickets to any ticket broker or agency located in any place outside of New York City. This exercise of power exceeds the authority delegated to the Commissioner of Licenses to make rules and regulations "as may be deemed necessary for the protection of the public" (General Business Law, § 169-b) against "fraud, extortion, exorbitant rates and similar abuses" (General Business Law, § 167). A statute, the sole object of which is to assure that licensed ticket brokers do not resell tickets at amounts in excess of a specified price, does not authorize a city official to completely prohibit trade in such tickets with agencies located outside the City of New York.

219 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. La Bello


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 598; 249 N.E.2d 412; 301 N.Y.S.2d 544; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1327


January 21, 1969, Argued  
April 24, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted them, upon their pleas of guilty, of attempted bribery as a felony.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, who had previously been convicted of assaulting a witness, were granted immunity to testify before a grand jury which was investigating a conspiracy to intimidate witnesses who were to testify at a probe of violations of law. They answered all of the questions and admitted that they had been hired to commit the assault. However, they denied that they knew who their principals were. Four days after defendants testified, the policeman who arrested them was called before the same grand jury. The policeman testified that defendants had attempted to bribe him not to be a witness against them and to get rid of the evidence. Defendants were then indicted for bribery and convicted, on their pleas of guilty, of attempted bribery. On appeal, the lower court affirmed the convictions. The court affirmed the judgment by holding that defendants' Fifth Amendment rights were not violated by their bribery indictments, pursuant to the former Penal Law § 2447, because whatever evidence might have been revealed by their grand jury testimony was wholly insubstantial, in that the indictments were the result of the policeman's testimony based on his personal knowledge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendants, upon their pleas of guilty, of attempted bribery as a felony.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- attempted bribery -- defendants properly convicted on pleas of guilty of attempted bribery -- immunity granted before Grand Jury did not encompass crime here charged -- section 2447 of former Penal Law bars only use of witness' testimony and any evidence derived therefrom.

1. Defendants, called before a Grand Jury investigating a conspiracy to intimidate witnesses who were to testify at a probe of violations of law involving public contracts, were granted immunity. They answered all questions and admitted they had been hired to commit an assault, but denied that they knew who their principals were. The policeman who had arrested defendants later testified to the same Grand Jury that defendants had tried to bribe him. Defendants were properly convicted on their pleas of guilty of attempted robbery.

2. The immunity granted before the Grand Jury did not encompass the crime charged in the present indictment. The police officer's testimony was in no way derived from anything said by defendants' to the Grand Jury and it established a prima facie case of bribery. The indictment was, therefore, based on sufficient and untainted evidence. Even if it be assumed that defendants' testimony was related to the bribery, the indictment was not the product of that testimony. Section 2447 of the former Penal Law bars only the use of the witness' testimony and any evidence derived therefrom.

3. It is immaterial whether or not defendants were the "targets" of this investigation. Although a defendant here did not assert his privilege against self incrimination, he was granted immunity before he could. This action of the Grand Jury could not operate to deny this defendant complete protection from any use of his testimony against him. His testimony could not furnish the basis for a finding of an indictment, and the record demonstrates that it did not.

 

220 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rye v. Metropolitan Transp. Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 627; 249 N.E.2d 429; 301 N.Y.S.2d 569; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1330


March 6, 1969, Argued  
April 24, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, without costs, and case remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city and intervenor town challenged a judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York County (New York), which had ruled that authorization to build bridges was in contravention of the state constitutional requirement that the public corporation authorized to do so must have been created by special act.


OVERVIEW: The legislature had amended the Public Authorities Law. Among other things, it created the Metropolitan Transportation Authority or continued a previously created authority under this name, N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 83. Appellant city and intervenor town sued the Metropolitan Transportation Authority for declaratory judgment, contending that ch. 717 of N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 96 was unconstitutional because it did not conform to the mandate of N.Y. Const. art. X, § 5, which provided that no public corporation having authority to contract indebtedness and to collect fees and charges shall have been created except by special act of the legislature. The trial court found for respondents, city and town, on the ground that the authorization to build bridges was in contravention of the constitutional requirement that the public corporation authorized to do so must have been created by special act. The court reversed, and held that it was manifest that appellant Metropolitan Transportation Authority was created by special act and that the implementation of its powers, including the right to build bridges, was a valid exercise of legislative authority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the trial court, which had ruled that the public corporation authorized to build bridges must have been created by special act, because the legislature had legal authority to implement the powers of its transportation authority to build bridges.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- general or special act -- chapter 717 of Laws of 1967 which created Metropolitan Transportation Authority not in conflict with article X (§ 5) of New York Constitution requiring creation of public corporation by special act -- Authority was created by special act within intention of constitutional provision -- additional asserted grounds of constitutional invalidity properly before court although not passed on at Special Term -- no separate causes of action -- act was not local bill (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 15) nor law in relation to property, affairs or government of town (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 2, subd. [b], par. [2].)

1. Chapter 717 of the Laws of 1967 which created the Metropolitan Transportation Authority is not in conflict with the provisions of section 5 of article X of the New York Constitution which provides that no public corporation having authority to contract indebtedness and to collect fees and charges shall be created except by special act of the Legislature, and is not unconstitutional.

2. What was intended by the constitutional provision was a requirement that the State Legislature itself create public corporations of the type described and that their creation not be left either to administrative officers or to local governments. The term "special act", as it is used here, can only mean that a public corporation of this type must be created by a particular enactment of establishment by the Legislature. It cannot mean that only one subject could be stated in the statute by which the corporation is created; or that in the statute creating the corporation the Legislature could not deal additionally with other matters, including the kinds of powers to be exercised or duties to be performed by the public corporation thus created. The Legislature created the Metropolitan Transportation Authority by a special act within the intention of section 5 of article X and the incidental power to build a State bridge was relevant to its corporate function and within the frame of legislative power.

3. The court at Special Term did not pass on additional asserted grounds of constitutional invalidity to the 1967 statute, but the additional grounds have been argued by the appellants, by the amicus curiae, by the intervenor town and by the plaintiff city. These additional objections are properly before the court and they should be determined.

4. The separate "causes of action" based on different constitutional provisions are not different causes of action but different legal and constitutional arguments based on precisely the same factual allegations.

5. Chapter 717 of the Laws of 1967 is not a "local bill" within section 15 of article III of the Constitution.

6. Nor in authorizing a State corporation to build a State bridge or in describing where a State highway is to be located is it a law within article IX (§ 2, subd. [b], par. [2]) in relation to the property, affairs or government of the Town of Oyster Bay.

7. The defendants' mistaken concession that the act was not a special act, not repeated in the Court of Appeals, does not bind the court to hold it unconstitutional.

 

221 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Finn's Liquor Shop, Inc. v. State Liquor Auth.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 647; 249 N.E.2d 440; 301 N.Y.S.2d 584; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1332


February 26, 1969, Argued  
April 24, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In the Matter of Finn's Liq. Shop v. State Liq. Auth.: Order affirmed.

In Matter of La Penta v. State Liq. Auth.: Order affirmed.

In Matter of Malik v. State Liq. Auth.: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Liquor Authority (authority) sought review of orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First and Fourth Judicial Departments (New York) in three cases consolidated on appeal. In the cases, the appellate court on the basis of evidence seized illegally under U.S. Const. amend. IV annulled the authority's determinations that suspended or cancelled the liquor licenses held by appellee licensees.


OVERVIEW: The authority contended that that the appellate court erred in applying an exclusionary rule to the administrative license proceedings. On appeal, the court held that the appellate court properly annulled the authority's determinations. The court found that the logic of the exclusionary rule for illegal searches and seizures under U.S. Const. amend. IV applied to administrative proceedings. Specifically, the court held that (1) an authority investigator impermissibly searched the pockets of a coat hanging in the rear of a liquor store, discovered credit sales slips and used the slips as evidence to suspend a liquor store license for making unlawful liquor sales on credit, (2) the authority improperly cancelled a hotel liquor license on the basis of evidence of gambling that had been determined by a county court in a criminal proceeding to have been the product of unauthorized wiretaps, and (3) the authority improperly suspended a liquor license on the basis of evidence of betting slips that was suppressed in a criminal proceeding brought against the license holder because the evidence was seized pursuant to an illegal search.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's orders in each of the three cases.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- licenses -- evidence -- exclusionary rule of Mapp v. Ohio (367 U.S. 643) applies to administrative proceedings before State Liquor Authority as well as to criminal prosecutions -- broad grant of statutory authority to State Liquor Authority to conduct searches without warrant would be unconstitutional -- State Liquor Authority's determination suspending liquor license, based on evidence which was product of unlawful search and seizure, properly annulled -- where, with regard to another petitioner, County Court had voided eavesdropping order and subsequently issued search warrant, petitioner not required to litigate validity of order and warrant again before State Liquor Authority -- in third proceeding, also, State Liquor Authority may not disregard court determination that evidence was fruit of illegal search and seizure.

1. The exclusionary rule of Mapp v. Ohio (367 U.S. 643) applies to administrative proceedings before the State Liquor Authority as well as to criminal prosecutions.

2. Two inspectors from the State Liquor Authority entered a liquor shop and asked the store manager, the only person present, if they could inspect the premises. When the manager told them to "go ahead," one of the inspectors went immediately to a room in the rear of the liquor store where a coat was hanging and went through the pockets of the coat and found sales slips indicating that sales had been made on credit. It is not clear that the Authority has a broad statutory power to make an administrative inspection of the licensed premises, including all items of personal property such as the coat in the rear room. In any event, such a broad grant of statutory authority to conduct warrantless searches would be unconstitutional. The investigator's entry into the back room of the store and, particularly, his search of the coat may not be constitutionally justified.

3. No basis exists for disturbing the conclusion reached by the Appellate Division that the questioning of the liquor store's principal, like all of the other items in evidence, was the product of the unlawful search and seizure, and that the Authority's determination, grounded on such evidence, must fall.

4. In the case of another licensee, evidence from his home was seized during a search made pursuant to a search warrant obtained after interception of telephone conversations pursuant to an ex parte eavesdropping order. A court thereafter voided the eavesdropping order and the search warrant. Since the search was unlawful per se in the absence of a valid warrant, the evidence obtained as a result of the search was inadmissible for any purpose and the Authority's determination based upon such evidence was, therefore, properly annulled.

5. Similarly, in the case of another licensee, from whose premises evidence was seized during a search which was made incidental to an arrest, the Authority may not disregard a court determinaton, made upon a motion to suppress the use of said evidence in criminal proceedings against the licensee, that the arrest to which the search was incidental was made without probable cause and the evidence must be suppressed. The court's decision is determinative of the issue and, although it is reviewable through the normal appellate process, neither the administrative hearing nor the article 78 proceeding to review it constitutes an appropriate proceeding to pass upon its propriety.

 

222 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Friedman v. State


Claim No. 44913


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 528; 249 N.E.2d 369; 301 N.Y.S.2d 484; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1340


January 23, 1969, Argued  
April 23, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and the case remitted to the Court of Claims for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein. Certified questions (1) and (2) answered in the affirmative and certified question (3) answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a judgment dismissing respondent supreme court justice's action to recover that portion of his salary payable from the date of his removal to the date of the filing of the claim.


OVERVIEW: Respondent supreme court justice brought an action to recover that portion of his salary payable by the state from the date of his removal to the date of the filing of the claim. The lower court granted the state's motion to dismiss the claim, and respondent appealed. The appellate court reversed, and the state appealed. The court held that the doctrine of res judicata did not estop respondent from collaterally attacking the subject matter jurisdiction of the Court on the Judiciary because there was no available means of appeal to review that question and because the determination as to its jurisdiction was a mere legal conclusion, not based upon any litigated fact. The court held that the claim failed to state a cause of action because respondent's removal was valid. The order appealed from was reversed and the order and judgment of the lower court dismissing the complaint was reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order and reinstated the lower court's order dismissing respondent supreme court justice's claim to recover that portion of his salary payable by the state from the date of his removal to the date of the filing of the claim, holding that the judgment of the Court on the Judiciary was not res judicata, and that the claim did not state a cause of action.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- removal -- legal conclusion of Court on Judiciary that it had jurisdiction of proceedings to remove claimant as Justice of Supreme Court, since constitutional provision under which claimant was removed provided no right of appeal, is subject to collateral attack in claim by Justice for unpaid salary from date of his removal; Court of Claims had jurisdiction of subject matter -- on direct appeal to Supreme Court of United States, that court's dismissal for want of substantial Federal question is equivalent to affirmance on merits; accordingly, removal was valid and claim fails to state cause of action -- assuming that Supreme Court's decision is not res judicata, procedural defects on which claimant attacks his removal do not reach jurisdictional dimensions -- phrase "for cause" in constitutional provision providing for removal is not vague and means legal cause -- Court on Judiciary was not at once complainant, prosecutor and judge.

1. Claimant, an elected Justice of the Supreme Court, was removed from his office by the Court on the Judiciary. By this claim, claimant seeks to recover that portion of his salary payable by the State from the date of his removal to the date of filing the claim, alleging that he was never legally removed from office. Since no appeal from the Court on the Judiciary is provided by section 9-a (now § 22) of article VI of the State Constitution, under which claimant was removed, the determination of the Court on the Judiciary is not res judicata. While a court's determination sustaining jurisdiction against attack is res judicata in a collateral action, it is not where a court's determination as to subject-matter jurisdiction is a legal conclusion. Accordingly, claimant is not prevented from asserting the instant claim, and the Court of Claims had jurisdiction of the subject matter of the action.

2. Claimant took a direct appeal from the Court on the Judiciary to the United States Supreme Court. That appeal was dismissed for want of a substantial Federal question. Such dismissal is equivalent of an affirmance on the merits insofar as Federal constitutional questions are involved. Accordingly, claimant's removal was valid and having had a final determination on the merits claimant is estopped from again litigating them and this claim fails to state a cause of action.

3. Assuming that the Supreme Court's dismissal is not res judicata, the procedural defects on which claimant attacks his removal do not reach jurisdictional dimensions and in no way deprived him of a fair trial.

4. That the term "for cause" is not defined by the constitutional provision authorizing the removal of a Judge does not render it void for vagueness. The phrase "for cause" means "legal cause" affecting or concerning the ability or fitness of the incumbent to perform the duty imposed upon him.

5. The functions of complainant, prosecutor and judge were not combined in the Court on the Judiciary in violation of claimant's constitutional rights. The Court on the Judiciary was not the accuser, for it was the report of a Supreme Court Justice which served as the complaint. Such court had no interest in the outcome of the case because it reviewed the allegations to determine whether basis existed for preferral of charges. The final charges were preferred by counsel designated by the court. It cannot be assumed that, because the court designated the attorneys, it became so intimately involved with the case as to deprive claimant of an impartial tribunal.

People v. Cooper


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 877; 249 N.E.2d 469; 301 N.Y.S.2d 624; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1344


February 24, 1969, Argued  
April 23, 1969, Decided

HEADNOTES

224 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sher


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 454; 248 N.E.2d 887; 301 N.Y.S.2d 46; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1364


January 24, 1969, Argued  
April 17, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction, as amended, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which convicted him on a jury's verdict finding him guilty of two counts of murder in the first degree arising out of a jewelry store holdup. The trial was had and the verdict was entered after defendant had been found to be not sufficiently capable of understanding the charges pending against him to make a defense and confer intelligently with counsel.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the only incident during the trial on which there was disagreement by members of the court as to whether its occurrence required a new trial was the anonymous communications by telephone with several of the jurors following the close of the People's case and before submission of the defense. The court found that the incident resulted in no palpable prejudice to defendant because each of the jurors during the trial was questioned whether he could sit impartially and confine his verdict to the record evidence, and each answered in the affirmative, despite the telephone calls to some jury members. The questioning and admonition of the jurors cured the possible harm under the accepted precedents because it served a real purpose in drawing their attention to the unfairness of their considering anything but the record evidence. The court found that the situation involved merely an anonymous or other irresponsible communication which a juror should easily understand was to be given no credence, if so advised and admonished. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of conviction for murder in the first degree.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment entering defendant's conviction for first degree murder.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder in first degree -- anonymous communications by telephone with jurors following close of People's case and before submission of defense did not prejudice defendant -- each of jurors related conversations and was questioned as to impartiality and replied that he would be impartial and confine verdict to record evidence.

Anonymous communications by telephone with several of the jurors following the close of the People's case and before the submission of the defense in a trial which resulted in defendant's conviction of murder in the first degree did not prejudice defendant. Each of the jurors during the trial related his own conversation with the anonymous caller and with his fellow jurors and was questioned as to whether he could sit impartially and confine his verdict to the record evidence, and each answered in the affirmative. Any possible harm was thus cured.

 

225 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Schanbarger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 288; 248 N.E.2d 16; 300 N.Y.S.2d 100; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1387


February 28, 1969, Argued  
April 10, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Rensselaer County Court (New York), which affirmed defendant's conviction for loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6).


OVERVIEW: A state trooper witnessed defendant walking in the early morning hours and asked him certain questions regarding defendant's identity and his destination. Defendant refused to answer the trooper's questions and was subsequently convicted of loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6). On appeal, the lower appellate court affirmed. On further appeal, the court reversed the conviction and ordered the information dismissed. The court held the information did not state that the trooper was justified in suspecting defendant might have been engaged or was about to engage in crime as was required by § 240.35(6). The court stated the trooper's testimony that defendant was walking in an area in which there had been frequent burglaries was insufficient to establish a reasonable suspicion of criminal activity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower appellate court, which affirmed defendant's conviction for loitering. The court ordered the information dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- defendant improperly convicted of violation of section 240.35 (subd. 6) of Penal Law -- information failed to refer to provision of section allegedly violated which states that loitering, remaining or wandering must be under circumstances which "justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime".

When a State trooper observed defendant walking along a highway in the early morning hours, the trooper stopped his vehicle and asked the man to come over to the car and tell him where he was going, where he was coming from and his identity and where he resided. The man replied that he did not think he had to answer the questions. The trooper informed the man that his refusal to answer the questions could result in his being arrested for loitering and when the man still refused to answer the questions, he was arrested and charged with violation of section 240.35 (subd. 6) of the Penal Law in that he "did remain & wander about in a public place without apparent reason under circumstances which justify suspicion" and that, upon inquiry of a peace officer, he refused to identify himself and failed to give a reasonable credible account of his conduct or purpose. The subdivision of the statute under which defendant was convicted contains clauses which are conjunctive, rather than disjunctive, and, in order to sustain a conviction under it, each of the conjunctive elements must be proved beyond a reasonable doubt. The information under which he was tried failed to refer to the terms of the section allegedly violated which states that the loitering, remaining or wandering must be under circumstances which "justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime". Defendant's failure to answer the trooper's questions did not constitute a violation of subdivision 6 of section 240.35.

 

226 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Di Piazza


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 342; 248 N.E.2d 412; 300 N.Y.S.2d 545; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1390


January 17, 1969, Argued  
April 10, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) which sustained his murder conviction. He claimed that he was deprived of a fair trial by pretrial publicity and by denial of his motion for a change of venue, and that evidence of his sanity at the time of the commission of the crime, found during a N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 658 hearing, was improperly admitted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for murder in the first degree. His motion for a change of venue due to pretrial publicity was denied. Upon motion of counsel, he was committed for psychiatric evaluation under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 658. The jury returned a verdict of guilty, and the appellate court affirmed. On review, the court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that the pretrial publicity was not of such a character as to require a change of venue because there was no evidence that newspaper coverage prejudiced his chance for a fair trial, and further, defense counsel did not use all his peremptory challenges and pronounced himself satisfied with the jury. The court further held that procedure employed in the examination of the prosecution's expert witnesses satisfied the requirements of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4515, and that defendant could not complain that the § 658 psychiatric evaluation violated his right against self-incrimination or to counsel because he himself requested it. Finally, the court affirmed the constitutionality of N.Y. Jud. Law § 663 because it did not allow a jury to fall short of the impartiality guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. VI, XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of murder in the first degree. The court held that the pretrial publicity was not of such a character as to require a change of venue, that procedure employed in the examination of the prosecution's expert witnesses satisfied statutory requirements, and that, having requested a mental examination, defendant could not complain that it violated his right against self-incrimination or to counsel.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder in first degree -- on defendant's conviction for murder in first degree, denial of motion for change of venue did not deprive defendant of fair trial -- proof of defendant's sanity not required on direct case -- report of psychiatrists was not received in evidence by "indirection" -- use upon trial of defendant's statements to psychiatrists while mental examination requested by defendant's counsel was going on was not objected to -- by requesting examination, defendant's privilege against self incrimination was not violated -- defense counsel knew that examination would cover defendant's mental condition at time crime was committed -- section 663 of Judiciary Law dealing with jurors who have conscientious scruples against death penalty is not unconstitutional.

1. On defendant's conviction for the crime of murder in the first degree, defendant was not deprived of a fair trial by the denial of his motion for a change of venue.

2. It was unnecessary for the People to prove on their direct case that defendant was sane. Proof of sanity upon rebuttal was sufficient to establish that fact upon the whole case.

3. There was more than sufficient evidence from which the jury could conclude that defendant was sane and able to premeditate the crime and intend to kill the victim.

4. There is no support for defendant's claim that the report of the psychiatrists was improperly received in evidence by "indirection".

5. After he was indicted, defendant, at the request of his counsel, was committed to a State mental hospital for a mental examination under section 658 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. His failure to object to the use upon the trial of his statements to the psychiatrists precludes him from now complaining that his privilege against self incrimination or his right to counsel was thereby violated.

6. Defense counsel, when he requested the mental examination, could not have been unaware that the examination would cover defendant's mental condition at the time of the crime as well. Defendant may not be permitted to invoke the defense of insanity and foreclose the People from the benefit of a mental examination to meet this issue.

7. Section 663 of the Judiciary Law, which prohibits the selection of a person for a jury in a criminal action who states that he has conscientious scruples against the death penalty which would prevent him from finding a verdict of guilt of any crime punishable by death, is not unconstitutional.

People v. Burke


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 852; 248 N.E.2d 919; 301 N.Y.S.2d 90; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1409
