HEADNOTES

Crimes -- indictments -- County Court sustained demurrers to indictments and dismissed them, but Appellate Division reinstated them, stating that it was impossible to determine, in absence of Grand Jury minutes, for which no request had been made, whether Grand Jury had indicted for same crimes as were set forth in indictments and in bill of particulars filed for one of them, that, accordingly, indictments, which were in simplified form authorized by statute and previously upheld as constitutional, should be sustained at this stage of proceedings,  [*4] but that defendants could subsequently move against them if inspection of Grand Jury minutes indicated it had not indicted them for crimes set forth therein -- contentions by defendants, variously, that statute authorizing simplified indictment was unconstitutional, that decision upholding constitutionality thereof should be overruled and that specific indictments against them failed to satisfy statutory requirements for indictment, including one in simplified form, and deprived them of their constitutional rights -- orders of Appellate Division affirmed. 

 

228 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fuller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 292; 248 N.E.2d 17; 300 N.Y.S.2d 102; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1682


December 10, 1968, Argued  
April 10, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment reversed and case remitted to the Criminal Court of the City of New York for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, convicted drug addicts, sought review of decisions of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court for the Second and Eleventh Judicial Districts in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which failed to give appellants a jury trial on the issue of their addictions pursuant to the Mental Hyg. Law § 208(2).


OVERVIEW: Appellants, convicted drug addicts, attacked the constitutionality of the provisions of the Mental Hygiene Law (Law) after being denied a jury trial by the lower court. They were ultimately convicted of possession of narcotic instruments under the Law. The court reversed, holding that § 208 (2) of the Law was unconstitutional because it failed to accord a convicted addict a jury trial on the issue of his addiction. The court held that there was no rational basis for denying a jury trial. Appellants' privilege against self incrimination and right to counsel were not violated when evidence was received of admissions of addiction made to examining physicians during the course of court ordered examinations. The court held that the applicability of constitutional rights and privileges was equally suitable for the statements made to a police officer, such as an admission of addiction, and having concluded that the protections of the criminal law were not required here because of the nature of the program, the statement to the police officer was admissible.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate courts' convictions of appellants, convicted drug addicts, because the section of the Mental Hygiene Law that did not allow appellants' a jury trial on the issue of their addiction violated the Equal Protection Clause and was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- drug addiction -- privilege against self incrimination and right to counsel of appellants were not violated when evidence was received, at hearings to determine whether appellants were drug addicts, of admissions of addiction made to examining physician -- addiction may be found on preponderance of evidence -- appellants were unconstitutionally denied jury trial on issue of their addiction -- physician-patient privilege inapplicable -- defense counsel was improperly prevented from cross-examining People's expert about criteria used to determine addiction.

1. Under the Narcotics Control Act of 1966 (Mental Hygiene Law, § 200 et seq.), appellants' privilege against self incrimination and right to counsel were not violated when evidence was received, at the hearings to determine whether appellants were addicts, of admissions of addiction made to examining physicians during the course of court-ordered examinations and by one appellant made to a police officer upon her arrest.

2. Since the purpose of certification is a rehabilitative one, addiction may be found on a preponderance of evidence rather than beyond a reasonable doubt.

3. The requisites of a criminal trial are not applicable to these proceedings. Appellants are entitled to a jury trial on the issue of their addiction and subdivision 2 of section 208 of the Mental Hygiene Law (providing for a hearing without a jury) violates the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.

4. Because of the rehabilitative purposes of the treatment afforded under the Narcotics Control Act, the physician-patient privilege ( CPLR 4504), which would normally protect medical records and statements made to physicians, is inapplicable.

5. The trial court improperly limited the scope of one attorney's cross-examination of the People's medical expert about the criteria the expert used in reaching his conclusion that his client was an addict. It was proper cross-examination to ask the doctor what his interpretation of addiction was and, in reaching his conclusion, what weight he gave to each criterion.

 

229 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


W. v. Family Court


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 196; 247 N.E.2d 253; 299 N.Y.S.2d 414; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1453


December 9, 1968, Argued  
March 6, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed an order of the family court adjudging the juvenile to be a juvenile delinquent following a finding of fact made at a hearing that the juvenile had committed an act which, if committed by an adult, would have constituted the crime of larceny.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was adjudicated a delinquent upon facts that he had removed money from a pocketbook and stolen it. The family court held that the finding met the preponderance of the evidence standard, as required under § 744(b) of the New York Family Court Act. The appellate court affirmed, and the court, on appeal under constitutional grounds, affirmed. After a comprehensive overview of the objectives of the juvenile offender system, the court held that the preponderance of the evidence standard was expressly stated in § 744(b) and went a long way toward providing due process safeguards for children in delinquency proceedings in family court. There was no constitutional violation because the delinquency status was not made a crime and the proceedings were not criminal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court, which, in turn, had affirmed the juvenile's adjudication as a delinquent.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- juvenile delinquency -- sufficiency of proof -- section 744 (subd. [b]) of Family Court Act, which provides that determination, after hearing, that child did act "must be based on a preponderance of the evidence", is not unconstitutional -- not necessary that proof be beyond reasonable doubt -- no deprivation of due process -- no substantial equal protection question.

1. Section 744 (subd. [b]) of the Family Court Act, which provides that any determination at the conclusion of an adjudicatory hearing that the child did an act "must be based on a preponderance of the evidence", is not unconstitutional. In the procedure governing juvenile delinquency it is not necessary that the proof be beyond a reasonable doubt.

2. The finding by the Family Court, by a fair preponderance of the evidence, that the allegation against the 12-year-old child to the effect that he had removed $ 112 from a person's pocketbook and stolen it was true was a constitutionally sufficient finding. There is no deprivation of due process in the statutory provision.

3. Since the proceeding is different from a criminal prosecution, there is no substantial equal protection question in the case. It is not an absence of procedural due process that a noncriminal status determination have a different measure of proof than that required for conviction of crime.

 

230 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hogan v. Rosenberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 207; 247 N.E.2d 260; 299 N.Y.S.2d 424; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1454


January 22, 1969, Argued  
March 6, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: In Matter of Hogan v. Rosenberg: Judgment reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.

In People v. Baldwin: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, New York County, Special Term (New York), which dismissed the State's petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 that prohibited enforcement of a trial court's order mandating that a young adult defendant required a jury trial. In another case, defendant sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, New York County, Appellate Term, which affirmed his conviction for the crime of jostling.


OVERVIEW: For one appeal, the defendant was convicted of jostling, a misdemeanor carrying a maximum term of one-year imprisonment, and the trial court denied the right to a jury trial under New York City, N.Y., Crim. Ct. Act § 40. The court held that those crimes termed as misdemeanors in New York were not characterized as serious and thus those charged with such crimes were not entitled to a Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial. The court further found that § 40 did not violate the Equal Protection Clause because territorial discrimination was permitted to meet the demands of a large metropolitan area. The other defendant was charged as a young adult with a possibility of receiving a reformatory sentence of up to four years. The court found that § 40 was not unconstitutional, but it held that the trial court had no jurisdiction to impose a reformatory sentence pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law art. 75. However, the trial court was able to prosecute any defendant, including a young adult, for any misdemeanor and was further able to impose an authorized sentence not greater than one year, without a jury. Therefore, the appellate court erred in dismissing the State's petition for prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for the crime of jostling. The court reversed the appellate court's dismissal of the State's petition to prohibit enforcement of the order requiring a jury trial and granted the petition.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- right to trial by jury -- section 40 of New York City Criminal Court Act which provides for trial without jury of misdemeanors for which punishment is not more than one year in prison is constitutional -- section does not violate equal protection clause because persons outside City of New York charged with misdemeanors do receive right to trial by jury -- as to defendant charged with misdemeanor who, because he is between ages of 16 and 21, can receive sentence of four-year imprisonment in reformatory, section 40 is constitutional but court lacks jurisdiction to impose reformatory sentence.

1. A defendant charged with the class A misdemeanor of jostling for which the maximum term of imprisonment is not more than one year is not entitled to a trial by jury as guaranteed by the Sixth Amendment, and section 40 of the New York City Criminal Court Act, which provides for trial of such misdemeanors without a jury, is not unconstitutional.

2. Nor does section 40 violate the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment because elsewhere in the State defendants charged with identical misdemeanors receive the right of trial by jury. Such territorial discrimination is proper and, moreover, the caseload in the criminal courts of the highly populated City of New York requires the distinction.

3. With respect to respondent in the above-entitled proceeding, who is charged with a misdemeanor and a violation but who, because he is between the ages of 16 and 21 and thus subject to treatment under article 75 of the Penal Law, can receive a four-year term in a reformatory, section 40 is constitutional but the New York City Criminal Court lacks jurisdiction to impose a reformatory sentence on young adults, in the absence of legislation authorizing a jury trial in such cases and providing procedural machinery therefor. Accordingly, since respondent is still awaiting trial and cannot receive a reformatory sentence, he is not entitled to a trial by jury.

Boikess v. Aspland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 136; 247 N.E.2d 135; 299 N.Y.S.2d 163; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1490


November 26, 1968, Argued  
February 27, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant faculty members sought review from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the lower court's order denying the faculty members' motion to quash certain subpoenas that required their appearance before the Grand Jury.


OVERVIEW: The grand jury was investigating possible drug abuse on the university campus and the subpoenaed faculty members were targets of that inquiry. The faculty members sought further review after the lower court affirmed the initial denial of their motion to quash the subpoenas. The court affirmed the denial, holding that the protections of U.S. Const. amend. V did not extend to the situation at bar. The court concluded that the fact that the faculty members were prospective defendants was not a reason to excuse them from being compelled to appear before the grand jury. Further, the court determined that the proposed questions concerning advocacy of drug use did not violate the faculty members' U.S. Const. amend. I right of academic freedom and, accordingly, there was no reason to strike them. The court noted that the grand jury was inquiring into matters that the faculty members freely discussed in their classrooms and that was relevant to an investigation of misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which denied the faculty members' motion seeking to quash a subpoena to compel their appearance before the grand jury.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- witnesses who are targets of investigation -- order denying application to quash subpoena is final and appealable -- teachers who are targets of Grand Jury's inquiry may be subpoenaed and must attend since no constitutional right is violated by issuance of subpoena.

1. The denial of an application to quash a subpoena, instituted in the County Court, results in a final and appealable order.

2. Where the District Attorney sought to ask faculty members of the State University, who are targets of an investigation conducted by a Grand Jury, whether they used illegal drugs with students, whether they advocated to students the use of illegal drugs and whether they discussed with an administrator their advocacy of illegal drugs or their use of drugs with students, they may be subpoenaed by the Grand Jury. The issuance of the subpoena does not of itself constitute a violation of the teachers' Fifth Amendment rights, and they may be compelled at the very least to attend the Grand Jury investigation.

Briguglio v. New York State Board of Parole


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 21; 246 N.E.2d 512; 298 N.Y.S.2d 704; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1512


November 12, 1968, Argued  
February 20, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing defendant's petition alleging that he was denied parole in violation of his constitutional right to counsel and due process.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the crime of attempted grand larceny in the first degree and sentenced to an indeterminate term of imprisonment. After defendant became eligible for parole, he was examined by the Board of Parole (board) and subsequently denied parole on the ground that he was then a poor risk. Defendant filed a petition for judicial review in the trial court to challenge the board's action as unconstitutional. The trial court dismissed the petition and the court of appeals affirmed. The court affirmed and held that the trial court correctly refused to review the merits of defendant's claim. The court held that the parole release proceeding did not require an adversary hearing and that it did not involve constitutional due process. The court held further that defendant had no right to counsel, to confront and cross-examine witnesses, or to present evidence in his behalf in a parole release proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court, affirming the trial court's judgment that the denial of parole did not result in a violation of defendant's constitutional rights.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- counsel -- in parole release proceeding, convict not entitled to adversary-type hearing and counsel -- merits of claim to release on parole not reviewable by courts.

1. In a parole release proceeding, the convict is not entitled to a full adversary-type hearing including counsel. Such parole hearings do not involve constitutional due process. They are given at the discretion of trained officials and the nature and extent of the examination of the prisoner is solely within their discretion.

2. The merits of the prisoner appellant's claim to release was properly not reviewed by the courts below. Section 212 of the Correction Law provides that "The action of the board of parole in releasing prisoners shall be deemed a judicial function and shall not be reviewable if done according to law."

Affiliated Distillers Brands Corp. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


24 N.Y.2d 31; 246 N.E.2d 518; 298 N.Y.S.2d 713; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1514


January 14, 1969, Argued  
February 20, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs to petitioner in this court and in the Appellate Division, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, New York County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner distiller appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a trial court order granting the distiller's petition to the extent of remanding the matter to respondent State Liquor Authority and dismissed the petition challenging the Authority's disapproval of an application for a brand label registration.


OVERVIEW: The Authority withheld approval of a brand label registration under N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 107-a with regard to the distiller's eight-year-old bourbon on the ground that shortly before the date of the application, the distiller had withdrawn from sale in New York a six-year-old bourbon bearing a similar brand name and sold at lower prices. The distiller brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to compel the Authority to approve the label. The trial court held that the denial of registration was arbitrary and capricious and remanded to the Authority. The appellate court reversed, holding that § 107-a gave the Authority discretion so as to give effect to N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 101-b, and that the Authority's action was not reviewable. Reversing, the court held that as the two products were different from each other, the Authority's attempted regulation was beyond the scope of its statutory powers. As the proposed label was not deceptive or incomplete, and the products were different, there was no lawful basis to deny the application.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order and remanded to the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- brand label registration -- State Liquor Authority lacked power to prevent distiller from withdrawing from sale in New York six-year-old bourbon and offering at higher price similarly named but different eight-year-old bourbon instead -- Authority's attempted regulation is reviewable under section 121 of Alcoholic Beverage Control Law.

1. Nothing in paragraph (a) of subdivision 4 of section 107-a of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, which provides that no liquor "shall be labeled, offered or advertised for sale" unless done in accordance with regulations of the State Liquor Authority and unless "the brand or trade name label" affixed to the container of the beverage "shall have been registered with and approved by the authority and the appropriate fee paid", prevents petitioner from withdrawing its six-year-old bourbon from the New York market and offering, at a higher price, a similarly named but clearly labeled and concededly different eight-year-old bourbon whiskey instead. There is nothing in the affirmation section (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 101-b, subd. 3, pars. [d]-[i]) which requires a distiller of whiskey to offer its products in New York at prices not higher than the lowest prices at which it sells them in any other State, that confers on the Authority the power to forbid a distiller to withdraw one of its products from sale in New York or to make the offering of one of a distiller's products a condition to the approval of a label for a different product.

2. Since the Authority's attempted regulation lies beyond the scope of its statutory powers, its action, purportedly under section 107-a, is reviewable. Moreover, even if its action were within section 107-a, its action would constitute the "[refusal] * * * to issue * * * a permit" within section 121 and thus be reviewable.

 

234 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Chrysler Properties, Inc. v. Morris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 515; 245 N.E.2d 395; 297 N.Y.S.2d 723; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1606


September 26, 1968, Argued  
January 16, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed an order of the trial court in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78(1) denying the corporation's application for a refund of mortgage recording taxes from respondent Register of the City of New York, County of New York.


OVERVIEW: The corporation applied to the register for an exemption from the mortgage recording tax, which was denied. The corporation then applied to the state tax commission for a refund of the taxes. On March 20, 1967, the commission ordered a refund and directed the recording officer of New York County to make the refund, which he refused to do. Shortly thereafter, the legislature amended N.Y. Tax Law § 251 to grant the city a right to seek review of adverse commission determinations. The law was made retroactive to commission determinations made after January 1, 1967. The corporation then filed for a proceeding in the trial court under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78(1), which the trial court dismissed. The appellate division affirmed the order of dismissal. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the appellate division and granted the corporation's petition. The court held that the corporation had obtained a "vested right" in the refund and that the statutory amendment could not alter the right to repayment. The court stated that the retroactive provision was enacted without any discernable reason and that its application to the corporation constituted a due process violation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied the corporation's application to compel the register to refund mortgage recording taxes, and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- retroactive legislation -- amendment to Tax Law ( § 251) which authorizes City of New York to obtain judicial review of determination of State Tax Commission is unconstitutional when applied retroactively to then unreviewable determinations -- right to payment of refund ordered by State Tax Commission of mortgage tax paid by petitioner, which order was not reviewable when legislation was signed, cannot be altered by retroactive legislation; absent showing of public interest served thereby, retroactive legislation may not be sanctioned.

1. An amendment to section 251 of the Tax Law which authorizes the City of New York to obtain judicial review of determinations of the New York State Tax Commission is unconstitutional when applied retroactively to determinations which were unreviewable on the date the amendment was enacted.

2. Accordingly, where the State Tax Commission ordered respondent on March 20, 1967 to refund to petitioner a mortgage recording tax in the amount of $ 67,500, collected over petitioner's application for an exemption on the ground that no loans of new money were involved, and it is conceded that such order was final and not subject to review, an amendment of section 251 of the Tax Law effected by the signing into law of chapter 499 of the Laws of 1967 on April 24, 1967 authorizing the city to seek review of an adverse determination of the State Tax Commission retroactive to January 1, 1967 cannot alter petitioner's right to payment. Courts view with great suspicion the type of retroactive legislation here involved and, absent any showing of public interest to be served by this retroactive legislation, it should not be sanctioned.

 

235 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Westwood Forest Estates, Inc. v. South Nyack


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 424; 244 N.E.2d 700; 297 N.Y.S.2d 129; 1969 N.Y. LEXIS 1646


November 25, 1968, Argued  
January 8, 1969, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant village sought review of a decision of an appellate court (New York) which affirmed an opinion holding that an amendment to a zoning ordinance that barred the construction of multiple dwellings was constitutionally invalid.


OVERVIEW: The village asserted that its amendment to its zoning ordinance was proper because its purpose was to prevent an increase in the amount of effluent discharged into its sewer system until construction of improved sewer facilities was completed. The court rejected the village's contention, finding that the amendment was an improper use of zoning power because the amendment was not properly related to zoning purposes and because it restricted the use of respondent property owner's land to a use for which it was not adaptable. The court ruled that the amendment was impermissible because it singled out the property owner's land on the basis of a general condition in the community rather than a condition caused by the nature of the property owner's land. The court noted that the amendment constituted a taking of the property owner's land that was permissible only through the power of eminent domain because the amendment destroyed the value of the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court. The court held that the village's amendment was improper because the amendment was not within the limits of necessity and permanently prevented the property owner from the use of his land for the only purposes to which it was practically adapted.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- amendment to zoning ordinance of Village of South Nyack, which barred new construction of multiple dwellings throughout village in order to alleviate burden on village's sewage disposal plant, was not properly related to zoning purposes and restricted plaintiff's property to use for which it is not adaptable and is, therefore, invalid.

1. An amendment to the zoning ordinance of the Village of South Nyack, which barred the new construction of multiple dwellings throughout the village in order to alleviate the burden on the village's sewage disposal plant, was not properly related to zoning purposes and restricted plaintiff's property to a use for which it is not adaptable and is, therefore, invalid.

2. The village concededly rezoned plaintiff's land to prevent an increase in the amount of effluent discharged into its sewer system, pending the construction of improved sewer facilities. The sanitation problem that would be created by the construction of a garden apartment development was not due to the inadequacy of the sewer lines and pumping station. Rather, the problem was the hazard of increased pollution of the Hudson River, after inadequate treatment of the sewage effluent. The sanitation problem is general to the community and not caused by the nature of plaintiff's land. It is not permissible to make this plaintiff bear a heavy financial burden because of a general condition in the community.

3. The amendment was also improper because it was not adopted in furtherance of a comprehensive plan.

4. The amendment, which is not limited to any period of time, effectively prevents plaintiff from using its land for any purpose to which it is reasonably adapted.

5. The village may, pursuant to its other and general police powers, impose other restrictions or conditions on the granting of a building permit to plaintiff, but the restraint must be kept within the limits of necessity and may not prevent permanently the reasonable use of private property for the only purpose for which it is practically adapted.

 

236 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Buttonow


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 385; 244 N.E.2d 677; 297 N.Y.S.2d 97; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 923


October 10, 1968, Argued  
December 12, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Suffolk County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant director of a state hospital sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the supreme court holding that the conversion of the status of respondent adjudicated incompetent and patient at the hospital from that of an involuntary admission to that of a voluntary admission was null and void.


OVERVIEW: The incompetent had been involuntarily committed to the hospital but had later executed a "voluntary application" to remain there pursuant to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 71. Her committee sought to have this change in status declared null and void and asserted that the change deprived the incompetent of certain protections afforded involuntarily committed patients without due process and equal protection of the law. Both of the lower courts held in favor of the incompetent. On appeal, the court reversed. The court recognized that, were the incompetent to be deprived of the protections previously afforded her by her transfer to voluntary status, grave doubts of the constitutionality of her conversion under N.Y. Mental Hyg. § 71 would arise. The court held, however, that the constitutionality of the statute could be preserved by reading into it the requirement (1) that a person converted to voluntary status be accorded the right to a judicial hearing and review of his change in status, as well as his retention in such changed status, and (2) that he be provided the same assistance from the state mental health information service as was furnished those who were involuntarily committed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division of the supreme court for the incompetent and remitted the case to the supreme court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Insane and other incompetent persons -- change of status -- Supreme Court has jurisdiction of proceeding by committee of incompetent for declaration that her change of status from involuntary to voluntary patient was null and void -- withholding of statutory safeguards for involuntary patient from those converted to voluntary status would deny equal protection of laws -- constitutionality of statute preserved by reading into it requirement of same safeguards for those on voluntary status -- adjudicated incompetent has legal capacity to request change of status -- matter remitted for hearing as to suitability and willingness of incompetent to be voluntary patient.

1. An adjudicated incompetent, admitted to a State hospital as an involuntary patient, later filed an application to convert her status from an involuntary to a voluntary patient, pursuant to section 71 of the Mental Hygiene Law. The Supreme Court has jurisdiction of the present proceeding brought by her committee against the director of the hospital for a declaration that the change of her status was null and void (Mental Hygiene Law, § 100).

2. The detention of an involuntary patient is subject to court review (§ 73), and he or a relative or friend may obtain a jury trial on the issue of his sanity (§ 74). In addition, the Mental Health Information Service, which performs services for the voluntary patient only upon his request, must be notified of an involuntary patient's admission and is charged with a duty to study and review his admission and retention and provide him and other interested parties with advice and relevant information (§ 88). A withholding of these safeguards from those converted to voluntary status would deny them the equal protection of the laws and, perhaps, due process of law. The constitutionality of the statute is preserved by reading into it a requirement that a mentally ill patient, converted from involuntary to voluntary status, must be accorded these rights which are available to those who are in hospitals on an involuntary basis.

3. A person who has been adjudicated incompetent has the legal capacity to request conversion of his status from involuntary to voluntary.

4. The matter should be remitted to the Supreme Court for a hearing with respect to the suitability and willingness of the incompetent to be a voluntary patient.

 

237 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Stewart v. Citizens Casualty Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 407; 244 N.E.2d 690; 297 N.Y.S.2d 115; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 925


September 25, 1968, Argued  
December 12, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, New York County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) in which the court found appellant insolvent and ordered the Superintendent of Insurance to take possession of appellant's property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insurer sought review of an order from the lower court ordering the Superintendent of Insurance (Superintendent) to take possession of appellant's property. Appellant asserted that it was denied due process and not given an opportunity to dispute whether it was insolvent. The Superintendent argued that it had the power to take appellant's property without affording appellant a hearing. The appeals court reversed the lower courts' judgment holding that appellant was entitled to a full hearing and an opportunity to offer proof regarding its insolvency. The appeals court held that a fair reading of N.Y. Ins. Law §§ 23, 30, 512, 521, and 526 resulted in the conclusion that an evidentiary hearing was required. The appeals court expressly rejected the Superintendent's argument. The appeals court further held that the offer of proof at the trial level was insufficient because appellant was prevented from introducing any contradictory evidence of solvency.


OUTCOME: The appeals court reversed the lower courts' judgment holding that appellant was entitled to a full hearing and an opportunity to offer proof regarding its insolvency.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- rehabilitation of insurance company -- insurance company whose business was sought to be rehabilitated by Superintendent of Insurance because it was insolvent (Insurance Law, § 526) was denied full hearing accorded by that statute where Trial Judge refused to permit insurer to offer evidence on issue of its solvency -- Appellate Division improperly concluded that offer of proof was insufficient to put solvency in issue.

1. The Superintendent of Insurance determined after an examination that an insurance company was insolvent and brought a proceeding under article XVI of the Insurance Law to rehabilitate the company. Section 526 thereof provides that after a "full hearing" the court shall either deny or grant the application to rehabilitate. At the hearing, the Superintendent offered in evidence the report of insolvency but the insurance company was not allowed to introduce any evidence in support of its claim that it was solvent. The company was denied a "full hearing" and the order should be reversed and the case remitted for further proceedings. Analysis of the provisions of the Insurance Law indicate that section 526 was not intended to provide a summary proceeding. By using the phrase "full hearing" the Legislature is presumed to have intended that the procedures normally followed by judicial tribunals would be continued.

2. Since the insurer was not permitted to offer evidence to controvert the claim that the insurer was insolvent, the Appellate Division improperly concluded that the insurer's offer of proof was insufficient to put solvency in issue.

239 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Baker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 307; 244 N.E.2d 232; 296 N.Y.S.2d 745; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 981


May 17, 1968, Argued  
November 27, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments of conviction reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted of murder in the first degree, attempted murder in the first degree, and attempted robbery in the first degree. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the convictions. Defendants sought further review of their convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendants stabbed their victim to death and fled. Subsequent to their arrest, they were convicted and sentenced. Their convictions were affirmed by the intermediate court, but reversed by the court because of trial errors that deprived defendants of a fair trial and resulted in a constitutionally defective trial. The court held that the prime error that compelled reversal and a new trial as to all defendants was the implication of defendants in the crime by the confessions and statements of some of their codefendants, who testified for the state. Particularly, certain defendants' statements were admitted as evidence against other defendants when the former did not testify at trial. The improper admission of statements by nontestifying defendants without any attempt to redact them violated the defendants' constitutional right to confront their accusers. The court then turned to the second error that mandated reversal as to one defendant. This defendant's confession was obtained after he had been surrendered to the police by his attorney for the purposes of arraignment. Police obtained the statement after his attorney had made it clear that he wished his client to make no statement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' judgments of conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- admission of statements by witnesses that some of defendants inculpated codefendants violated right to confront accusers -- error was prejudicial -- statements not redactable -- statements taken by police in absence of attorney inadmissible -- seizure of sweater on warrant permitting search for knife violated Fourth Amendment -- new trial granted as to all defendants.

1. At a joint trial all six of the defendants were found guilty of murder in the first degree, attempted murder in the first degree and attempted robbery in the first degree. During questioning by the prosecutor, the witness Barnes and a detective testified that some of the defendants made statements inculpating their codefendants. The admission of these statements violated the defendants' constitutional right to confront their accusers.

2. The error cannot be ignored upon the ground that no proper objection was made since, in this case tried prior to the decision in Bruton v. United States (391 U.S. 123), their admission under limiting instructions was proper.

3. Because of the manner in which the oral confessions and statements were used at the trial, the error cannot be disregarded as nonprejudicial.

4. None of the written statements or confessions were and none could be effectively redacted.

5. There must, therefore, be a new trial, not only as to the three nonconfessing defendants but as to the confessing defendants as well.

6. The statements taken by the police and the Assistant District Attorney from defendant Baker after his attorney had left the police station, having made it clear that he wished his client to make no statement and to be present at any questioning of said defendant, were inadmissible.

7. The seizure of a sweater allegedly worn by one of the attackers on a warrant permitting the search for a knife violated the Fourth Amendment to the Constitution and the evidence should not have been received. ( Warden v. Hayden, 387 U.S. 294, distinguished.)

8. After cross-examination, Barnes was permitted to testify that he had given the same account to the detective when called out of the "bullpen" shortly after his incriminating admission to a narcotics addict. Later, the detective testified to this original conversation. This testimony was not improper. A portion of the attack on Barnes was based on events arising subsequent to the initial confrontation with the detective. Therefore, it was not error for the court to admit the testimony of Barnes on redirect and of the detective.

9. A certain witness' testimony before the Grand Jury concerning her photographic identification of defendant Felder was admissible. The witness, who was in the drugstore at the time of the murder, identified defendant Felder as one of the persons she saw running away from the murder scene. In addition, she was permitted to testify that she had identified a photograph of Felder before the Grand Jury. On cross-examination, it was brought out that she had testified before the Grand Jury that she did not recognize some of the boys that she saw running around the corner. On redirect, the People sought to correct this impression that she had not recognized the boys. A party may read another pertinent portion of a statement used for the purposes of impeachment to correct a false impression that reading only a part of the statement may give. Thus the statement here was admissible for purposes of showing that there was in fact no contradiction.

10. As there is no evidence that defendants Hamm or Rice were ever given Miranda warnings by the police, the jury should have been charged that the absence of Miranda warnings should be considered on the question of voluntariness of the confessions or admissions.

11. Better practice would suggest that the court should itemize the pieces of evidence which could be considered by the jury as corroborative of testimony that a defendant had participated in the crime.

241 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York v. De Lury


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 175; 243 N.E.2d 128; 295 N.Y.S.2d 901; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1009; 69 L.R.R.M. 2865


October 9, 1968, Argued  
November 21, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, union and its president, sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order granting a motion by plaintiff city for a preliminary injunction to enjoin a strike, and adjudging the union and its president guilty of criminal contempt. The union and president challenged the constitutionality of the Taylor Law, N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 200-212.


OVERVIEW: A strike was instituted by a union composed of sanitation workers. As a result, garbage was not collected for several days and serious hazards were presented by the mounting trash. The city sought and received a preliminary injunction against the strike. The president and the union refused to comply with the terms of the injunction, and criminal contempt orders were issued. The union and the president challenged the orders, and the court affirmed both the injunction and the finding of criminal contempt. The union and the president challenged the constitutionality of the applicable law, the Taylor Law (law), codified at N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 200-212, contending that its prohibition against strikes by public employees violated due process. The court held that the statutory prohibition against strikes by public employees was reasonably designed to effectuate a valid State policy in an area where the State had authority to act. In addition, the provisions of the law did not offend any due process rights of the union or the president. The court additionally concluded that the union and the president were not entitled to a jury trial in a criminal contempt proceeding.


OUTCOME: The decision upholding the judgment that granted the city's motion for an injunction and found the union and its president guilty of criminal contempt was affirmed, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- strikes by public employees -- Taylor Law prohibiting strikes by public employees does not deprive union of public employees and its president of due process of law -- condemnation of strikes by public employees effectuates valid State policy -- prohibition does not violate equal protection clause -- public employees and officer of their union are not entitled to jury trial in criminal contempt proceeding -- president of union failed to obey court's directive; order adjudging him and union in contempt of court is affirmed.

1. The Taylor Law (Civil Service Law, art. 14, §§ 200-212) prohibiting strikes by public employees does not deprive defendants -- a union of sanitation men whose members are employed by plaintiff city and its president -- of due process of law. The State, in governing its internal affairs, has the power to prohibit any strike if the prohibition is reasonably calculated to effectuate a valid State policy in an area where it has authority to act.

2. The condemnation of strikes by public employees does effectuate a valid State policy.

3. The prohibition does not violate the equal protection clause. Legislative differentiation between public and private employees, insofar as restrictions on their right to strike and to jury trials are concerned, is reasonable.

4. A jury trial in a criminal contempt proceeding such as this is not available to public employees or to officers of labor organizations representing them charged with violation of the Taylor Law.

5. The individual defendant, president of the union, failed to obey the directive of the court that the strike be terminated and instead urged his men to make the strike 100% effective. The order adjudging defendants union and its president in contempt of court is affirmed.

 

242 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Boback


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 189; 243 N.E.2d 135; 295 N.Y.S.2d 912; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1010


October 7, 1968, Argued  
November 21, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed and judgment of the trial court reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the judgment of the Herkimer County Court (New York) reversing defendant drivers' traffic violation convictions and dismissing the informations against them.


OVERVIEW: The defendants were convicted of traffic violations. Both defendants had been issued simplified traffic informations as authorized by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 147-e. The troopers who issued the informations did not witness the offenses and it was clear that the trooper had obtained his information from someone else. The informations did not show the source of the troopers' information and the foundation for their belief. The defendants challenged their convictions. The trial court found that informations defective. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that it was evident that neither the language nor the legislative history of the simplified traffic information statute limited the use of the information to those cases where the officer making the information had some personal knowledge of the violation. Consequently, the court found that the trial courts were not deprived of jurisdiction because the information was made by an officer having no personal knowledge of the relevant events. Finally, the court held that the simplified traffic information was being used solely as a pleading and, consequently, defendants' constitutional argument failed.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the informations against the defendants and reversing their convictions was reversed and the judgment of the trial court was ordered reinstated.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- Simplified Traffic Information -- use of Simplified Traffic Information (Code Crim. Pro., § 147-a et seq.) authorized where information based solely on information and belief.

1. Use of the Simplified Traffic Information (Code Crim. Pro., § 147-a et seq.) is authorized where the information is based solely upon information and belief. Neither the language nor the legislative history of the Simplified Traffic Information statute limits the use of the information to those cases where the officer making the information has some personal knowledge of the violation. The trial courts were not deprived of jurisdiction because the information was made by an officer having no personal knowledge of the relevant events.

2. The Simplified Traffic Information cannot itself furnish a basis for the issuance of a warrant of arrest or procure the defendant's appearance before the court. If he should choose to ignore the summons and not appear on the return date of the summons, the court must take testimony or have before it affidavits which would establish probable cause before issuing an arrest warrant. The Simplified Traffic Information is being used solely as a pleading and such use is not unconstitutional. ( People v. James, 4 N Y 2d 482, and People v. Jeffries, 19 N Y 2d 564, distinguished.)

244 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rankin ex rel. Board of Educ. v. Shanker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 111; 242 N.E.2d 802; 295 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1698; 69 L.R.R.M. 2746


October 29, 1968, Argued  
October 30, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent board of education sought a criminal contempt order against defendants, teachers and union, claiming that they had willfully disobeyed a temporary injunction and violated § 210(1) of the Taylor Law, N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law art. 14, §§ 200-212. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the denial of the teachers and union's motions for a jury trial. The teachers and union appealed.


OVERVIEW: The teachers and union argued that the legislature intended to apply N.Y. Jud. Law §753-a and N.Y. Lab. Law § 808, which granted the right of jury trial in a proceeding to punish a failure or refusal to obey an injunction order granted in any case involving or growing out of a labor dispute, to strikes by public employees because although N.Y. Lab. Law § 807 was specifically inapplicable to injunctions to restrain violations of § 210(1), no similar exclusionary language applied to § 808. On appeal, the court held that the Taylor Law was never intended to, and did not, make provision for jury trials in contempt enforcement proceedings and that the reference therein to § 807 could not possibly create a right to a jury trial under § 808. The court further found that the denial of a jury trial to public employees was not a constitutional violation because a reasonable distinction could be drawn between public and private employment. Finally, the court held that the teachers and union were not entitled to jury trial under the Fourteenth Amendment because, based on the possible punishment if the teachers and union were found guilty, the offense was petty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order and the denial of the motion of the teachers and the union for a jury trial.


HEADNOTES

Contempt -- criminal contempt -- jury trial -- public employees and unions which represent them are not entitled, as matter of right, to jury trial in criminal contempt proceeding brought for alleged violation of Taylor Law in that they engaged in strike or instigated, encouraged or condoned strike.

1. Public employees and the unions which represent them are not entitled, as a matter of right, to trial by jury in a criminal contempt proceeding brought against them for alleged volation of the Taylor Law (L. 1967, ch. 392; Civil Service Law, art. 14, §§ 200-212), in that the employees engaged in a strike and the organizations instigated, encouraged or condoned such strike.

2. Section 753-a of the Judiciary Law and section 808 of the Labor Law grant the right of jury trial in a proceeding to punish, as a criminal contempt, a failure or refusal to obey an injunction order granted "in any case involving or growing out of a labor dispute". The Legislature did not intend to apply those sections to strikes by public employees.

3. To refuse a trial by jury to public employees or their representative organizations and to grant it to those in private industry does not deny the equal protection of the laws under the Constitution of either the United States or New York State. A distinction between public and private employment is constitutionally permissible.

4. The Federal Constitution (art. III, § 2; 5th, 6th, 14th Amdts.) does not require that defendants be accorded a trial by jury in a contempt proceeding where the maximum term of imprisonment authorized is 30 days. ( Bloom v. Illinois, 391 U. S. 194, distinguished.)

 

245 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


French v. Banco Nacional De Cuba


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 46; 242 N.E.2d 704; 295 N.Y.S.2d 433; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1092


January 4, 1968, Argued; September 24, 1968, Reargued  
October 15, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff investor brought suit against defendant bank of Cuba for breach of contract. The trial court entered a judgment in favor of investor. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department affirmed the trial court's judgment. Bank of Cuba appealed.


OVERVIEW: Investor purchased land in Cuba. Investor obtained a certificate of tax exemption from Cuba, which stated the owner of the certificate would receive from the bank of Cuba the appropriate amount of American dollars. Subsequently, the Cuban government adopted a regulation, which prevented American and other foreign investors from receiving currency other than Cuban pesos on their Cuban investments. The court determined that bank of Cuba was not entitled to sovereign immunity from the suit, because its activities were commercial in nature. The court concluded that the regulation of the Cuban government was an act of state, which American courts were required to give effect to that act of state. Since the record established that there was no taking of property to which a claim of title or other rights was asserted, the Hickenlooper Amendment did not apply; therefore the court was not required to disregard such an act of state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment and dismissed investor's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Banks and banking -- certificates of Cuban Currency Stabilization Fund -- after American investor in Cuban farm acquired certificates issued by Currency Stabilization Fund of Cuban Government, providing that, upon delivery of appropriate number of pesos and surrender of certificates, he would receive from Banco Nacional de Cuba check for equal amount of American dollars, Fund issued decision suspending processing of such certificates -- bank thereafter refused to pay him in American dollars, under mandate of said decision -- in action by investor's assignee against bank, based upon breach of contract, complaint is dismissed -- although bank is not entitled to sovereign immunity from suit, actions complained of constitute act of state to which court is required to give effect -- application of act of state doctrine is not barred by Hickenlooper Amendment -- although it is not necessary to reach question, action of Cuban Government, taken to protect Cuba's foreign exchange reserves, did not violate international law.

1. An American citizen acquired eight certificates, aggregating $ 150,000, each signed by Banco Nacional de Cuba and the Currency Stabilization Fund and each providing that, upon delivery of an appropriate number of Cuban pesos and surrender of the certificate, he would receive from the bank a check for an equal amount of American dollars, exempt from an exportation tax. Prior to his attempted redemption of the certificates, the Currency Stabilization Fund issued a "Decision" suspending the "processing" of such certificates "until reorganization of the system of exemptions". When the American tendered his certificates, with the appropriate number of Cuban pesos, the bank, under the mandate of the "Decision", refused to pay him in American dollars. In this action by his assignee against the bank, based upon breach of contract, the court declines to accord the bank sovereign immunity from suit as an agency of the Cuban Government. The State Department has concluded that the activities out of which the action arose were of a commercial nature and its position is that immunity should not be granted in such cases.

2. However, the complaint must be dismissed since the actions complained of constitute an act of state to which the court is required to give effect ( Banco Nacional de Cuba v. Sabbatino, 376 U.S. 398). The alleged breach of contract resulted from, and itself constituted, an act of state.

3. Application of the act of state doctrine is not barred by the Hickenlooper Amendment to the Federal Foreign Assistance Act of 1961, enacted to "reverse in part" the decision in the Sabbatino case (supra). That amendment declares that "no court in the United States shall decline on the ground of the federal act of state doctrine to make a determination on the merits * * * in a case in which a claim of title or other right to property is asserted by any party including a foreign state * * * based upon (or traced through) a confiscation or other taking * * * by an act of that state in violation of the principles of international law." The loss suffered by plaintiff's assignor was due, not to a "taking", but to the breach of a promise upon which he had relied.

4. Moreover, even if his title to, and right to enforce, his contract rights had been taken over by the Cuban Government, such a confiscation would be outside the compass of the Hickenlooper Amendment. The amendment does not cover ordinary contract rights, or anything other than specific, identifiable and "traceable" property.

5. In view of this determination, it is not necessary to reach the question of whether the action of the Cuban Government was "in violation of the principles of international law".

6. In any event, since the control of national currency and foreign exchange is an essential governmental function and there is no question that the action here complained of was aimed at protecting Cuba's foreign exchange reserves, it may not be characterized as so unreasonable or unjust as to outrage current international standards of governmental conduct.

 

246 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Long Island College Hospital v. Catherwood


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 20; 241 N.E.2d 892; 294 N.Y.S.2d 697; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1093; 69 L.R.R.M. 2605


April 15, 1968, Argued; October 8, 1968, Reargued  
October 14, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital challenged the decision of appellee industrial commission and the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the trial court decision granting the commission summary judgment and dismissing the hospital's claim without prejudice.


OVERVIEW: The labor board certified a union as the bargaining representative of the maintenance employees at the hospital. The hospital thereafter refused to bargain collectively with it on the ground that it did not represent the employees as it claimed. The hospital brought the actions seeking to enjoin a fact-finding panel appointed by the commission from functioning and to stay compulsory arbitration proceeding directed by the commission. The court was required to decide whether an issue as to representation was a dispute under N.Y. Labor Law § 716(1). The court found that § 716 could not be invoked by a group of employees or by a minority union to arbitrate disputes with a nonprofitmaking hospital. The commissioner, therefore, lacked the authority to appoint the fact-finding panel and to send the issue to compulsory arbitration.


OUTCOME: The orders appealed from were reversed and the appellant's application for a declaration regarding the commission's lack of authority and for an order permanently staying the arbitration order was granted.


HEADNOTES

Labor unions -- collective bargaining in nonprofitmaking hospital -- arbitration -- section 716 of Labor Law, which empowers Industrial Commissioner to submit "dispute" between nonprofitmaking hospital and union to fact-finding commission and, in event of rejection of commission findings, to compulsory and binding arbitration, may not be invoked where employer institution challenges representation status of union -- as defined in said section, "dispute" encompasses only economic and union security issues and does not embrace representation issues, which are left for resolution to Labor Relations Board -- accordingly, where nonprofitmaking hospital refused to bargain with union certified by Labor Relations Board, basing refusal on ground that union did not represent employees, and union invoked provisions of section 716 rather than filing unfair labor practice charge with Labor Relations Board, hospital is entitled to declaration that Industrial Commissioner had no authority to appoint fact-finding commission and is entitled to stay of compulsory arbitration proceeding until union's certification has been judicially reviewed and found valid -- legislation to avoid undue delay suggested.

1. Section 716 of the Labor Law, newly enacted in 1963, which empowers the Industrial Commissioner or the State Board of Mediation to submit a "dispute" between a nonprofitmaking hospital or residential care center and a union to a fact-finding commission and, in the event of rejection of the findings of the commission, to compulsory and binding arbitration, may not be invoked where the employer institution challenges the representation status of the union. As defined in section 716, a "dispute" encompasses only economic and union security issues. It does not embrace representation issues.

2. Accordingly, where a nonprofitmaking hospital refused to bargain collectively with a union which had been certified by the State Labor Relations Board as the bargaining representative of its maintenance employees, basing its refusal upon the ground that the union did not represent the employees as it claimed, and the union invoked the provisions of section 716 rather than following the judicially accepted method of filing with the Labor Relations Board an unfair labor practice charge based on the hospital's refusal to bargain, the hospital is entitled to a declaration that the Industrial Commissioner had no authority to appoint a fact-finding commission and is entitled to an order staying a compulsory arbitration proceeding until the union's certification has been judicially reviewed and found valid.

3. The court suggests to the Legislature that all undue delay could be avoided by legislation requiring that certification and refusal to bargain proceedings be expedited and that the period for the prosecution of appeals from the board's ensuing orders be shortened.

 

247 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York C. R. Co. v. Lefkowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


23 N.Y.2d 1; 241 N.E.2d 730; 294 N.Y.S.2d 519; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1094; 70 L.R.R.M. 2835


April 10, 1968, Argued; October 7, 1968, Reargued  
October 10, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Upon reargument: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff railroads appealed the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified and affirmed the trial court's order in favor of defendants, state and state agencies (State) and intervenor union. The railroads argued that N.Y. R.R. Law § 54-b was invalid and that it violated the U.S. and N.Y. Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The railroads filed suit against the State seeking a declaratory judgment to invalidate the full crew laws under N.Y. R.R. Law §§ 54-a, 54-b, 54-c, and seeking to enjoin their enforcement. The union intervened. The trial court dismissed. While the railroads' appeal was pending, the legislature repealed N.Y. R.R. Law §§ 54-a and 54-c, but refused to repeal N.Y. R.R. Law § 54-b. The appellate court affirmed the trial court, modifying only to explicitly declare that § 54-b was constitutional. The railroads appealed. The court affirmed. Although § 54-b added substantial costs to the railroads' freight operations, and despite the fact that it was adopted in 1936 and might have been outmoded, § 54-b was valid. It did not violate the due process and equal protection clauses of the U.S Constitution or the N.Y. Constitution, and was within the commerce clause of the U.S. Constitution. The railroad's argument that § 54-b was confiscatory was not sustained by the record. Section 54-b was not a burden on interstate commerce. The requirements of § 54-b were validly related to railroad safety, so in the absence of federal preemption , they were a valid exercise of the State's police power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court, upholding the constitutionality of the State's full crew law.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- railroad full crew laws -- section 54-b of Railroad Law, prohibiting operation on any railroad within New York State of any fuel-electric engine unless it is manned with crew of "not less than one engineer and one fireman or helper", is constitutional -- by expression "and one fireman or helper", Legislature intended to require assignment of either fireman or engine crewman equivalent to fireman to assist engineer -- assignment of brakeman from train crew does not meet this requirement -- requirement is constitutionally valid exercise of police power of State since it serves rational purpose in promotion of safety -- section does not deprive railroads of due process or equal protection of laws and does not constitute burden on interstate commerce.

1. Section 54-b of the Railroad Law, prohibiting the operation on any railroad within New York State of any fuel-electric, or diesel, engine unless it is manned with a crew of "not less than one engineer and one fireman or helper", is constitutional.

2. By the expression "and one fireman or helper", the Legislature intended to require the assignment of either a fireman or an engine crewman equivalent to a fireman to assist the engineer. The assignment of a brakeman from the train crew does not meet this requirement.

3. The legislative intention to require the "helper" to be a member of the engine crew, as distinguished from the train crew, is made clear by reading section 54-b together with section 54-a of the Railroad Law, enacted in 1913 and repealed in 1966, and section 54-c, enacted in 1937 and repealed in 1966 along with section 54-a. Section 54-a, the original full crew law, was addressed to steam operations and required a freight train of more than 25 cars to be manned with a crew of not less than one engineer and one fireman, jobs only related to the engine, and one conductor and three brakemen, jobs only related to the train. In 1936, when section 54-b was enacted, diesel engines did not, and still do not, have a special identifying name for an engine crew member additional to the engineer and, if the purpose of the section was to require an additional man on the engine crew, the safe approach was to use the then well-understood term "fireman", even though it had no literal application to diesel engines, and, to give free scope to the statute, add "or helper". This language suggests the intention to continue the distinction made by section 54-a between the engine crew and the train crew. This intention is further indicated by the enactment during the following year of section 54-c, which affected both steam and diesel engines and required, in switching and transfer operations, a crew of not less than "one engineer, one fireman or helper, one conductor or foreman and two trainmen or two helpers". Also of significance is the fact that the Public Service Commission, which extensively examined all three sections encompassing the full crew policy of the State, construed section 54-b as requiring a fireman or the engine-crew equivalent of a fireman in the diesel engine.

4. The requirement is a constitutionally valid exercise of the police power of the State since it serves some rational purpose in the promotion of safety. Findings have been made, and affirmed, that, in road freight service, the fireman is frequently the only crew member in position to take and pass signals and act as a lookout and, in all types of service, he is the only employee in the cab who may be expected to be capable of relieving the engineer in case of fatigue or emergency. A factual conclusion has been made, and affirmed, that it is not unreasonable to require, for safety, the presence in the engine cab of a second man who has, in addition to the ability to act as a lookout and to read and pass signals, the training and qualifications which are ordinarily possessed by firemen and which may make it possible for him to be of assistance to the engineer in the operation of the locomotive. The evidence is legally sufficient to support the essential factual conclusion on which the declaration of the constitutionality of section 54-b is based.

5. The section does not deprive the railroads of due process or equal protection of the laws and does not constitute a burden on interstate commerce.

 

248 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cox v. Katz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


22 N.Y.2d 903; 241 N.E.2d 747; 294 N.Y.S.2d 544; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1146


September 25, 1968, Argued  
October 4, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff electors appealed, on constitutional grounds, the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the trial court denying an request by the electors for a temporary injunction and dismissing their complaint in an action for a judgment declaring unconstitutional the statutes implementing the establishment of a single court of city-wide civil jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The electors filed an action for a temporary injunction and a declaration that a statute implementing the establishment of a court city-wide civil jurisdiction was unconstitutional. The lower courts denied the request for an injunction and dismissed the electors' complaint. On appeal, the court affirmed the order of the lower courts. The court held that there was no basis for the application of the "one man-one vote" principle. That doctrine, designed to assure representative government in a democracy, was never intended to regulate the election of judges whose functions were solely judicial. Judges were not to be elected to represent a particular point of view. The civil court did not receive cases on the basis of the racial or ethnic origins of the parties or of their places of residence within the city. The judiciary was not responsible for achieving representative government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower courts.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Civil Court of City of New York -- Appellate Division affirmed dismissal of complaint in action for judgment declaring unconstitutional various statutes implementing establishment of single court of city-wide civil jurisdiction, concluding that constitutional provision for choosing of Judges of said court "by the electors of the counties included within the city of New York from districts within such counties established by law" enabled Legislature to make provision, in its discretion, for election and allocation of said Judges, that statutes enacted by it, providing that vacancy in said judicial office was to be filled, in case of former Justice of Municipal Court of successor, by electors of former Municipal Court district and, in case of former Justrice of City Court or successor, by electors of county, were constitutional, as was statute providing for 25 additional judgeships for Civil Court, to be elected on county-wide basis, and that, in light of circumstances, nominations for said additional judgeships might properly be made by political parties rather than by electors -- order of Appellate Division affirmed -- Court of Appeals concurs in views expressed in opinion at Appellate Division and finds, as to Federal questions raised by plaintiffs, that there is no basis for application of "one man-one vote" principle and that said doctrine was never intended to regulate election of Judges, there being, in light of their functions, no reason or justification for requirement that they be allocated on per capita basis. 

 

249 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


22 N.Y.2d 465; 239 N.E.2d 879; 293 N.Y.S.2d 280; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1176


June 5, 1968, Argued  
July 2, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v. Allen: Judgment reversed and case remitted to the Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.

In People v. Fuller and People v. Klein: Judgments reversed and the complaints dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants in three consolidated appeals sought review of decisions from the Supreme Court of the Second Judicial Department (New York), which adjudged them wayward minors and imposed sentence accordingly. Defendants asserted the trial court erred in determining they were wayward minors and challenged the constitutionality of the statute for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: One defendant was disobedient to her parents by dating someone much older, a second was disobedient to his mother, stayed out late and associated with persons of whom she disapproved, and the third was disobedient to his parents, addicted to narcotic drugs, and stole money from home. Defendants were teenagers between the ages of 16 and 21, were adjudged wayward minors, and were sentenced to state reformatory institutions. In their consolidated appeal, defendants challenged the constitutionality of the statute but the court refused to reconsider the issue because it was previously sustained. The court reversed the convictions of all three defendants holding that the statute required a finding that a minor was either "disobedient to parents" or had "deserted his home" in conjunction with a separate finding that the minor was either "morally depraved" or "in danger of becoming morally depraved" before a minor could be adjudged a wayward minor within the meaning of the statute and sentenced to a reformatory facility. The court held that in each of the cases, the minor was lacking the combination necessary for such a finding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court finding defendants to be wayward minors as to all three defendants. The court dismissed charges against two defendants and remitted the third case for consideration of mental health treatment.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- wayward minors -- 18-year-old girl, in conflict with her parents and disobedient to them, and boy between 17 and 19 years old, disobedient and abusive to his mother, not shown to be "morally depraved" or "in danger of becoming morally depraved" (Code Crim. Pro., § 913-a, subds. [5], [6]) and, therefore, not wayward minors -- complaints against them dismissed -- third defendant essentially narcotic addict rather than wayward minor -- charge against him remitted for consideration of treatment under article 9 of Mental Hygiene Law.

1. Defendants were adjudicated to be wayward minors under a statute (Code Crim. Pro., § 913-a, subds. [5], [6]), which requires proof that the minor is "morally depraved" or "is in danger of becoming morally depraved". In the one case an 18-year-old girl was in conflict with her father concerning her conduct. She had formed an attachment to a man 30 years of age, to whom her parents objected, and her father stated that she had a key to his apartment. There was no proof of sexual intercourse and she herself denied it. The record shows that this defendant is in growing conflict with her parents and disobedient to them within the terms of the statute, but it fails to show that she is "morally depraved" or "in danger of becoming morally depraved".

2. In another case a boy, at the time of trial, between 17 and 19 years old, was said by his mother to have disobeyed her, stayed out late, been abusive to her and associated with people of whom she did not approve. She also believed he used narcotics, but he denied using them. There was no proof of any substantive act except disobedience and being abusive. The proof did not justify his adjudication as a wayward minor.

3. In the third case the proof was adequate that defendant was addicted to narcotic drugs. Essentially he is a narcotic addict and needed treatment, rather than a wayward minor. The charge against him should be remitted for the court to consider treatment under article 9 of the Mental Hygiene Law. As to the other two defendants the complaints should be dismissed.
