Narcotic Addiction Control Com. v. James


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


22 N.Y.2d 545; 240 N.E.2d 29; 293 N.Y.S.2d 531; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1183


April 3, 1968, Argued  
July 2, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and judgment of Supreme Court, New York County, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant alleged drug addict challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a judgment of the trial court directing the release of the addict from the custody of respondent Narcotic Addiction Control Commission and directed that the addict be returned to the custody of the Commission.


OVERVIEW: Upon the affidavit of the addict's mother, the addict was arrested and placed in the custody of the Commission. The addict was detained for three days prior to being brought before the trial court, at which time an examining physician testified that the addict was dependant on narcotic drugs. A hearing was held 10 days later, and the addict was certified to be an addict. The addict sought a jury trial, and after trial, the jury found him to be a drug addict. Thereafter, the trial court ordered the addict released from custody upon the ground that the procedure mandated by New York Narcotics Control Act of 1966, N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 206, for the compulsory apprehension and detention of the addict violated his constitutional rights. The appeals court reversed, and on appeal, the court reversed the appeals court and reinstated the judgment of the trial court. The court held that while the Act was a valid and legitimate exercise of police power, the procedural provisions contained in the Act deprived the addict of due process of law as provided by the Fourteenth Amendment. There was no justification for holding the addict for three days without notice or an opportunity to be heard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the court of appeals, which reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court reinstated the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- drug addiction -- persons dependent upon narcotic drugs may be compelled, in exercise of police power, to undergo rehabilitative care under proper statute -- procedure of Mental Hygiene Law ( § 206) whereby alleged addict was detained for three days, without notice of reason for detention and opportunity to be heard, is unconstitutional -- determination that appellant was drug addict, based upon information obtained during illegal detention, is set aside.

1. Persons dependent upon narcotic drugs who are no longer able to control craving for narcotics, which persons have been found by the Legislature to be responsible for one half of the crimes committed in the City of New York and who endanger their own physical and mental health, may be compelled, in the exercise of the police power by the Legislature, to undergo rehabilitative care under a properly restricted statute.

2. However, the procedure of section 206 of the Mental Hygiene Law under which appellant, who was charged by his mother with being dependent upon narcotic drugs, was detained, without being charged with a crime, against his will, for a period of three days, without notice of the nature of the proceeding and an opportunity to contest the basis upon which the determination to restrain his liberty was predicated, is unconstitutional.

3. The determination that appellant was an addict, which was based almost entirely on information obtained during the period of illegal detention, must be set aside. It is immaterial that appellant eventually received a hearing.

In re Estate of Leikind


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


22 N.Y.2d 346; 239 N.E.2d 550; 292 N.Y.S.2d 681; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1244


April 15, 1968, Submitted  
June 14, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and matter remitted to the Surrogate's Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein, with costs to abide the event.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner estate administrator appealed, and respondent state cross-appealed, from an order of the Appellate Division (New York), which reversed a decree of the trial court that declined to release funds impounded from the decedent's estate for satisfaction of the estate administrator's previously obtained default judgment against a nonresident estate beneficiary's bequest.


OVERVIEW: The beneficiary was unable to claim a portion of the decedent's estate pursuant to § 269-a of the Surrogate's Court Act, N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act 2218, which impounded the amount bequeathed her until the court was satisfied it would be used for her benefit. The administrator claimed that the beneficiary owed him certain sums, and he produced a letter in which she purportedly acknowledged a promise to repay him. In remanding the matter, the court concluded that the law impounding such funds was not unconstitutional, but the effect of the default judgment required determination. The court ruled that the impounding was not proscribed under existing caselaw, and that the trial court acquired the necessary jurisdiction to enter judgment in the administrator's favor upon the beneficiary's default. In consequence of the statute's constitutionality, however, the court held that the default judgment did not automatically entitle the administrator to satisfaction from the impounded funds. Instead, he was required to satisfy the trial court that the purposes of the restraint would not be frustrated, and that the funds would not be vulnerable to confiscation in a foreign country.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the cause of action be remanded to the trial court for a hearing and further findings as to the default judgment, but concluded that the impounding of the funds was not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Executors and administrators -- funds due to foreign national -- former section 269-a of Surrogate's Court Act providing for deposit in court of funds due foreign national who would not have "benefit or use or control" thereof is not unconstitutional under holding of Zschernig v. Miller (389 U.S. 429) -- petitioner's default judgment, obtained by attaching impounded fund due Soviet beneficiary, based on letter of beneficiary acknowledging debt to petitioner is valid -- matter remanded to Surrogate to determine if petitioner is person entitled to fund and if satisfaction of judgment from fund would not conflict with purpose of statute.

1. Former section 269-a of the Surrogate's Court Act (now SCPA 2218) provided that, where the beneficiary of a decedent's estate "would not have the benefit or use or control of the money or other property due him", such property must be paid into court "for the benefit of [such beneficiary] or such person or persons who may thereafter appear to be entitled thereto". A beneficiary of decedent's estate was a resident of the Soviet Union and her share of the estate was impounded pursuant to an order of the Surrogate. If the courts of this State, in applying the "benefit or use or control" requirements, determine whether or not a foreign country, by statute or otherwise, prevents its residents from actually sharing in the estates of New York decedents, the statute would not be unconstitutional under Zschernig v. Miller (389 U.S. 429) as interference in the field of foreign affairs reserved to the President and Congress. Consequently the impounding of the beneficiary's share was not proscribed.

2. Petitioner, by warrant of attachment with respect to the sum due the Soviet beneficiary, obtained a default judgment against her based on a cause of action for money owed, relying on a letter from her wherein she promised to repay money he had sent her and suggesting that he try to obtain the money out of her inheritance. A fund, although deposited in court, may be attached for the purpose of obtaining jurisdiction. In this matter the Supreme Court acquired jurisdiction and upon the beneficiary's default had power to enter judgment in favor of petitioner. Petitioner's judgment, on the present record and with the present parties, is both legally effective and valid.

3. Petitioner, as a judgment creditor of the beneficiary, may be a person, upon impounding of the fund, "who may thereafter appear to be entitled thereto" within section 269-a. The Surrogate has power to order satisfaction from the impounded fund if he first determined that such satisfaction would not conflict with the provisions or purposes of the statute. There is no rule completely immunizing the impounded fund from all obligations derived from the beneficiary. Only those obligations are excluded which rest solely on the voluntary transfer by, or commitment of, the beneficiary in contradiction of his disability to control the fund. The matter is remanded to enable the Surrogate to hold a hearing and make further findings concerning whether the underlying debt was a genuine one, in the sense that it was derived from an actual benefit to the beneficiary on a promise to pay, or was even a bona fide gift, and whether payment therefor to the judgment creditor would be solely for his use or benefit and would not be used directly or indirectly to defeat the purposes of the statute.

People v. Jiggetts


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


22 N.Y.2d 796; 239 N.E.2d 642; 292 N.Y.S.2d 898; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1255

Saratoga Harness Racing Ass'n v. Agric. & New York State Horse Breeding Dev. Fund


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


22 N.Y.2d 119; 238 N.E.2d 730; 291 N.Y.S.2d 335; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1366


February 20, 1968, Argued  
May 16, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff harness racing association challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which found that 1965 N.Y. Laws 567 was constitutional and that defendant horse breeding development fund was entitled to collect "breakage" moneys held by the association.


OVERVIEW: The harness racing association sought to enjoin the horse breeding development fund from collecting "breakage" moneys held by the association. The lower court entered judgment in favor of the fund, and the association appealed. The appellate division modified and affirmed as modified, and the association appealed. The court held that the legislation creating the fund and providing that pari-mutuel race tracks should pay a percentage of "breakage" to the fund did not violate N.Y. Const. art. I, § 9, which created an exception to the constitutional prohibition of gambling and permitted pari-mutuel betting on horse races as prescribed by the legislature and from which the state would derive a reasonable revenue for the support of government because harness racing was a source of substantial revenue for the support of government, and a certain portion of the revenues derived from racing could be set aside for the general improvement of the sport and the facilities used. The court held that the legislation did not violate N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 7 because the fund was not a fund under the management of the state as that term was employed in the constitution. The order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment finding that the legislation challenged by the harness racing association was constitutional, and that the horse breeding development fund was entitled to collect "breakage" moneys held by the association.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Horse Breeding Development Fund -- Agriculture and New York State Horse Breeding Development Fund, public benefit corporation, validly permitted to collect portion of "breakage" from racing associations to foster continued growth and prosperity of industry -- not in violation of New York State Constitution (art. I, § 9) which provides that State shall derive reasonable revenue for "support of government" from pari-mutuel betting -- no violation of State Constitution (art. VII, § 7) -- fund is not under management of State as that term is employed in Constitution.

1. The Agriculture and New York State Horse Breeding Development Fund, a public benefit corporation within the State Harness Racing Commission (Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law, § 55-a, as added by L. 1965, ch. 567), derives its revenues from private racing associations which are required to pay to it 25% of the "breaks". The fund is authorized to deposit the moneys received in several segregated accounts and to disburse them for various programs designed to foster the continued growth and prosperity of the industry. Section 9 of article I of the New York State Constitution permits pari-mutuel betting on horse races "as may be prescribed by the legislature and from which the state shall derive a reasonable revenue for the support of government". This provision does not require that all revenue in excess of expenses be devoted to the direct support of government or prohibit the Legislature, as a condition to granting a license to a private racing association, from requiring that a certain portion of the revenues derived from racing be set aside for the general improvement of the sport and the facilities used. The statute is not in violation of this constitutional provision.

2. Section 7 of article VII provides that "No money shall ever be paid out of the state treasury or any of its funds, or any of the funds under its management, except in pursuance of an appropriation by law; nor unless such payment be made within two years next after the passage of such appropriation act; and every such law making a new appropriation or continuing or reviving an appropriation, shall distinctly specify the sum appropriated, and the object or purpose to which it is to be applied; and it shall not be sufficient for such law to refer to any other law to fix such sum." The Agriculture and New York Horse Breeding Development Fund is not a fund under the management of the State as that term is employed in the Constitution.

 

254 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


State v. Perla


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 608; 237 N.E.2d 215; 289 N.Y.S.2d 957; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1441


February 19, 1968, Argued  
April 17, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State of New York (government), appealed a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondent city commissioner of street sanitation and denied the government's motion for summary judgment on the government's action to forfeit the commissioner's office for refusing to sign a waiver of immunity before a grand jury.


OVERVIEW: A grand jury conducted an inquiry into the commissioner's conduct while he was the head parking fee collector. He was subpoenaed before the grand jury, advised of the nature of the inquiry by the district attorney, offered a waiver of immunity covering his parking fee offices, and refused to sign it. The appellate court affirmed the grant of the commissioner's motion for summary judgment on the government's action brought pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that reasonably construed to give sensible effect to its policy purpose, the provision embraced the office of parking fee collector. The constitutional policy was to require any man holding a place in public service, high or low, to choose between keeping his job and disclosing his knowledge of criminal wrongdoing in that place. A public official had the undoubted right to claim a constitutional privilege against self incrimination, but the government had a concomitant right to discharge him from his job if his refusal to testify related to his public employment.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting summary judgment in favor of the commissioner on the government's action to forfeit his office was reversed and remitted with instructions to grant the government's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury -- waiver of immunity -- "public officer" -- Acting Head Parking Fee Collector and Parking Fee Collector for City of Buffalo was "public officer" (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6) and, having refused to sign waiver of immunity when subpoenaed before Grand Jury in inquiry into his conduct of parking fee collecting offices, may be removed from his position as Commissioner of Street Sanitation of City of Buffalo -- compulsion exerted by forfeiture of office is not violation of defendant's protection against self incrimination.

1. Defendant, from April, 1954 to January, 1966, was the Acting Head Parking Fee Collector and Parking Fee Collector for the City of Buffalo. In January, 1966 he was appointed Commissioner of Street Sanitation of the City of Buffalo. The Grand Jury of Erie County, in March, 1966, conducted an inquiry into defendant's conduct of the parking fee collecting offices and, when he was subpoenaed before the Grand Jury, he refused to sign a waiver of immunity. In this action by the People and the Attorney-General to forfeit defendant's present office of Commissioner of Street Sanitation pursuant to section 6 of article I of the New York Constitution, which provides that "any public officer" who refuses to sign a waiver of immunity against a subsequent criminal prosecution when called before a Grand Jury to testify concerning his present office or "any public office held by him" within five years "shall be removed from" his present office or shall forfeit it at the suit of the Attorney-General, a motion by plaintiffs for summary judgment should be granted. The parking fee collector positions held by the defendant come within the definition of "public office".

2. The compulsion exerted by the forfeiture of the office is not a violation of defendant's protection against self incrimination ( Spevack v. Klein, 385 U.S. 514, distinguished). A public official has the right to claim a constitutional privilege against self incrimination but the State has also a right to discharge him from his job if his refusal to testify relates to his public employment.

 

255 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dobkin v. Chapman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 490; 236 N.E.2d 451; 289 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1550


January 11, 1968, Argued  
February 29, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: In Dobkin v. Chapman: Order affirmed, without costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.

In Sellars v. Raye: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.

In Keller v. Rappoport: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, defendants sought review of orders from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) in which the court held that the method used by plaintiffs to serve defendants under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308(4) was proper.


OVERVIEW: In three cases consolidated for appeal, plaintiffs brought suits against defendants to recover for personal injuries sustained in an accident. Defendants objected to the method of service arguing that due process requirements were violated. Each defendant was served under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308(4). The appeals court affirmed the lower court's judgment holding that the notice given was adequate to inform defendants of the lawsuit. The appeals court held that 308(1)-(3) delineated specific methods for serving a defendant. However, according to the appeals court, if service was impracticable under 308(1)-(3), then a plaintiff could serve a defendant by using any method prescribed by the court. The appeals court held that 308(4) was limited only by the constitutional safeguard of due process. The appeals court held that plaintiffs' need, the public interest, the reasonableness of plaintiffs' efforts under all the circumstances to inform defendants, and the availability of other safeguards for defendants' interests were factors determining whether notice was adequate and due process was met.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the lower court's judgment in each case, holding that the method of service was adequate notice informing defendants of the lawsuit.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service in such manner as court directs -- in action arising out of automobile accident in New York, if whereabouts of resident or of nondomiciliary defendant are unknown, court may, ex parte, direct manner of service ( CPLR 308, subd. 4) if reasonably calculated, under circumstances of case, to give notice -- circumstances of case include plaintiff's need and efforts, State's interest in motor vehicle control, defendant's failure to communicate, insurance carrier's presence in case, and CPLR 317 providing that defendant who does not receive notice may be allowed to defend within one year after he learns of judgment -- orders under CPLR 308 (subd. 4), in three cases, directing service by mail to last known address and by other means, reasonable and proper.

1. In an action against a natural person for damages for injury or death arising from an automobile accident in New York, if it is shown that the defendant's whereabouts are unknown and that it is impracticable to serve him personally or by delivery to a duly designated agent or by mailing and affixing (under CPLR 308, subds. 1, 2, 3), personal jurisdiction of him may nevertheless be obtained by service "in such manner as the court" ex parte "directs" ( CPLR 308, subd. 4). An attachment is not a prerequisite to such an order. Nor does the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( §§ 253, 254) prescribe the sole method of service. Nor do the methods set forth in CPLR 308 apply to residents only; they apply to nondomiciliaries too, except that the service must be made by certain categories of persons ( CPLR 302, 313).

2. The court's broad discretion in directing a method of service under CPLR 308 (subd. 4) is subject to the due process requirement that the method be reasonably calculated to give notice. However, what might be inadequate notice in the situation in one case might be reasonable and adequate in the circumstances of another case.

3. In fashioning a method of notice sufficient to afford due process in the particular circumstances of a case, the court should consider the plaintiff's need and our State's interest as shown by the creation of the MVAIC (Insurance Law, art. 17-A) and the financial responsibility required in order to register a motor vehicle (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 312). Moreover, a person who has been involved in a motor vehicle accident knows that he is likely to be sued. He should not unduly obstruct the process of law by sedulously avoiding communication with the victim and with his own insurance carrier or by failing to notify his own State licensing authorities of his changes of address. The reasonableness of the plaintiff's efforts to inform the defendant should be judged in the light of such circumstances. Furthermore, the court should take into account that the defendant's insurance carrier or the MVAIC may really be the party in interest and that they have been apprised of or are present in the case. Finally, the court should consider that, if the defendant does not personally receive notice, he "may be allowed to defend the action within one year after he obtains knowledge of entry of the judgment, but in no event more than five years after such entry" ( CPLR 317).

4. Judged by the foregoing standards, proper orders for service under CPLR 308 (subd. 4) were made in each of these three cases; in all of them service was directed to be made by ordinary mail to defendants' last known addresses and in two of them by certain other means. Accordingly, motions by the attorneys for the defendant's insurance carrier in one case and by the MVAIC in two cases to set aside the service were properly denied.

 

256 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Udell v. Haas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 463; 235 N.E.2d 897; 288 N.Y.S.2d 888; 1968 N.Y. LEXIS 1567


January 8, 1968, Argued  
February 28, 1968, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs in all courts, and case remitted to Supreme Court, Nassau County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of the order of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division, Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court that Lake Success, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance No. 60, which rezoned part of the landowner's property from a business district to a residential district, was valid.


OVERVIEW: The lower courts had found that the rezoning was not valid with respect to the parcel to the west of a road dividing the parcel, but was valid with respect to the parcel to the east of the dividing road. The court held that the rezoning ordinance was invalid with respect to both parts of the parcel. The court concluded that the rezoning was not done in accordance with the comprehensive plan of the municipality, noting that the change was not the result of a deliberate change in community policy and was enacted without sufficient forethought or planning. The court noted that the parcel had been zoned for business for the previous 20 years, and that as a result of the zoning change, the landowner would lose $ 260,000, or 60 percent of its value. The court also concluded that the zoning change was discriminatory, as there was no showing of purpose in distinguishing the two parcels, and in fact there was conflicting testimony as to the suitability of each of the parcels for particular uses.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed, and the ordinance was declared ultra vires, unconstitutional, and void as to the landowner's property.


HEADNOTES

 Municipal corporations -- zoning -- amendment to village zoning ordinance reclassifying appellant's property from Business "A" and "B" to Residence "C" was ultra vires as not "in accordance with a comprehensive plan" (Village Law, § 177), and was also invalid as constituting unjustifiable discrimination and arbitrary imposition on rights of appellant who would sustain substantial loss by operation of ordinance.

1. A 1960 ordinance amending the Building Zone Ordinance of the Village of Lake Success by reclassifying appellant's property from Business "A" and "B" to Residence "C" must be held to be ultra vires as not meeting the requirement of section 177 of the Village Law that zoning be "in accordance with a comprehensive plan". Accordingly, the ordinance was invalid with respect to that part of appellant's land located on the east side of Lakeville Road, a main north-south traffic artery of the village. The amendment did not conform to the village's general development policy as set forth in a 1958 statement of zoning policy, and was also inconsistent with what had, for more than 20 years, been the fundamental rationale of the village's zoning law and map. Moreover, the radical change from business to residential use, recommended by the village planning board on the evening of the same day on which appellant's associate had submitted a preliminary sketch for development of the area with a bowling alley and supermarket, and adopted a month or so later, was not the result of a deliberate change in community policy, but was enacted without sufficient foresight or planning, and was not supported by any particular conditions existing in the area. This race to the statute books was not in accord with sound zoning principles and the root cause of this failure was a basic misconception of the nature of zoning. Even assuming that, as the village argues, there was no longer any need for shopping facilities in the area, this would not explain why consideration was given only to rezoning the area as residential, whereas the community's need for taxables, as set forth in its "developmental policy" statement, required consideration of other possible land use controls.

2. Furthermore, the ordinance was invalid as constituting unjustifiable discrimination. In finding that the amendment discriminated against that portion of appellant's property on the west side of Lakeville Road because the rezoning did not include a nearby retail service area, the trial court pointed out that there was nothing to differentiate between these two properties, and, moreover, that the amendment likewise discriminated against the appellant's premises on the east side, the subject of this appeal, for that same reason (and, additionally, because most of the east parcel was already being used for a nonconforming commercial use as a restaurant). By thereafter rezoning the west parcel into a new Business "C" category and by withdrawing its appeal from the trial court's holding the amendment invalid as discriminating against that west parcel, the village thus accepted such finding of invalidity. That being so, the village was bound to show that dissimilar treatment of the east parcel was still warranted. However, the village offered no acceptable reason to justify the distinction. Although the serious traffic conditions on Lakeville Road might have substantially justified the rezoning, the village's expert testified that business use of the east parcel would create less of a traffic problem than such use of the west parcel so that the west parcel should be restricted to residential use, but the east parcel could properly be zoned for nonresidential use inasmuch as access thereto could be restricted to another street. That concession removed any basis for argument that needs of the zoning plan required different treatments of the east and west parcels.

3. Consideration of the fact that, beside the lack of any showing of purpose in distinguishing the two parcels, the appellant would sustain a substantial loss -- amounting to some 60% of the property value, or $ 260,000 -- if the zoning change is upheld makes the invalidity of the ordinance unquestionably clear.

In re Estate of Leikind


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

259 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Liberty Nat'l Bank & Trust Co. v. Buscaglia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 357; 235 N.E.2d 101; 288 N.Y.S.2d 33; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 987


October 24, 1967, Argued  
December 29, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant director of county sales tax (director) challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which held that (1) certain sales and use taxes could not be imposed on the receipts from sales and services made or rendered to respondent bank, and (2) directed the director to refund the taxes collected from the bank.


OVERVIEW: The bank claimed that because it was an instrumentality of the United States Government it was immune from sales and use taxes imposed by the state and county. The trial court agreed, prohibiting the director from levying the taxes and ordering him to refund taxes paid. On appeal, the bank claimed that a national bank was an instrumentality of the federal government. The court found that while immunity was justified during the period when national banks issued currency, the tax-immune status of current national banks was not based on its issuance of currency. The court held that the bank did not qualify for immunity because the bank performed no governmental service and the taxes were not discriminatory. Privately owned businesses that performed services for the government and/or operated by virtue of a federal charter were not per se immune from nondiscriminatory state taxation which did not impede the performance of their service for government. The judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the bank forbidding the director from collecting certain taxes and ordering the refund of certain taxes was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- national banks -- Liberty National Bank and Trust Company not such instrumentality of United States Government as would make it immune from sales and use taxes imposed by New York State and Erie County.

1. Liberty National Bank and Trust Company is not such an instrumentality of the United States Government as would make it immune as a purchaser from sales and use taxes imposed by the State of New York and Erie County.

2. National banks perform no governmental functions. Like State banks, they are privately owned and operated. Also like State banks, they are depositories for Federal funds. And, like any private enterprise engaging in activities affected with a public interest, they are subject to severe government regulation, which is accomplished through membership in the Federal Reserve System -- membership which is open to State banks upon a voluntary basis.

3. Privately owned and operated business enterprises, which perform services for the government or exist and operate by virtue of a Federal charter or franchise, are not impliedly immune from State taxation, provided that the taxation does not impede the performance of their service for the government or the right which the government charter or franchise granted and, further, that the taxation does not discriminate against the federally chartered institutions.

4. The taxes here involved are not discriminatory.

5. Section 548 of title 12 of the United States Code, the only Federal statute dealing with State taxation of national banks, does not indicate an intent to immunize national banks from nondiscriminatory State taxation.

Simpson v. Loehmann


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 305; 234 N.E.2d 669; 287 N.Y.S.2d 633; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 984; 33 A.L.R.3d 979


November 1, 1967, Argued  
December 29, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied, see 21 N Y 2d 990.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Connecticut citizen appealed, by permission, an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to vacate a warrant of attachment against a liability insurance policy. The question was certified as to whether the trial court's order denying defendant's motion was properly made.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, an infant and his father, brought a negligence suit in New York against defendant Connecticut citizen for injuries suffered by the infant when he was cut by the propeller of a boat owned by defendant in the waters off of Connecticut. Unable to obtain personal jurisdiction over defendant in New York, plaintiffs had the summons and complaint delivered to defendant at his Connecticut residence, and they attached a liability policy issued to defendant in Connecticut by a Pennsylvania insurance corporation doing business in New York. Defendant filed a motion to vacate the attachment on jurisdictional and due process grounds. The trial court denied the motion, and the intermediate court affirmed. The question was certified as to whether the trial court's order denying defendant's motion was properly made. The court answered in the affirmative, holding that the contractual obligation of the insurance company to its insured under its liability policy constituted a debt under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5201, and as such, was subject to attachment at the suit of an injured third party under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6202.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified question in the affirmative, that the trial court's order denying defendant Connecticut citizen's motion to vacate a warrant of attachment against a liability insurance policy was properly made.


HEADNOTES

Attachment -- attachment of "debt" -- contractual rights of nonresident insured under liability policy issued by insurer doing business in New York constitute attachable debt and upon attachment gives court in rem jurisdiction of defendant -- Seider v. Roth (17 N Y 2d 111) controls.

The contractual rights of a nonresident insured defendant under a policy of liability insurance issued by an insurer doing business in New York constitute a "debt" owed to the nonresident insured defendant which is subject to attachment and when attached gives the court in rem jurisdiction over defendant. Seider v. Roth (17 N Y 2d 111) controls. (Reargument denied, see 21 N Y 2d 990.)

Spahn v. Julian Messner, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 124; 233 N.E.2d 840; 286 N.Y.S.2d 832; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1008; 30 A.L.R.3d 196


October 23, 1967, Reargued  
December 28, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, an author and a publisher, sought review of a decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a trial court's decision enjoining the author and publisher from disseminating a book about plaintiff athlete and awarding damages to the athlete.


OVERVIEW: The author and the publisher attempted to disseminate a book about the athlete, a public figure. The trial court enjoined such publication and awarded damages to the athlete. The trial court order was upheld in previous appeals. The United States Supreme Court vacated the decision and ordered the matter set for reargument in light of newly decided case law. Upon reconsideration, the trial court again enjoined publication and awarded damages to the athlete. On appeal, the injunction was again affirmed. The athlete demonstrated that the author and publisher's unauthorized presentation of his life was infected with material and substantial falsification and that the book was being published with reckless disregard for the truth. The author, by his own admission, had invented dialogue, supplied imaginary incidents, and attributed thoughts and feelings to the athlete. The author had not interviewed the athlete or his teammates, but had relied solely on unverified newspaper and magazine articles for his information. The defect in the athlete's complaint was purely technical and did not warrant reversal because the author and publisher failed to allege any resulting prejudice.


OUTCOME: The decision affirming a trial court's decision enjoining the author and publisher from disseminating a book about the athlete and awarding damages was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- right of privacy -- in addition to other requirements of Civil Rights Law ( §§ 50, 51), public figure such as prominent baseball player must show, and did show, that unauthorized fictionalized biography was published with knowledge of falsification or with reckless disregard of truth; judgment for such plaintiff affirmed -- defendants were not prejudiced by complaint's lack of allegation of knowing falsity or reckless disregard of truth.

1. Before a public figure -- in this case, a prominent professional baseball player -- may be awarded damages or an injunction against the publication of an unauthorized, fictionalized presentation of his life, he must show, in addition to the other requirements of the Civil Rights Law ( §§ 50, 51), that the presentation is infected with material and substantial falsification and was published with knowledge of such falsification or with reckless disregard for the truth ( Time, Inc. v. Hill, 385 U.S. 374, followed). Such is the case here, where the evidence showed that, for purposes of selling a biography for children to read, the author invented incidents and dialogues, as well as thoughts and feelings, and knowingly manipulated the chronology and the facts of the hero's childhood, his relationship to his father, his courtship of his wife, important events during their marriage, and his military experience including a fictionalized Bronze Star. A judgment of injunction and damages was properly awarded against the author and the publisher.

2. The defendants were not prejudiced by the complaint's lack of an allegation of knowing falsity or reckless disregard of truth; moreover, a motion was made to conform the pleading to the proof.
