People v. Katz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 132; 233 N.E.2d 845; 286 N.Y.S.2d 839; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1009


October 30, 1967, Argued  
December 28, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed the defendant's conviction for encumbering or obstructing a street with an article or thing pursuant to New York City, New York, Admin. Code § 692h-1.0.


OVERVIEW: Near a street intersection in Queens, the defendant, a 20-year-old college student, set up a 3'x3' card table, to which was pinned a sign that read: "Stop the War in Viet Nam." On the table were various pamphlets containing information relating to the Vietnam situation, and the defendant requested people who stopped at the table write to their congressmen or the president protesting policies in Vietnam. The defendant was convicted of obstructing a street under § 692h-1.0 and appealed. The court reversed the conviction and dismissed the charges, holding that: (1) in proscribing any obstruction "whatsoever," § 692h-1.0 placed on law enforcement officers the burden of determining in each case what, in their judgment, constituted an obstruction; (2) not every object on a public street necessarily obstructed it; (3) the broad prohibitive language of the statute together with the lack of any defined standards for judging violations rendered it peculiarly susceptible to arbitrary enforcement; thus, (4) § 692h-1.0 was unconstitutional, particularly where it operated to restrain the exercise of First Amendment rights to free speech.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and dismissed the charge.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- obstruction of streets -- section 692h-1.0 of Administrative Code of City of New York, which provides that "It shall be unlawful for any person * * * to incumber or obstruct any street * * * with any article or thing whatsoever", is unconstitutional.

Section 692h-1.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York provides that "It shall be unlawful for any person * * * to incumber or obstruct any street * * * with any article or thing whatsoever." Its susceptibility to arbitrary enforcement and its use of total prohibition rather than reasonable regulation render the statute unconstitutional.

 

263 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Goldman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 152; 234 N.E.2d 194; 287 N.Y.S.2d 7; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1012


October 23, 1967, Argued  
December 28, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of perjury in the first degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 1620, 1620-a. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a former police officer, was told that in order to maintain his employment he had to sign a limited waiver of immunity. Defendant signed the waiver then testified before a grand jury investigating bribery of public officials. Defendant during his testimony denied that he ever met a certain gambler or ever accepted bribes from him. Evidence was offered to the grand jury and at defendant's trial of a four-year old photograph of a man resembling defendant handing money to the gambler. The court held that a public officer could properly have been compelled to testify before a grand jury even though his testimony was the product of "duress" by way of the threat of removal from public office. Any waiver exerted by the duress did not prohibit defendant from exercising his right against self-incrimination. But defendant could still have been legally sworn. The perjury charges were brought well within the limitations period. The fact that the picture was of an incident four years old was only one factor for the jury to have considered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for perjury in the first degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- perjury -- police officer who signed waiver of immunity for testimony before Grand Jury under compulsion of losing his job, while immune from prosecution for substantive crime, could be sworn and could be prosecuted for perjurious testimony -- defendant was properly convicted of perjury -- record of testimony before Grand Jury established that defendant denied knowing gambler, and testimony of People's witnesses established that denials were false -- delay of four years between meeting with gambler and defendant's testimony before Grand Jury was factor considered by jurors.

1. The signing of a waiver of immunity by a police officer who is called before a Grand Jury is done under compulsion of losing his job (New York City Charter, § 1123), is void under Garrity v. New Jersey (385 U.S. 493) and is ineffective to prohibit him from claiming the privilege against self incrimination with respect to a substantive crime. However, the oath may be legally administered and he is compelled to testify. While he is immune from prosecution for substantive crimes, he may be subsequently prosecuted for perjurious testimony. Accordingly, defendant police officer who signed a waiver of immunity and testified falsely that he did not know a named gambler was properly convicted of perjury.

2. The record of defendant's testimony before the Grand Jury establishes that he there testified that he did not know said named gambler and did not receive anything from him. The falsity of these denials was proved by the testimony of the People's witnesses.

3. The delay of more than four years between the meeting with said gambler and defendant's appearance before the Grand Jury was a factor to be considered by the jurors in determining whether defendant could not remember meeting said gambler, and they, in view of defendant's outright denials, must have found that time had not clouded defendant's memory.

 

264 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Seitz v. Drogheo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 181; 234 N.E.2d 209; 287 N.Y.S.2d 29; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1015


December 4, 1967, Argued  
December 28, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the family court's orders enforcing a foreign alimony decree in an action that the wife brought against respondent husband.


OVERVIEW: The wife and her husband were divorced in Mexico. The wife filed an action to enforce the alimony decree and to recover arrears thereunder. The family court issued two orders requiring the husband to begin paying the alimony and the arrears. The appellate court reversed the family court's orders, holding that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 466(c), which authorized the family court to enforce and modify decrees of alimony granted by foreign courts, was unconstitutional for the reason that, under the Constitution of New York, the family court had no jurisdiction to enforce or modify the support provision of a foreign divorce decree, and the legislature did not have the authority to confer such jurisdiction on the family court. The court reversed the appellate court's order. The legislature had the power under N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 7(c) to confer such jurisdiction on the family court because matrimonial actions were a "new class of actions and proceedings" over which the state courts had no jurisdiction under the common law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order that reversed the family court's orders enforcing a foreign alimony decree in an action that the wife brought against the husband.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- jurisdiction -- Family Court -- Legislature had authority under State Constitution (art. VI, § 7, subd. c) to confer upon Family Court power to entertain request to enforce or modify provisions of foreign matrimonial decree (cf. Family Ct. Act, § 466, subd. [c]) -- proceeding to enforce and modify alimony and support provisions of foreign decrees without regard to grounds upon which decrees were granted is new class of proceeding -- under State Constitution, jurisdiction over such new class of proceeding could be given to Family Court.

1. The Legislature had the authority under article VI (§ 7, subd. c) of the State Constitution to confer upon the Family Court the powers contained in subdivision (c) of section 466 of the Family Court Act to entertain a request to enforce or modify the provisions of a foreign matrimonial decree.

2. Section 7 of article VI of the Constitution reads as follows: "a. The supreme court shall have general original jurisdiction in law and equity * * * c. If the legislature shall create new classes of actions and proceedings, the supreme court shall have jurisdiction over such classes of actions and proceedings, but the legislature may provide that another court or other courts shall also have jurisdiction and that actions and proceedings of such classes may be originated in such other court or courts." The right to commence a proceeding to enforce or modify the provisions of a foreign divorce decree in the courts of this State constitutes a new class of action or proceeding. The "new classes of actions and proceedings" are those which the Supreme Court would be without jurisdiction to entertain -- actions and proceedings which were unknown at common law. Prior to the enactment of section 466, the courts of this State were without jurisdiction to entertain a request to enforce or modify the provisions of a foreign matrimonial decree unless the decree was entered on grounds which were recognized in this State. Subdivision (c) of section 466 of the Family Court Act empowered the Family Court to entertain applications to enforce and modify the alimony and support provisions of foreign decrees irrespective of the grounds upon which the decrees were granted. Since the right to commence such proceedings was not recognized at common law and could not, therefore, have been exercised prior to the amendment of section 466, it should be viewed as a new class of proceeding.

 

265 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Gonzalez v. Warden, Brooklyn House of Detention


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 18; 233 N.E.2d 265; 286 N.Y.S.2d 240; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1039


October 31, 1967, Argued  
December 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) dismissing his writ of habeas corpus and remanding him to custody.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was charged with assault and robbery of a police officer. The prisoner could not raise bail, which was set at $ 1,000. The trial court dismissed the prisoner's first writ of habeas corpus, holding that the bail was reasonable. The appellate division dismissed the prisoner's second writ of habeas corpus, holding that the lower courts had not abused their discretion in setting the bail amount. On appeal, the court affirmed. The lower courts were well within their discretion in determining that $ 1,000 bail was necessary for assuring the prisoner's presence. The court refused to adopt a nonfinancially oriented bail system, holding that was the province of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the writ of habeas corpus and remanding the prisoner to custody.


HEADNOTES

Bail -- amount of bail -- bail was properly set at $ 1,000 for relator who was arrested and charged with assault and robbery of police officer -- adoption of nonfinancially oriented system of bail is matter for Legislature, not court -- even if rules of such system were applied, relator would not be entitled to release on his own recognizance.

1. Relator was arrested and charged with assault and robbery of a police officer. Bail was ultimately set at $ 1,000 but relator was unable to raise the bail or secure a bond for that amount. A writ of habeas corpus was properly dismissed.

2. Although the present bail system may be subject to abuse, this court will not adopt a nonfinancially oriented system of bail. The adoption of such a system is more properly within the province of the Legislature.

3. Even if this court were to adopt a bail reform system along the lines of the Federal Bail Reform Act of 1966, the relator would still not be entitled to release on his own recognizance. The Bail Reform Act directs a Judge or Magistrate to release a defendant on his personal recognizance or upon the execution of an unsecured appearance bond if he determines that such terms will reasonably assure the defendant's presence. In determining whether or not a defendant should be released on his own recognizance, a Judge would take into consideration the same factors which are applied in a financially oriented bail system. Applying these criteria, the lower courts did not abuse their discretion in setting bail at $ 1,000 and in denying relator's request that he be released on his own recognizance or on $ 100 bail. The relator is accused of a vicious crime and is subject to a possible sentence of 13 years in prison. He has been in New York for only three years. While relator contributes to the support of his father, brother and sister, there is no evidence that they are dependent upon relator for their support. It was well within the courts' discretion to determine that defendant's roots in the community were not strong enough to assure relator's appearance for trial if he were released on his own recognizance or on $ 100 bail.

 

266 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Proceedings v. Sarisohn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 36; 233 N.E.2d 276; 286 N.Y.S.2d 255; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1042


September 25, 1967, Argued  
December 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and matter remitted to the Appellate Division, Second Department, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judge sought review of an order of respondent, the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which removed him from office and prohibited him from thereafter holding any judicial office, appointive or elective.


OVERVIEW: The judge was charged with advising a prostitute on a criminal prosecution pending in another court and on how she could continue to practice her trade; confining a defendant, who was not a witness when she refused to disclose information; imposing excessive bail; and bringing untoward pressure to bear upon trial counsel in abusive language. The only evidence of the consultation with the prostitute was their telephone conversations, which were intercepted under eavesdropping orders obtained to survey the prostitute's phone. Reversing the appellate court's decision, the court held that (1) the judge, as a party to the conversations, had standing to challenge the orders, and (2) the wiretaps were illegal because the orders were obtained with insufficient affidavits and thus there was no evidence to support the charge. The court concluded that although there was substantial evidence to support the other charges, they were insufficient to justify the sanction of debarment. The court noted that it was immaterial that some of the charges involved conduct that occurred while the judge was a justice of the peace because the conduct affected general character and fitness for office.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order removing the judge from office and prohibiting him form holding any other judicial office and remanded the case for further proceedings on the charge involving advising a prostitute. The court ordered that if that charge was dismissed on remand, the sanction based on the remaining charges was to be reconsidered.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- removal -- appeal lies to Court of Appeals from order of Appellate Division removing Judge from office -- Appellate Division has power to determine sufficiency of cause for removal -- charge that Judge, while he was Justice of Peace, advised prostitute not sustained where evidence consisted of intercepted conversations under eavesdropping orders not properly obtained -- either party on intercepted telephone line could object -- charge that Judge caused woman to be placed in jail to coerce disclosure of evidence supported by substantial evidence -- charge of imposing excessive bail properly sustained -- charge that he improperly intervened in case involving traffic summons sustained -- charge that he instructed court attache to obliterate docket entries supported by evidence -- proper in this proceeding to consider his conduct prior to election to District Court -- charge that he displayed prejudice against attorneys sustained -- matter remanded to Appellate Division to consider proper disposition if first charge cannot be established by proper evidence and, if first charge survives, issues of claim of privilege against self incrimination and attorney-client privilege.

1. An appeal lies from the order of the Appellate Division which removed appellant from his office as Suffolk County District Court Judge. While it had been held that an appeal would not lie in such case because a removal proceeding was not a special proceeding, the statutes have been broadened to embrace within an "action or special proceeding" any judicial proceeding ( CPLR 103, subd. [b]; 105, subd. [d]). The State Constitution does not contain any restrictive definition of an action or special proceeding (art. VI, § 3).

2. The Appellate Division, which has the power of removal for cause, has the power to decide upon the sufficiency or insufficiency of a cause. The Court of Appeals, however, may determine if there is a real or substantial cause.

3. One of the charges sustained accused appellant of having advised and consulted with a prostitute, while he was a Justice of the Peace, on how she should conduct herself in a pending criminal prosecution against her in another court and how she could, with impunity, continue to practice her trade. The sole source of the evidence consists of telephone conversations between appellant and the prostitute, all of which were intercepted under eavesdropping orders obtained to survey the telephone of the prostitute. The orders were not obtained in accord with the statutory requirements. The wiretaps were illegal and the evidence not usable. The charge is, therefore, not sustained if appellant has standing to object to the illegality of the wiretaps.

4. Appellant was not the subscriber but he was the other conversationalist on the intercepted telephone. The test as to who should have the right to object to the interception should be the same as that in the Federal cases, which do permit either party, on the intercepted line, to object. The charge, therefore, cannot be sustained.

5. There is substantial evidence to support a charge that, in an action to recover for property damage arising from an automobile collision, appellant, acting as a District Court Judge, caused a woman to be confined to a cell for a time when she refused to disclose the name of the owner of the automobile involved in the collision, seeking to coerce the disclosure from her. It was reasonable for the Appellate Division to consider such conduct as a ground for imposing a sanction.

6. A charge of imposing grossly excessive bail was supported by substantial evidence and properly sustained.

7. The Appellate Division also sustained the charge which accused appellant of injecting himself improperly into the acceptance of a plea of guilty before the return day of a traffic summons, without having been assigned to the court in which the summons was returnable and without having before him the counterpart papers customary in disposing of a traffic summons. The question whether this is "cause" is one for the determination of the Appellate Division.

8. A charge that while appellant was a Justice of the Peace he had instructed a court attache to obliterate docket entries directing the entry of a default judgment against the tenant in a landlord-tenant summary proceeding, without notice to the landlord's lawyer, on a telephoned statement by the tenant's lawyer that there had been no notice of the trial date, was supported by substantial evidence and properly sustained.

9. Other charges sustained by the Appellate Division are based on appellant's conduct as a District Court Judge in displaying overt prejudice against an attorney of record in two cases before him and in bringing untoward pressure to bear upon trial counsel in abusive and improper language. Substantial evidence supports these charges and the Appellate Division could find them cause for the imposition of sanctions.

10. It was proper in this proceeding to consider appellant's conduct prior to his election to the District Court.

11. The matter should be remanded to the Appellate Division and, if the first charge cannot be established by proper evidence, the Appellate Division should consider the disposition to be made on the basis of the remaining charges.

12. If the first charge survives, then the issues of the claim of privilege against self incrimination and the attorney-client privilege in connection with the first charge become material and, for the reasons stated in Matter of Gardner v. Broderick (20 N Y 2d 227), the claim of privilege with regard to matters otherwise available as charges for prior misconduct while holding a judicial office might properly have been considered cause for removal by the Appellate Division.

 

267 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morhouse


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 66; 233 N.E.2d 705; 286 N.Y.S.2d 657; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1046


October 30, 1967, Argued  
December 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified and matter remitted to Supreme Court, New York County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with offenses related to bribery. The trial court entered judgment, upon a jury verdict, convicting defendant of bribery and taking unlawful fees in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 378 and 1826. On review, the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department affirmed. Defendant appealed again.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a prominent political leader. In a six-count indictment he was accused of having been a party to the bribing of the former chair of the state liquor authority by the backers of a private club. The object of the bribe was to secure a restaurant liquor license. Defendant's convictions for bribery and taking unlawful fees were predicated upon his aiding and abetting in the commission of those acts. After the appellate division affirmed the judgment of conviction, defendant again sought review asserting multiple errors. On review, the court held defendant's guilt as an aider and abettor was proved beyond a reasonable doubt, with sufficient corroboration of the testimony of his three accomplices. However, the court found, and the prosecution conceded, that defendant was entitled to a full evidentiary hearing on the question of whether court orders authorizing certain eavesdrops were properly issued and executed. In addition, the court directed, if there were improper eavesdrops, it was necessary to determine whether the prosecution's evidence at defendant's trial was tainted by such improper eavesdropping.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed as modified, with a remand for a full hearing as to any claimed violations of defendant's rights through eavesdrops.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- bribery -- taking unlawful fees -- evidence sufficient for jury to find defendant guilty of bribery and taking unlawful fees where he allegedly aided and abetted in commission of those crimes -- accomplice testimony sufficiently corroborated -- defendant cannot challenge eavesdroppings where his own privacy not invaded but may object to eavesdropping on telephone in his own office -- defendant entitled to hearing on question whether eavesdrops were properly authorized -- decision in Berger v. New York (388 U.S. 41) not to be given retroactive application -- more complete record required.

1. Defendant was convicted of the crimes of bribery (former Penal Law, § 378) and taking unlawful fees (former Penal Law, § 1826), predicated upon his allegedly having aided and abetted in the commission of those crimes (see former Penal Law, § 2). He was accused of having been a party to the bribing of the former Chairman of the State Liquor Authority by the backers of the New York Playboy Club to secure a restaurant liquor license. The evidence was sufficient for the jury to find him guilty.

2. The accomplice testimony given in evidence against defendant was sufficiently corroborated.

3. Certain eavesdroppings conducted by the prosecution under court orders issued pursuant to section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, as to which defendant conceded his own privacy was not invaded, cannot be challenged by defendant.

4. Defendant's own business office was bugged and phone calls, to which he was a party, were tapped. Defendant has standing to object to this eavesdropping.

5. Defendant is entitled to a full evidentiary hearing on the question whether the court orders authorizing these eavesdrops were properly issued and executed. Further, if there were improper eavesdrops, it must be determined whether the People's evidence on defendant's trial was "tainted" by such improper eavesdropping.

6. On remand, however, the decision in Berger v. New York (388 U.S. 41), which declared section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure unconstitutional, is not to be given retroactive application. Therefore, section 813-a is applicable to this case.

7. On remand, the trial court must also consider defendant's claim that the prosecution, in failing to disclose additional eavesdrops, wrongfully suppressed evidence that might have helped the defendant and his claim that the prosecution, by means of this eavesdropping, interfered with his Sixth Amendment right to the effective assistance of counsel. A reversal of the conviction is not required. A remand for a full hearing on these questions is sufficient.

8. A more complete record should be made also on the prosecution's conduct in maintaining an electronic surveillance of defendant's office after the defendant had become a suspect in the investigation and after the prosecution had learned that he was accustomed to conferring with his then attorney at this office.

 

268 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kaiser


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 86; 233 N.E.2d 818; 286 N.Y.S.2d 801; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1047


October 26, 1967, Argued  
December 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), affirmed a judgment of the county court rendered upon a verdict convicting defendant of coercion, attempted extortion, and conspiracy to commit extortion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of coercion, attempted extortion, and conspiracy to commit extortion. His conviction was based substantially on evidence procured through a wiretap. On appeal, he contended that a decision by the United States Supreme Court struck down as unconstitutional on its face the statute that authorized the wiretap order and therefore the evidence seized pursuant to that order was erroneously admitted. The court concluded that the Supreme Court decision did not affect the wiretapping provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 813-a. The decision affected only the language that permitted a trespassory invasion of the home, by general warrant, contrary to the Fourth Amendment. The court also declined to hold that wiretap evidence obtained in substantial compliance with the strictest provisions of the United States and New York Constitutions must be excluded in criminal prosecutions in New York. Therefore, defendant's conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court that affirmed the county court's conviction of defendant. The wiretap order used to procure evidence used in aid of defendant's conviction was in substantial compliance with the strictest provisions of the federal and state constitutions.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- wiretapping -- although Berger v. New York (388 U.S. 41) struck down as unconstitutional those portions of section 813-a of Code of Criminal Procedure which authorized bugging orders, reversal of conviction of coercion, attempted extortion and conspiracy to commit extortion not required -- assuming Berger decision brought wiretapping within purview of Fourth Amendment that decision not applicable retroactively -- United States Supreme Court did not hold that wiretapping constitutes violation of Fourth Amendment -- evidence admissible in State court, although obtained in violation of section 605 of Federal Communications Act -- section 813-a construed to conform with requirements indicated in Berger opinion -- rehabilitation of statute proper, although declared unconstitutional on its face.

1. Although in Berger v. New York (388 U.S. 41) the United States Supreme Court struck down as unconstitutional those portions of section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure which authorized bugging orders, a reversal of defendant's conviction of coercion, attempted extortion and conspiracy to commit extortion is not required.

2. The Court of Appeals has held that the expressions "oath or affirmation" and "reasonable grounds to believe" contained in the statute require the same factual basis to be shown for the issuance of the eavesdropping order as in the case of search warrants, and applied the same constitutional standards governing ordinary searches to eavesdropping cases. The procedure of antecedent justification before a Magistrate was required and no wiretap or bugging order could issue except upon an adequate factual showing -- probable cause to believe that a crime had been committed and that the conversations particularized in the order would be seized. The order here in question complied with this requirement as with all the requirements of the statute. Although there were certain defects, as the opinion in the Berger case indicates, the reason for the defects was that there had been no previous decision outlining the necessity for those requirements which the Berger case found to be mandatory. Assuming that the Berger case brought wiretapping within the purview of the Fourth Amendment, a reversal of the conviction would not be warranted either under the decisions of the Court of Appeals or of the Supreme Court.

3. The Berger decision is applicable only to those cases in which the orders were issued or eavesdropping took place after the date of that decision.

4. The Supreme Court did not state that wiretapping as well as bugging constitutes a violation of the Fourth Amendment and it failed to overrule Olmstead v. United States (277 U.S. 438), which held that wiretapping accomplished without a trespass into the person's home or other premises does not violate the Fourth Amendment's prohibition against unreasonable searches and seizures. The wiretapping provisions of section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure were not affected by the decision in the Berger case. What was affected was only that language which would permit a trespassory invasion of the home, by general warrant, contrary to the Fourth Amendment.

5. Although the evidence was obtained in violation of section 605 of the Federal Communications Act and such wiretap evidence is inadmissible in Federal prosecutions, the States are not required to exclude such evidence.

6. In conformity with the traditional policy to construe statutes in such manner as to uphold their constitutionality, and in conformity with the specific policy of construing section 813-a so as to provide the same protections as are afforded by the Fourth Amendment, the Court of Appeals holds that, in addition to a showing of probable cause, an order issued under the statute must particularize to the extent possible the exact nature of the conversations sought, that it must provide for a return, that it must be severely limited in time, as outlined in the Berger opinion, and that it must provide for termination as soon as the conversations are seized. In addition, it should allege that exigent circumstances exist, viz., that the "destruction" of the object of the "search" will result unless the notice requirement is waived.

7. This rehabilitation of the statute is possible even though the Supreme Court struck the statute down on its face.

8. In the case at bar no special proceeding to obtain a limiting construction of the statute is necessary before it is applied.

 

269 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kerma Restaurant Corp. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


21 N.Y.2d 111; 233 N.E.2d 833; 286 N.Y.S.2d 822; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1048


October 30, 1967, Argued  
December 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs in all courts, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner restaurant owner challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which, in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, confirmed a determination of respondent State Liquor Authority revoking the restaurant owner's special on-premises liquor license.


OVERVIEW: The restaurant owner's retail liquor license was annulled by the authority on a charge that it suffered or permitted the licensed premises to become disorderly in that it permitted homosexuals, degenerates, and undesirables to be and remain on the licensed premises and conduct themselves in an offensive and indecent manner contrary to good morals. The court reversed the appellate court's decision and annulled the authority's determination. There was no proof of any breach of the peace. There was no evidence to attribute to the restaurant owner knowledge that a police officer had been solicited by a customer for homosexual purposes and the proof of the transaction should have been eliminated from consideration as offering any support of the finding of disorder. The solicitation was an essential basis of the authority's decision to sustain the charge. Indulgence in the inference that certain men in a grill containing 40 people were, from their dress and makeup, homosexuals did not support the additional inference that they would have created disorder. It was reasonable to think that a homosexual might be orderly in the sense in which the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law defined order.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision, which had confirmed a determination of the authority revoking the restaurant owner's special on-premises liquor license. The court annulled the authority's determination.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- licenses -- petitioner's retail liquor license was annulled on charge that it permitted licensed premises to become disorderly in that it permitted homosexuals, degenerates and undesirables to be on premises and conduct themselves in offensive manner -- no proof of breach of peace and no evidence to attribute to licensee knowledge that police officer had been solicited by customer -- determination should be annulled.

Petitioner's retail liquor license has been annulled on a charge that it suffered or permitted the licensed premises to become disorderly ( Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 106, subd. 6) "in that it permitted homosexuals, degenerates and/or undesirables to be and remain on the licensed premises and conduct themselves in an offensive and indecent manner contrary to good morals". There is no proof in the record of any breach of the peace and no substantial evidence to warrant cancellation of the license on the grounds that the premises were disorderly. There is no evidence to attribute to the licensee knowledge that a police officer had been solicited by a customer for homosexual purposes. Indulgence in the inference that several men in the grill containing 40 people were, from their dress and makeup, homosexuals, does not support the additional inference that they would create disorder. Assuming from the proof as a whole that the licensee knew some homosexuals were in the place, that is not sufficient, and the rest of the proof tending to show disorder is insufficient. The determination of the State Liquor Authority should be annulled.

 

270 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Trapp


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 613; 233 N.E.2d 110; 286 N.Y.S.2d 11; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1078


September 21, 1967, Argued  
November 30, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgment of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed their conviction for failing to contribute to welfare funds in violation of former N.Y. Penal Law. § 962-a (now N.Y. Lab. Law § 198-c).


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a corporation and its president, were convicted of failing to contribute to welfare funds in violation of § 962-a. The appellate court affirmed. Defendants appealed. The court affirmed because the evidence was sufficient to establish that the corporation did not pay moneys due the union pension and welfare funds under a collective bargaining agreement and that the president's position was such that he knew or should have known of the nonpayment. The court held that the fact that § 962-a gave the victim a civil remedy did not deprive the legislature of its power to prescribe criminal penalties for employers who wrongfully withheld benefits. The court found that the president, as the only active officer and principal shareholder, was intimately involved with the corporation's affairs and could not have been exculpated as a nominal officer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for failing to contribute.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- failure to pay money to union pension and welfare funds -- section 962-a of former Penal Law (now Labor Law, § 198-c), which provided that employer which fails to pay amounts due under agreement to pay or provide benefits or wage supplements shall be guilty of misdemeanor, is not unconstitutional -- evidence sustains charge of failure of corporation to pay moneys due union pension and welfare funds -- Legislature had right to place responsibility of enforcing compliance with statute on "president, secretary, treasurer or officers exercising corresponding functions".

1. Section 962-a of the former Penal Law (now Labor Law, § 198-c) provided that "any employer who is party to an agreement to pay or provide benefits, or wage supplements as defined in section two hundred twenty of the labor law, and who fails, neglects or refuses to pay the amount or amounts necessary to provide such benefits or furnish such supplements within thirty days after such payments are required to be made, shall be guilty of a misdemeanor * * *. Where such employer is a corporation, the president, secretary, treasurer or officers exercising corresponding functions shall each be guilty of a misdemeanor." The statute is not unconstitutional and the evidence sustains the charge of nonpayment by the corporation of moneys which were due certain union pension and welfare funds under a collective bargaining agreement.

2. The purpose of the statute was not to give a favored group of persons the right to enforce criminally a purely civil obligation. It is designed to protect the rights of a group of persons, who have long been recognized to be proper objects of legislative concern, by providing criminal sanctions against those employers who wrongfully withhold benefits which the employees have earned and to which they are entitled.

3. The Legislature had the right to place upon "the president, secretary, treasurer or officers exercising corresponding functions" the responsibility of enforcing compliance with the statute. If the officer is prevented by corporate management from complying with the statute, he may acquiesce and face the possibility of criminal prosecution or he can disobey and perhaps sacrifice his position. The choice may be a difficult one but that can have no bearing on the power of the Legislature to define criminal responsibility.

4. The same standards apply to a prosecution under section 962-a as applied under section 1272 of the former Penal Law. An officer may not be convicted of violating the statute unless he stood in such a relation to the corporate affairs that it may be presumed that he knew of or should have known of the nonpayment and should have taken some steps to prevent the nonpayment.

5. In any event the evidence indicates beyond any reasonable doubt that the defendant, who was apparently the only active officer as well as the principal shareholder of the corporation, was intimately involved in the affairs of the corporation and he knew or should have known of the nonpayment.

 

271 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zuckerman v. Greason


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 430; 231 N.E.2d 718; 285 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1151


September 18, 1967, Argued  
October 31, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: [See 20 N Y 2d 861.] 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorneys sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which suspended the attorneys from the practice of law for professional misconduct.


OVERVIEW: The attorneys argued that their suspensions were unconstitutional because the charges that led to their suspension were based upon information supplied by the attorneys. At the time, the law in New York allowed an attorney to be disbarred for refusing to testify in a disciplinary proceeding. That law was subsequently declared unconstitutional. In essence, the attorneys claimed that they were unconstitutionally coerced to testify against themselves. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court and found that although the attorneys could not be prosecuted criminally for any statements made in their disciplinary proceedings, nor could they have been disbarred had they refused to testify, the attorneys had no privilege against making disclosure usable only in a disciplinary proceeding. The protections afforded by the Fifth Amendment related only to self-incrimination on charges of crime. The attorneys' disciplinary proceedings for professional misconduct were civil in nature. The attorneys had no constitutional privilege to withhold evidence that could not lead to criminal prosecution and bore only upon their right to continue to practice law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court to suspend the attorneys from the practice of law for professional misconduct.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- disciplinary proceedings -- suspended attorneys contend that charges sustained against them were based upon disclosures made by them because assertion of their privilege against self incrimination would, at time they were made, have resulted in their disbarment, and that sustaining of charges on basis of such disclosures was unconstitutional in light of two recent decisions of United States Supreme Court -- while it might be that, under said decisions, disclosures would have to have been excluded from evidence in any subsequent criminal prosecution against them, and that they could not have been disciplined for having invoked privilege against self incrimination instead of making disclosures, neither of these things occurred -- they did make disclosures and it is sufficient protection that they cannot be prosecuted criminally on basis of them -- neither Supreme Court decision cited confers constitutional privilege against use of disclosures in disciplinary proceedings, which are civil in nature -- to determine motivation of attorney who chooses to make disclosures as to his professional conduct would be speculative inquiry, not resolvable as question of fact.

1. In the case of Spevack v. Klein (385 U.S. 511, decided Jan. 16, 1967), the United States Supreme Court expressly overruled an earlier decision made by it in the case of Cohen v. Hurley (366 U.S. 117, decided April 24, 1961) and held that an attorney could not be disbarred for asserting his privilege against self incrimination when being questioned concerning his professional conduct. At the same time it also held, in the case of Garrity v. New Jersey (385 U.S. 493), that the existence of a statute which provided for the removal of a public employee who invoked the Fifth Amendment in official inquiries had coerced certain police officers into failing to invoke said amendment and that, therefore, their statements should not have been admitted into evidence against them in a criminal prosecution. Subsequent to these decisions in the Spevack and Garrity cases, the United States Supreme Court vacated a 1965 order of the Appellate Division which had ordered the disbarment of one attorney and the suspension of the other attorney in the present disciplinary proceedings and remanded the proceedings "in the light of Spevack v. Klein". On reconsideration following the remand, the Appellate Division sustained three charges as to both attorneys. They now contend that these three charges were based, at least in part, upon disclosures which were made by them because assertion of their privilege against self incrimination with respect to the disclosures would have resulted in their disbarment while Cohen v. Hurley remained the law, and that sustaining those charges on the basis of such disclosures was unconstitutional in the light of the Spevack and Garrity cases. However, while it might be that, under the Garrity case, the disclosures made by them in the disciplinary proceedings would have to have been excluded from evidence in any subsequent criminal prosecution, and that, under the Spevack case, they could not have been disciplined for invoking their privilege against self incrimination instead of making the disclosures, neither of these things occurred. Here, appellants did make the disclosures which contributed to the punishment imposed upon them and it is sufficient protection that they cannot be prosecuted criminally on the basis of them. Neither the Garrity nor the Spevack case confers a constitutional privilege against the use of such disclosures in disciplinary proceedings, which are civil in nature.

2. Appellants may have made the disclosures because they thought they would be disbarred if they invoked their privileges, but to determine the motivation of an attorney who chooses to make disclosures as to his professional conduct would be a speculative inquiry, not resolvable as a question of fact.

 

272 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jackson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 440; 231 N.E.2d 722; 285 N.Y.S.2d 8; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1152


May 25, 1967, Argued  
October 31, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which was entered in defendant's second trial on the jury's verdict that convicted defendant of felony murder and sentenced him to death. The second trial resulted from the United States Supreme Court's decision that required the state to either accord defendant a hearing on the voluntariness of a confession introduced at his first trial, a new trial, or a release.


OVERVIEW: The underlying felony committed by defendant was robbery of a hotel. As defendant emerged from the hotel, he encountered a patrolman whom he shot and killed. At the sentencing stage of defendant's trial, the state introduced testimony from a police officer who overheard defendant confess to a series of unrelated robberies and burglaries. The court held that the conviction and sentence were proper and that defendant was not twice put in jeopardy for felony murder because the jury in defendant's first trial was not given a full opportunity to return a verdict on the felony murder offense and the jury's silence as to that offense did not constitute an acquittal. The trial court's failure to properly instruct the jury on the termination of the felony was harmless error. Defendant was not entitled to a "Huntley hearing" prior to the introduction of the confessions at the sentencing stage because he did not object to their admission on the ground that they were involuntary and he made no showing that the confessions were coerced. Defendant's trial before a special, or "blue ribbon," jury did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- first degree -- under indictment for murder in first degree, defendant was properly convicted at first trial of common-law first degree murder and, at second trial, of felony murder; no double jeopardy -- felony (robbery) had not yet terminated when defendant encountered and killed policeman; trial court's alleged error in answer to jury's request for further instructions on this point did not amount to reversible error -- jury properly decreed death penalty, after post-verdict hearing (Penal Law, § 1045-a) in which evidence of defendant's confession was introduced -- defendant did not offer any evidence that that confession was involuntary; defendant was not automatically entitled to preliminary trial by court of voluntariness of such confession -- trial by "blue ribbon" jury did not violate defendant's rights to equal protection of laws.

1. Under an indictment charging defendant with the crime of murder in the first degree, the jury was instructed at defendant's first trial in 1960, without any exception taken, to render a verdict on one but not on both of the theories of first degree murder which the evidence supported -- either a premeditated homicide or a homicide committed while engaged in the commission of a felony (Penal Law, § 1044, subds. 1, 2). The jury returned a verdict of common-law first degree murder, and accordingly defendant was sentenced to death; but, on appeal, a new trial was ordered. This did not unconstitutionally subject him to double jeopardy after an acquittal (U. S. Const., 5th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6). The defendant was not indicted or tried for two crimes, and was not acquitted of a crime by the jury's silence as to one of the theories of proof. Accordingly, on the retrial, in 1965, under the original indictment, evidence was properly received and the jury was properly instructed as to both of said theories of proof, and the jury properly found defendant guilty of murder in the first degree on the theory of felony murder.

2. The evidence proved that defendant robbed a hotel clerk at gunpoint and, emerging from the hotel doorway, encountered a policeman and killed the policeman in an exchange of gunfire. No jury could rationally have found that the defendant had already abandoned or terminated this robbery. Accordingly, the alleged error in the trial court's answer to the jury's request for further instructions as to what constitutes the termination of a felony did not seriously prejudice the defendant's rights (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 427, 542).

3. After being thus properly convicted of first degree murder, the defendant was given a hearing before the trial jury to determine whether he would suffer the penalty of death or life imprisonment (Penal Law, § 1045-a). Subdivision 3 of said statute provides as follows, as to such a hearing: "evidence may be presented by either party on any matter relevant to sentence including, but not limited to, the nature and circumstances of the crime, defendant's background and history, and any aggravating or mitigating circumstances. Any relevant evidence, not legally privileged, shall be received regardless of its admissibility under the exclusionary rules of evidence." The trial court properly rejected defendant's objections that the District Attorney had not notified defendant in advance of his intention to introduce a confession, and that the defendant was automatically entitled as a matter of due process to a preliminary trial by the court alone as to the voluntariness of the confession. The defendant did not allege or come forward with any evidence that the confession was involuntary. In the sentencing stage of a trial (under Penal Law, § 1045-a) the defendant is not automatically entitled to a hearing on voluntariness. The trial court made no finding as to voluntariness and did not submit such question to the jury. The jury decreed the death penalty. The judgment is affirmed.

4. Defendant's trial before a special so-called "blue ribbon" jury (Judiciary Law, § 749-aa) did not violate defendant's constitutional rights to the equal protection of the laws.

 

273 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Port Authority Trans-Hudson Corp. v. Hudson Rapid Tubes Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 457; 231 N.E.2d 734; 285 N.Y.S.2d 24; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1153


April 3, 1967, Argued  
October 31, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: On appeal by claimant Hudson Rapid Tubes Corporation, order of Appellate Division modified, with costs in this court and in the Appellate Division, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed, and matter remanded to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.

On appeal by claimant Hudson and Manhattan Corporation, order of Appellate Division affirmed, without costs.

In view of the disposition herein, cross appeal of the Port Authority Trans-Hudson Corporation dismissed as academic.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner condemnor brought an action seeking to condemn property of respondent condemnee. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) modified, on the law and the facts, and, as modified, affirmed the order condemning the property and awarding the condemnee just compensation and interest. Both parties sought review.


OVERVIEW: The condemned property was an interurban electric railroad system. The legislatures of both the states of New York and New Jersey determined that the continued operation of the facility could only be continued under public ownership. Therefore, pursuant to bi-State legislation, 1962 N.J. Laws ch. 8, the condemnor condemned the property. The issue before the court was what constituted just compensation to the owner and operator of an essential public facility when its property was condemned for continued dedication to the same use to which the owner had dedicated the property. The court noted that while the general rule was that the condemnee be paid the fair market value of the property, there were instances when that rule was unjust. The court found that this was such an instance. The court determined that an award of scrap value solely on the basis of the unprofitability of the enterprise was arbitrary. The court found that the award of interest was intended to compensate the condemnee for the delay in his receipt of the just compensation award and that it was inequitable to penalize the condemnee by denying him interest when he was protecting his rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, modified in part, and remanded in part the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- compensation -- depreciated original cost, rather than present market or scrap value, is proper award for tunnels of railroad, which public corporation will operate -- but award for cars, tracks, repair shops, equipment, etc., remanded to Special Term to consider obsolescence, cost of rehabilitation, and present unprofitability of operation -- going concern valuation, if any, must be based on evidence -- New York statute limiting interest on award for properties in New York to 4% per annum, constitutional -- bi-State legislation requires that New Jersey law apply to this railroad's properties in New Jersey; 6% interest on portion of award made for properties in New Jersey, fair and proper.

1. Normally, fair market value constitutes "just compensation", but not when this would work a manifest injustice to the owner or to the public. Here, a commuter railroad, running mostly through underground tunnels and tunnels under the Hudson River, which tunnels cost some $ 32,000,000 to construct and which would cost over $ 400,000,000 to reproduce today, has been appropriated by a public corporation for the same continued uses by the public. With some relatively minor expenditures, the tunnels will serve for an indefinite period. Special Term properly evaluated the tunnels at their depreciated original cost, $ 30,000,000. The Appellate Division erred in reducing this amount to liquidation value or scrap value.

2. The non-tunnel properties consist of passenger cars, tracks, roadbed, repair shops, signal and electrical and compressed air systems, equipment, etc. Some of the passenger cars were recently purchased; but most of these non-tunnel properties are severely deteriorated and it appears that an expenditure of almost $ 32,000,000 would be needed to rehabilitate them. Although the Appellate Division erred in awarding salvage or scrap value solely on the basis of the unprofitability of the enterprise, Special Term erred in evaluating these properties at $ 20,000,000. This portion of the award is remanded to Special Term, for consideration of the physical and technological obsolescence of these properties, and the expenditures required to rehabilitate these properties, and the present unprofitability of this business enterprise.

3. Special Term erroneously added, to its total award of $ 50,000,000 for the railroad, 10% or $ 5,000,000 for the going concern value. This, too, is remanded to Special Term for further determination. A finding of going concern value must be based on evidence of the cost of organizing and systematizing an enterprise, such as the cost of development of operating schedules and records and procedures and training personnel; and unprofitability must be considered to the extent that it evidences an inefficient organization.

4. The New York statute limiting interest on the amount of the award for the properties located in New York to 4% per annum from the date of the appropriation is valid under both the New York and Federal Constitutions.

5. The bi-State legislation authorizing this appropriation requires that New Jersey law shall apply to the portion of this property located in New Jersey. New Jersey has no statute governing the rate of interest on the award. The parties have stipulated that the properties which are located in New Jersey comprise 65% of the total value. Special Term properly awarded interest at 6% on this 65% portion of the award. This rate is fair and proper under New Jersey law.

Whitmier & Ferris Co. v. State


Claim No. 45318


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 413; 230 N.E.2d 904; 284 N.Y.S.2d 313; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1160


September 27, 1967, Argued  
October 26, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant advertising corporation sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order of the court of claims that granted a motion by the corporation to file a late notice of claim and denied a motion by respondent state to dismiss the claim.


OVERVIEW: The corporation leased various properties adjacent to the thruway for the purpose of constructing and maintaining advertising signs. The signs were more that 500 feet distant from the nearest edge of the pavement, but due to amendments to N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 361-a that changed the distances, the signs were within the forbidden area. The signs were removed by the thruway authority and the corporation filed its notice of intention one day beyond the 90 days limited by N.Y. Ct. Cl. Act § 10(3). The corporation sought permission to extend the time pursuant to N.Y. Ct. Cl. Act § (10)(5). The appellate division denied the corporation's motion to file a late notice of claim and granted the state's motion to dismiss the claim. The court rejected the corporation's argument that § 361-a was an unconstitutional exercise of the police power and constituted a taking of private property without just compensation because it authorized the removal or destruction of the signs. On review the court held that the reason for the statute was to provide safety to the motorists and there was no doubt about the power of the legislature to require the removal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate division's reversal of the order that granted the corporation's late notice of claim.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- highways -- billboards -- Legislature, which had prohibited advertising signs within 500 feet from edge of Thruway pavement ( New York State Thruway Auth. v. Ashley Motor Ct., 10 N Y 2d 151), properly exercised police power in extending distance to 660 feet from pavement (L. 1960, ch. 904) and, subsequently, to 660 feet from edge of right of way (L. 1961, ch. 316).

The Legislature, whose power to prohibit the erection of advertising signs within 500 feet from the edge of the Thruway pavement had been upheld ( New York State Thruway Auth. v. Ashley Motor Ct., 10 N Y 2d 151), properly exercised its police power in extending the distance to 660 feet from the pavement (L. 1960, ch. 904) and, subsequently, to 660 feet from the nearest edge of the right of way of the Thruway (L. 1961, ch. 316). It was within the competence of the Legislature to determine that the safety of the traveling public is endangered by the distraction of the attention of drivers of automobiles under these circumstances.

Landes v. North Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 417; 231 N.E.2d 120; 284 N.Y.S.2d 441; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1161


September 26, 1967, Argued  
October 26, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff candidate sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court of Nassau County (New York), which granted defendant town's motion for summary judgment that dismissed the candidate's action for a judgment that declared N.Y. Town Law §§ 23, 23(a), unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: Sections 23, 23(a) required the holder of an elective town office to be a record owner of real property within the town. The candidate brought an action to have § 23, 23(a) declared unconstitutional and void. The trial court granted the town's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the action based on prior precedent. On appeal, the court that it had to reconsider the prior precedent, reversed and granted the declaratory judgment. The court stated that the ownership of land as a prerequisite to holding an elective town office constituted an invidious discrimination against nonlandowners. The court stated that it was a sort of economic gerrymandering which ran afoul of the equal protection clauses of both state and federal constitutions. The court stated that the proscription against nonlandowners as town councilmen amounted to a dilution or debasement of the vote.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of the town's motion for summary judgment and granted the candidate's request for a declaratory judgment that declared the legislation that required candidates for town office to be a record owners of real property in the town was unconstitutional and void.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- elections -- real property qualification -- requirement of Town Law ( §§ 23, 23-a) that holder of elective town office must be owner of record of real property within town, unconstitutional ( Matter of Becraft v. Strobel, 274 N. Y. 577, overruled).

1. The provisions of sections 23 and 23-a of the Town Law which require a holder of an elective town office to be an owner of record of real property within the town are unconstitutional and void. ( Matter of Becraft v. Strobel, 274 N. Y. 577, overruled.) The statutes violate the equal protection and due process clauses of both the Federal and the State Constitutions. There is no rational connection between qualifications for administering town affairs and ownership of real property.

2. Moreover, to exclude from their choice for a town elective office those who do not own real property unconstitutionally circumscribes the rights of the voters.

3. The statutes also violate section 1 of article I of the State Constitution which provides that "No member of this state shall be disenfranchised, or deprived of any of the rights or privileges secured to any citizen thereof". Wide latitude in choosing public officers is among the rights secured by this constitutional provision.

Gardner v. Broderick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 227; 229 N.E.2d 184; 282 N.Y.S.2d 487; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1269


May 15, 1967, Submitted  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed, on constitutional grounds, an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously confirmed a determination of respondent officials and dismissed petitioner's request for an order reinstating him to his position as patrolman in the Police Department of the City of New York.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, who was under departmental charges, was subpoenaed to appear before a grand jury that was investigating accusations of police bribery and corruption. He was asked to sign a limited waiver of immunity from prosecution for any misconduct in office disclosed by his testimony. After he refused to execute the waiver, he was dismissed from the police force. In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, petitioner sought reinstatement, claiming that he was dismissed solely for refusing to waive his privilege and that the dismissal was unconstitutional. The trial court dismissed the petition, and on appeal the court affirmed. The city charter required petitioner, as a condition of his employment, to waive his constitutional privilege against self-incrimination and to answer questions regarding the performance of his official duties. There was no constitutional defect in the statute as applied. Petitioner stood on his constitutional privilege against self-incrimination when notified that he was to be questioned about malfeasance as a police officer. The information bore upon his fitness to remain in office. His refusal to speak was employee insubordination and he was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that unanimously confirmed a determination of the officials and dismissed the request for an order reinstating petitioner to his position as patrolman.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- police -- waiver of immunity -- police officer who refused to sign waiver of immunity when requested to do so by grand jury investigating charges of bribery and corruption of police officers was properly dismissed.

A grand jury was investigating accusations of bribery and corruption of police officers and petitioner, a former patrolman in the New York City Police Department, was subpoenaed to appear before the grand jury. He was requested to sign a limited waiver of immunity and informed that, under both the New York City Charter and the State Constitution, if he refused to sign the waiver, he would be discharged from his position as a police officer. Having refused to execute the waiver, he was properly dismissed from the police department. Section 1123 of the Charter properly required him, as a condition of his continued employment by the police department, to waive his constitutional privilege against self incrimination and to answer questions regarding the conduct of his office and the performance of his official duties. Petitioner, an employee, had a responsibility to account for his actions. Although people on the public payroll may not be compelled to surrender their constitutional privilege against self incrimination, they have no constitutional right to remain in their positions when they refuse to discuss whether they have faithfully performed their duties. ( Garrity v. New Jersey, 385 U.S. 493, distinguished.)

 

277 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Street


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 231; 229 N.E.2d 187; 282 N.Y.S.2d 491; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1270


May 17, 1967, Argued  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of publicly mutilating the United States flag, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1425(16)(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of publicly mutilating the United States flag, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1425(16)(d). On appeal, the intermediate appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. On further appeal, defendant argued that his protest demonstration was protected by the First and Fourteenth Amendments. The court affirmed defendant's conviction of publicly mutilating the United States flag. The court held that plaintiff state could, under its police powers, proscribe defendant from publicly mutilating the United States flag, because the conduct threatened the peace, security, or well being of the state's inhabitants. The state's prohibition did not violate the First Amendments where other channels of communication were open and available. The Constitution did not require that an exception be made for prohibited activities, which had symbolic significance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of publicly mutilating the United States flag.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- mutilation of flag -- defendant properly convicted of mutilating United States flag in violation of Penal Law ( § 1425, subd. 16, par. d) -- statutory purpose to prevent breach of peace valid -- since defendant could be convicted only once for such conduct, dismissal of charge of disorderly conduct not inconsistent.

1. Defendant was properly convicted for violating a provision of the Penal Law ( § 1425, subd. 16, par. d) which makes it a misdemeanor to "publicly mutilate" the United States flag. While nonverbal expressions may be a form of speech within the meaning and protection of the First and Fourteenth Amendments, the State may, under its police powers, proscribe forms of conduct which threaten the peace, security or well-being of its inhabitants. The statutory purpose is to prevent a breach of the peace -- to prevent the outbreak of violence by discouraging contemptuous and insulting treatment of the flag in public. The State may legitimately curb such activities in the interest of preventing violence and maintaining public order.

2. Since the act in question was specifically prohibited by a statute ( Penal Law, § 1425, subd. 6) and the defendant could be punished only once for having engaged in such conduct ( Penal Law, § 1938), the court did not render an inconsistent verdict when it dismissed the more general charge of disorderly conduct.

Iannucci v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 244; 229 N.E.2d 195; 282 N.Y.S.2d 502; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1272


May 24, 1967, Argued  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order modified in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant counties challenged two orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed rulings in favor of respondents, residents, taxpayers, and property owners, and invalidated as unconstitutional two plans of permanent apportionment through weighted voting by the counties' boards of supervisors (boards) and ordered the counties to provide new plans.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. County Law § 150 provided that the supervisors of each county's municipalities were each county's board; under the plans, the supervisors of larger municipalities would be able to cast more votes up to a maximum, based upon population. On review, the court found that equal representation on the boards of municipalities of varying populations did not satisfy the "one person, one vote" principle, that § 150 as applied to the counties violated constitutional requirements, and that there was nothing unconstitutional in a disparity of influence among the members of the boards. The court found that the proposed modified weighted voting schemes were not supported by sufficient mathematical evidence and computer analyses giving proof that the plans were not defective, and that the court below had correctly determined that the plans were invalid. The court modified the orders to provide that the boards adopt and submit constitutionally valid plans of reapportionment within 60 days and affirmed the orders as modified.


OUTCOME: The court modified the orders and ordered the boards to submit plans of reapportionment within 60 days. The court then affirmed the orders.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- apportionment of Boards of Supervisors -- "adjusted weighted" and "fractional weighted" voting plans to correct malapportionment of Boards of Supervisors of Washington and Saratoga Counties are unconstitutional -- sections 150 and 153 (subd. 4) of County Law are unconstitutional -- Boards of Supervisors directed to reapportion themselves.

1. "Adjusted weighted voting" and "fractional weighted voting" plans to correct malapportionments of the Boards of Supervisors of Washington and Saratoga Counties are constitutionally invalid. Such plans violate the principle of one man-one vote where the power of a representative to affect the passage of legislation by his vote does not roughly correspond to the proportion of the population in his constituency. A legislator's voting power, measured by the mathematical possibility of his casting a decisive vote, must approximate the power he would have in a legislative body which did not employ weighted voting. Since it is not readily apparent on its face whether either of the plans meets the constitutional standards, the plans are of doubtful validity, and to establish the facts one way or another would, in all likelihood, be most expensive. It was incumbent on the boards to come forward with the requisite proof that the plans are not defective. Since this was not done, the courts below correctly determined that the plans are invalid. The only issue involved is the factual one pertaining to the mathematics of the plans. The boards adopted them without obtaining the expensive mathematical analyses that would establish the facts. Under these circumstances, the boards are not entitled to rely on a presumption that their legislative acts are constitutional.

2. Section 150 of the County Law provides that the supervisors of the several cities and towns in each county "shall constitute the board of supervisors of the county" and subdivision 4 of section 153 provides that the board shall conduct its business "by the affirmative vote of a majority of [its] total membership". Such sections, which make no provision for population differences, are unconstitutional as applied to Washington and Saratoga Counties.

3. In the absence of a valid statute establishing the organization and composition of the Boards of Supervisors of those counties, the boards should be directed to reapportion themselves in accordance with the powers granted to them by the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 10, subd. 1, par. [ii], cl. a, subcl. [1]).
