Fenster v. Leary


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 309; 229 N.E.2d 426; 282 N.Y.S.2d 739; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1279; 25 A.L.R.3d 784


May 29, 1967, Argued  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individual appealed the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied his motion for summary judgment in his declaratory action seeking to strike as unconstitutional New York's vagrancy statute, N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §887(1). The individual had been arrested and acquitted under the statute and had applied for, and been denied, a prohibition barring the court from hearing further vagrancy cases against him.


OVERVIEW: The individual applied to the federal court for a declaration of the unconstitutionality of § 887(1), but was denied because he had a state remedy. The trial court denied his summary judgment motion in his declaratory action. On appeal, the court reversed. The court found that § 887(1) was unconstitutional because it violated due process and constituted an overreaching of the state's police power. The court noted that the statute was a "status crime" in that it looked at a person's condition or being rather than a person's actions. Status crime statutes cannot stand if they make criminal a condition over which a person has no control or if the conditions are vaguely defined, but since the vagrancy statute was not attacked as void for vagueness, the court did not reach the question of its vagueness. The court found that the statute was not a valid exercise of police power since its only purpose was to arrest and prosecute for vagrancy persons whose only crime, in any, was against themselves.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of the individual's motion for summary judgment in his declaratory action to have the vagrancy statute declared unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- vagrancy -- section 887 (subd. 1) of Code of Criminal Procedure which makes criminal "a person who, not having visible means to maintain himself, lives without employment" is unconstitutional -- it violates due process and overreaches proper limitations of police power -- declaratory judgment action proper -- appeal properly taken direct to Court of Appeals from Supreme Court.

1. On three occasions, each about a month apart, plaintiff was arrested and charged with violation of subdivision 1 of section 887 of the Code of Criminal Procedure with being "a person who, not having visible means to maintain himself, lives without employment." Following each arrest he was acquitted of the violation charged. In this action for a declaratory judgment he is entitled to judgment declaring the statute unconstitutional. 

2. The statute is unconstitutional in that it violates due process and constitutes an overreaching of the proper limitations of the police power in that it unreasonably makes criminal and provides punishment for conduct of an individual which in no way impinges on the rights or interests of others and which has in no way been demonstrated to have anything more than the most tenuous connection with prevention of crime and preservation of the public order.

3. Plaintiff's appeal is properly taken to the Court of Appeals directly from the Supreme Court since the sole question raised and decided below and the sole issue on the appeal is the constitutionality of the statute. Declaratory relief of the kind here sought is available under the circumstances.

 

280 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taggart


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 335; 229 N.E.2d 581; 283 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1282


April 19, 1967, Submitted  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a determination adjudging defendant to be a youthful offender on his plea of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for possession of a pistol by a detective who had been alerted by an anonymous telephone call. A motion to suppress the evidence was denied after a hearing. Defendant pleaded guilty and was adjudged a youthful offender. The appellate court affirmed the conviction. On further appeal, the court affirmed. The search was valid under the "Stop and Frisk" amendment to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 180-a. The detective had at least a reasonably based suspicion that defendant was committing a crime and was thus entitled to "stop" him. The detective was also warranted in suspecting that defendant possessed a dangerous weapon that threatened the life of the detective as well as others in the immediate vicinity. The detective had a reasonably based suspicion not only that defendant was carrying a pistol but also that the weapon was located in his left-hand jacket pocket. It would be unreasonable to require an officer in that situation to engage in a preparatory and undoubtedly dangerous frisk, particularly in view of the fact that defendant was standing in the middle of a group of children at the time of the search.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that affirmed a determination adjudging defendant to be a youthful offender on his plea of guilty.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- reasonable search and seizure -- anonymous person telephoned police that youth on street corner had loaded revolver in pocket of his jacket -- detective found youth who matched telephoned description there, in midst of children -- in such exigency, preliminary questioning or frisking would endanger detective or children -- detective properly seized youth by arm and took revolver out of pocket (Code Crim. Pro., § 180-a) -- motion to suppress such seized evidence was properly denied and youth was properly adjudged to be youthful offender.

An anonymous person telephoned the police and said that, on a certain street corner, a blond, blue-eyed male about 18 years old, who was wearing white chino-type trousers, had a loaded .32-calibre revolver in his left-hand jacket pocket. The caller remained unidentified and hence the police could not ascertain his reliability. A detective was dispatched to the street corner. There he saw a youth who matched the telephoned description. The youth was standing in the midst of a group of children. This was an exigency which required immediate police action. The safety of the officer and the children would not allow any preliminary questioning or frisking. The detective seized the youth by the arm and placed him against the wall of a building and took the revolver out of his left-hand jacket pocket, which was inside the jacket lining and did not noticeably bulge. This was a case where, as set forth in the Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 180-a), a police officer "reasonably suspects" that a person in a public place "is committing" a crime and that he, the police officer, "is in danger of life or limb", in which case, "he may search such person for a dangerous weapon" and, if he "finds such a weapon * * * he may take * * * it" and "arrest such person." Subsequently, after a hearing, a motion by the arrested youth to suppress such seized evidence was properly denied, and thereafter he was adjudged a youthful offender on his plea of guilty and was committed to the Elmira Reception Center; and such judgment of the New York City Criminal Court was properly affirmed by the Appellate Term.

 

281 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Grossman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 346; 229 N.E.2d 589; 283 N.Y.S.2d 12; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1283


May 31, 1967, Argued  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of Appellate Division reversed and that of the Supreme Court, Kings County, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reinstated an indictment against them following the reversal of a trial court order granting their motions to suppress.


OVERVIEW: Defendants had been indicted for conspiracy to commit murder and for possession of revolvers. Defendants' conversations had been overheard and recorded by electronic devices. The conversations revealed a plot to murder witnesses against defendants in a larceny investigation including the obtaining of suitable weapons. The electronic devices had been installed as a part of the larceny investigation after obtaining a court order pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 813-a. Also involved were two pistols obtained as incident to arrests made on the basis of information obtained under the eavesdropping order. The trial court had not only granted the motions to suppress but as a further consequence of the suppression dismissed the indictment. The State sought review and the appellate division reversed. On appeal, the court found that the Supreme Court of the United States held the § 813-a was unconstitutional. The court held that consequently, it was constrained to hold that the evidence obtained in the case, as well as the "fruits" of such evidence, was illegally obtained and must be suppressed, despite the fact that the police officers acted in good faith.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, the motion to suppress was granted, and the indictment was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- eavesdropping -- evidence consisting of recorded conversations obtained pursuant to order (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-a) and pistols obtained as incident to arrests made thereafter were illegally obtained and must be suppressed -- indictment dismissed -- rule excluding evidence illegally obtained, developed to deter deliberate violations of constitutional rights, applicable herein although police acted in good faith.

1. Evidence consisting of recorded conversations obtained through the use of electronic devices authorized by an order issued pursuant to section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure and two pistols obtained as incident to arrests made on the basis of information obtained under the eavesdropping order were illegally obtained ( Berger v. New York, 388 U.S. 41) and they must be suppressed.

2. The rule excluding evidence illegally obtained is a rule developed by the courts to deter police officials from deliberately violating constitutional rights. Here, however, the police were acting in good faith under statutes they believed to be valid and the policy of deterrence is not applicable. Nevertheless, the rule of exclusion has been applied without recognition of this difference and the indictment must be dismissed.

Willets v. Murray

283 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Victor Fischel & Co. v. R. H. Macy & Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 180; 229 N.E.2d 26; 282 N.Y.S.2d 234; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1264; 1967 Trade Cas. (CCH) P72,154


May 31, 1967, Reargued  
July 7, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Upon reargument: Order of Appellate Division reversed and matter remitted to the Supreme Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein, with costs to abide the event.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff liquor distributor and defendant retailers filed cross appeals from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which modified a judgment for plaintiff by striking the award of damages, but otherwise affirmed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was the exclusive distributor of two brands of liquor. Defendant was a retailer. The trial court entered an injunction, pursuant to the Feld-Crawford Act, N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 369-a et seq., prohibiting the retailing by defendants of those branded liquors at less than the prices fixed by the distiller or plaintiff as its distributor. The lower appellate court had affirmed the judgment but modified it to eliminate the award of damages, and both parties appealed. The court reversed and remanded the case for further proceedings in accordance with its opinion. The issue presented was whether new liquor legislation, 1964 N.Y. Laws 531, prohibited agreements fixing retail prices. The court held that such agreements were possible, but that the proponent of such an agreement would have the burden of establishing that consumers would benefit from a reduction in wholesale prices. The court noted also that the law was not enacted to enrich wholesalers or retailers and that any party seeking an injunction would be required to show that they had equitable standing to obtain affirmative equitable relief.


OUTCOME: The order appealed from was reversed and the injunction was vacated, and the matter was remitted to the trial court for further proceedings in accordance with the court's opinion.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- prices of branded liquors -- chapter 531 of Laws of 1964, as amended, which amended Alcoholic Beverage Control Law so as to bring about lower prices to consumers may not be frustrated by Feld-Crawford Act (General Business Law, § 369-a et seq.) injunction -- prices may be fixed by Feld-Crawford agreement which approximately reflect reduction in retail prices which would result in normal course of business from mandated reductions in sales to wholesalers and to retailers.

Chapter 531 of the Laws of 1964, as amended by chapter 798 of the Laws of 1967, which effected amendments to the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law so as to forestall (§ 8) "monopolistic and anti-competitive practices designed to" eliminate "discrimination and disadvantage" to consumers by requiring that the bottle and case prices of branded liquors to wholesalers and by wholesalers to retailers shall be no higher than the lowest price at which such items of liquor were sold to any wholesaler or retailer in any other State of the United States or in the District of Columbia, was designed to benefit consumers and may not be frustrated by a Feld-Crawford Act (General Business Law, § 369-a et seq.) injunction designed to maintain fixed prices of branded commodities. The prices to be fixed by liquor injunctions against the retailer cannot be set at higher figures than those which would result in the normal course of business from the application of the price reduction provisions of chapters 531 and 798. The burden of showing the extent to which reductions in consumer prices would reflect the reductions in prices to wholesalers and retailers rests upon plaintiff in an action for a Feld-Crawford injunction. The matter is remitted to Special Term. If the prices sought to be enforced by this injunction are the same as they were under the now repealed mandatory price-fixing by the State Liquor Authority, an injunction may not issue and the complaint should be dismissed. Upon the other hand, if the prices fixed by this Feld-Crawford agreement do, approximately, reflect the reduction in retail prices which would result in the normal course of business from the statutorily mandated reductions in the case of sales to wholesalers and to retailers, then an injunction may issue.

 

284 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Barash v. Association of Bar


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 154; 228 N.E.2d 896; 281 N.Y.S.2d 997; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1353


May 25, 1967, Argued  
June 15, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of Appellate Division reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to that court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney was convicted of felony charges. After the attorney's conviction was reversed, respondent bar association began proceedings to strike his name from the roll of attorneys, after which the attorney filed a motion for reinstatement to the bar. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) denied his motion for reinstatement. The attorney appealed.


OVERVIEW: After his conviction in a federal court, the attorney was automatically disbarred under N.Y. Jud. Law § 90. Before his name was stricken from the rolls, the federal court reversed the attorney's conviction and ordered a new trial. The court stated that in Matter of Ginsberg, 1 N.Y.2d 144, 134 N.E.2d 193 (1956) it had been held that when a lawyer asked for reinstatement, the appellate division could consider all of the circumstances of the alleged criminal conduct to see if there was convincing proof of innocence. The court held that this rule was unjust when applied to reversals of convictions. It stated that because a reversal nullified the conviction, a lawyer who had been disbarred for a felony conviction and sought reinstatement was entitled either to prompt reinstatement or to the institution of proceedings to preclude him from reinstatement on the basis of charges and proof sufficient to debar him. It overruled the Ginsberg case to the extent that it conflicted with this holding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's order denying the motion for reinstatement. It remanded the case for such proceedings as might be appropriate and consistent with the court's opinion.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- disciplinary proceedings -- felony -- reversal -- when felony conviction for which lawyer's name was stricken from roll of attorneys is reversed, his application for reinstatement should be granted by Appellate Division, unless disciplinary proceedings on specific charges of misconduct are promptly instituted, or unless circumstances show danger to well-being of clients or public.

When a lawyer is convicted of a felony, he is ipso facto disbarred. Such fact may be recorded, as in this case, by an Appellate Division order striking his name from the roll of attorneys (Judiciary Law, § 90, subd. 4). But, if the judgment of conviction is reversed -- as in this instance, where the United States Court of Appeals reversed the judgment of the United States District Court for errors in the trial and ordered a new trial -- the judgment of conviction no longer exists. In such situation, the petitioning Bar Association or the Appellate Division may promptly institute proceedings to discipline the lawyer for the misconduct involved in the criminal prosecution or for any other matters meriting discipline, before he is entitled to resume the practice of law. Any proceedings thus brought on charges will be subject to the usual rules as to specificity of charges and burden of proof. If for any reason it is not desired to proceed in this fashion, especially if out of a sense of fairness or otherwise the Bar Association or the Appellate Division prefers to have the criminal prosecution follow its normal course before the institution of disciplinary proceedings, then the lawyer should be promptly reinstated if he applies for reinstatement. He does not have the burden of proving his innocence. ( Matter of Ginsberg, 1 N Y 2d 144, limited.) However, if the judgment of conviction arose out of a pattern of misconduct which directly affects the well-being of clients or public, or if there are other circumstances requiring the protection of clients or public, the Appellate Division has discretion under the statute (Judiciary Law, § 90, subd. 5) to take appropriate preventive steps.

 

285 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Moseley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 64; 228 N.E.2d 765; 281 N.Y.S.2d 762; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1381


April 17, 1967, Argued  
June 1, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction reversed to the extent that the sentence of death is set aside and the case remanded to the Supreme Court, Queens County, for imposition of a sentence of life imprisonment, pursuant to subdivision 6 of section 1045-a of the Penal Law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court in Queens County (New York), which convicted him of murder in the first degree and, in a separate jury proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 1045-a, sentenced him to death. Defendant challenged the trial court's refusal to allow mitigating evidence at the sentencing hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed to killing, robbing, and attempting to rape a woman. He was charged with murder in the first degree, and the only defense was insanity. The jury found him sane, convicted him, and recommended the death penalty, which was imposed. Defendant appealed, claiming that the trial court erred in refusing to admit mitigating evidence at the sentencing hearing. The court held that: (1) admission of testimony that defendant was incapable of self-control was admissible at the sentencing hearing and was not a relitigation of the sanity question because he was entitled to persuade the jury that, while legally sane, his mental condition should have been considered in mitigation; (2) the issue at the sentencing hearing was not defendant's guilt but whether he deserved mercy; and (3) under the circumstances, N.Y. Penal Law. § 1045-a mandated a sentence of life imprisonment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's judgment of conviction was reversed to the extent that the sentence of death was set aside and the case was remanded for imposition of a sentence of life imprisonment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- in trial at which defendant was found guilty of murder in first degree and death penalty was imposed, jury was properly charged on defense of insanity -- error, however, to hold that evidence as to defendant's mental condition was not admissible at sentencing hearing (Penal Law, § 1045-a) -- upon reargument defendant must be sentenced to life imprisonment (Penal Law, § 1045-a, subd. 6).

1. Defendant was found guilty of murder in the first degree and the death penalty was imposed upon a recommendation of the jury in a separate sentencing proceeding pursuant to section 1045-a of the Penal Law. His only defense at the trial was insanity. With respect to that issue, the jury was properly charged and the People proved beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant knew it was wrong to kill decedent.

2. The trial court, however, committed substantial error in holding that evidence as to defendant's mental condition was not admissible at the sentencing hearing. Defendant was not attempting to relitigate the issue of his legal insanity or his criminal responsibility. He was endeavoring to persuade the jury that, while "legally sane", his mental illness may have rendered it impossible for him to exercise any self-control, and should, therefore, be considered in mitigation.

3. Subdivision 6 of section 1045-a of the Penal Law provides: "On an appeal by the defendant where the judgment is of death, the court of appeals, if it finds substantial error only in the sentencing proceeding, may set aside the sentence of death and remand the case to the trial court, in which event the trial court shall impose the sentence of life imprisonment." Upon remand to the trial court, there must be an automatic sentence of life imprisonment for defendant.

 

286 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 109; 228 N.E.2d 791; 281 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1388


March 2, 1967, Argued  
June 1, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff boards of education appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a judgment that declared N.Y. Educ. Law § 701(2) unconstitutional and enjoined defendant Commissioner of Education from apportioning money for the purchase of textbooks for pupils attending religious schools.


OVERVIEW: The boards of education sought a judgment that declared that N.Y. Educ. Law § 701(2) violated both N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 3 and U.S. Const. amends. I, IV. The Commissioner of Education alleged that the complaint failed to state a cause of action and that N.Y. Educ. Law § 701(2) was constitutional. The lower court indicated that the boards of education had no standing to challenge the constitutionality of N.Y. Educ. Law § 701(2). The court reviewed the merits of the case. The court found that in providing textbooks to students of both public and private schools, there was no intention to assist parochial schools. The court determined that any benefit accruing to parochial schools in receiving textbooks for its students was a collateral effect of N.Y. Educ. Law § 701(2), so it could not be classified as the giving of aid to religious schools. The court concluded, therefore, that the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment was not violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- loan of textbooks -- section 701 (subd. 2, renumbered subd. 3) of Education Law, which permits school authorities to purchase textbooks and loan them free to children in public or private schools, is not in conflict with article XI (§ 3) of New York State Constitution which provides that State shall not use its property or credit or any public money, directly or indirectly, in aid of any school wholly or in part under control or direction of any religious denomination.

1. Subdivision 2 (renum. subd. 3 by L. 1966, ch. 795) of section 701 of the Education Law permits the appropriate school authorities to purchase textbooks and to loan them free, upon individual request, to all children who are enrolled in grades seven to twelve of a public or private school which complies with the compulsory Education Law. Section 3 of article XI of the New York State Constitution provides that "Neither the state nor any subdivision thereof shall use its property or credit or any public money, or authorize or permit either to be used, directly or indirectly, in aid or maintenance, other than for examination or inspection, of any school or institution of learning wholly or in part under the control or direction of any religious denomination, or in which any denominational tenet or doctrine is taught". Since there is no intention to assist parochial schools as such, any benefit accruing to those schools is a collateral effect of the statute, and, therefore, cannot be properly classified as the giving of aid directly or indirectly. The statute is not in conflict with the constitutional provision. ( Judd v. Board of Educ., 278 N. Y. 200, not followed.)

2. Since the statute entails no aid to the parochial schools, there is no Federal constitutional question under the First Amendment.

3. Plaintiffs, Boards of Education, have capacity to sue.

Bell v. Waterfront Com. of New York Harbor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 54; 228 N.E.2d 758; 281 N.Y.S.2d 753; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1430; 65 L.R.R.M. 2746


April 11, 1967, Argued  
May 31, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant longshoreman challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which, in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, unanimously confirmed a determination of respondent Waterfront Commission of New York Harbor (commission) denying the longshoreman's application for registration as a checker and revoking his registration as a longshoreman.


OVERVIEW: The longshoreman was called before a commission lawyer to answer questions relating to his application for registration as a checker and, under oath, denied or claimed he could not recall having belonged to a subversive organization when he was a teenager. Thereafter, at a hearing ordered by the commission, testimony was given which indicated that not only had the longshoreman been a member of the organizations, but also that he had been active in the organizations' affairs. The commission found him guilty of fraud, deceit, and misrepresentation, revoked his registration as a longshoreman, and denied his application for registration as a checker. The commission's decision was affirmed on review. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the discipline imposed was too severe. It found that had the longshoreman told the truth, nothing untoward would have resulted since the activities in question occurred many years ago. Further, during the time he was registered, the longshoreman did not engage in unlawful or subversive activities or do anything else, apart from his deception in the interview, which would justify revoking his longshoreman's registration.


OUTCOME: The court found that the discipline imposed on the longshoreman by the commission was too severe, that a period of suspension would have been more appropriate and, since his registration was terminated nearly three years ago, any suspension beyond the date of its decision would constitute an abuse of discretion. Accordingly, it reversed the order of the appellate division and remanded the matter to the commission for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- membership in subversive organization -- Waterfront Commission revoked registration of petitioner as longshoreman upon finding that his testimony regarding membership in subversive organization was incredible and his denials and lapses were products of "fraud, deceit and misrepresentation" (Waterfront Commission Act, § 5-h, subd. 3, as added by L. 1954, ch. 220, § 2) -- matter remitted with direction that suspension beyond date of Court of Appeals decision would be abuse of discretion -- case is such that court will decide constitutional issues although, in view of petitioner's fraud and deception, he could be denied opportunity to challenge constitutionality of statute (L. 1953, ch. 882, pt. I, art. VIII, § 3, subd. [b]).

1. The Waterfront Commission revoked the longshoreman registration of petitioner, who was 33 years old and had been a longshoreman since he was 19. Upon his application to the Commission for registration as a checker, he was called before one of the Commission's lawyers for a series of interviews and, although notified that he had the right to be represented by counsel, he chose to appear at the interviews alone. He was questioned under oath about alleged subversive activities when he was 15 or 16 years old and he denied, or claimed he could not recall, having belonged to, held office in, known officers of, or participated in the activities of the Young Progressives of America or the American Youth for Democracy, the latter an organization cited by the Attorney General of the United States in 1947 as subversive. The Commission concluded that his testimony was incredible and that his denials and lapses of memory were the products of "fraud, deceit and misrepresentation" (Waterfront Commission Act, § 5-h, subd. 3, as added by L. 1954, ch. 220, § 2). The matter is remitted with a direction that any suspension beyond the date of this decision would constitute an abuse of discretion.

2. In view of the petitioner's fraud and deception, he could be denied the opportunity of challenging the constitutionality of the statute (L. 1953, ch. 882, pt. I, art. VIII, § 3, subd. [b]) which authorizes the Commission to discipline longshoremen for subversive activities. However, the court will decide questions which need not be reached when a case raises important constitutional issues and the controversy is of a character which is likely to recur. The present is such a case.

 

289 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rosenfeld v. Hotel Corp. of America


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


20 N.Y.2d 25; 228 N.E.2d 374; 281 N.Y.S.2d 308; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1450


April 5, 1967, Argued  
May 25, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, out-of-state executors of the estate of a corporate director, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York), which affirmed an order granting respondent stockholders' motion to substitute the executors as defendants in the stockholders' derivative action. The appellate court certified the question of whether the order of substitution was properly entered.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was a corporate director of a company sued by the stockholders in a derivative action. The executors contested their substitution in the lawsuit asserting that New York courts could not obtain valid in personam jurisdiction over them. The decedent had voluntarily submitted to the jurisdiction of the trial court. The court's decision was based solely on the issue of the constitutionality of out-of-state service upon nonresident executors of a tortfeasor's estate. The court held that NY. C.P.L.R. 302 and N.Y. C.P.L.R. 313 were constitutionally valid. Although prior decisions in New York had held that jurisdiction could not be obtained over foreign executors, the trend in modern caselaw decisions was to expand the concept of jurisdiction, particularly in light of the pronouncements by the United States Supreme Court on in personam jurisdiction. Those pronouncements held that service of process on out-of-state parties did not violate due process so long as sufficient minimum contacts existed with those parties so that the maintenance of a lawsuit did not offend traditional notions of fair play and substantial justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting the motion to substitute the executors and answered the certified question in the affirmative.


HEADNOTES

Process -- nonresident executors -- nonresident defendant was served in Massachusetts under CPLR 302 (subd. [a], par. 1) and appeared voluntarily; subsequently he died and executors were appointed in Massachusetts; executors were ordered substituted for defendant pursuant to CPLR 1015 (subd. [a]) and 1012 -- service upon nonresident executors of deceased nonresident defendant is valid -- application for substitution made within two years after decedent's death was within reasonable time; granting of order was not abuse of discretion by Special Term as matter of law; court powerless to review exercise of discretion upon appeal of certified question.

1. One of 19 individual defendants in a stockholders' derivative action was personally served a summons and complaint in Massachusetts pursuant to CPLR 302 (subd. [a], par. 1) based upon his transaction of business in New York. After voluntarily appearing in the action by filing an answer, defendant died and executors were appointed in Massachusetts. Later, upon motion by the other defendants, a stay of all proceedings was granted until plaintiffs posted the required security. After the stay was vacated, plaintiffs moved to substitute the nonresident executors for the deceased nonresident defendant pursuant to CPLR 1015 (subd. [a]) and substitution was ordered. The executors contend that the New York courts cannot constitutionally obtain in personam jurisdiction over nonresident executors who have not committed acts or transacted any business within this State. Out-of-State service upon the nonresident executors is permitted by CPLR 302 and 313, and such out-of-State service is not invalid or unconstitutional.

2. The Court of Appeals is without power to review the exercise of discretion in ordering the substitution and upon an appeal based on a certified question.

3. In any event the order of substitution pursuant to CPLR 1015 (subd. [a]) and 1021 was effected within the discretion of the court and there is insufficient reason to find that it abused its discretion as a matter of law.

4. At this stage of the proceedings, it is no valid objection to declare that any judgment rendered in this action would be of no force or effect against decedent's estate in Massachusetts under laws of that State. Such conjecture is speculative and prematurely made.

Atlantic Beach v. Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

291 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Farrell v. Drew


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 486; 227 N.E.2d 824; 281 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1513


February 23, 1967, Argued  
May 16, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord appealed orders of the Civil Court of the City of New York, Bronx County (New York), which dismissed her eviction proceedings against respondent tenants after finding that N.Y. Social Welfare Law § 143-b was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The landlord filed eviction proceedings against tenants, who were welfare recipients. The trial court dismissed the proceedings after finding that a hazardous condition existed in the building. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that N.Y. Social Welfare Law § 143-b, which provided for rent abatement for welfare tenants who lived in buildings that contained a violation of law which was dangerous, hazardous or detrimental to life or health, was constitutional. The statute did not deny equal protection to landlords of welfare recipients because there was a reasonable basis for differentiating among landlords of welfare recipients and other landlords. The state could, in the exercise of its police power, provide for the curtailment of rent payments to landlords as a means of inducing them to eliminate dangerous housing conditions. Section 143-b provided landlords notice and an opportunity for a hearing. Section 143-b did not unconstitutionally impair the landlord's contractual rights because it was reasonably aimed at correcting the evil of substandard housing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the trial court dismissing the landlord's eviction proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- abatement of rent of welfare tenants for failure to correct violations in buildings -- section 143-b of Social Welfare Law provides for abatement of rent for welfare tenants who live in buildings containing violations of law which are dangerous; in summary proceedings for nonpayment of rent against welfare tenants, trial court dismissed petitions and held section constitutional; judgments affirmed -- no unlawful discrimination in statute against landlords of welfare tenants and no denial of equal protection of laws -- no denial of due process of law by total abatement of rent -- statute provides for notice and hearing to landlord -- section does not unconstitutionally impair landlord's contractual rights.

1. Section 143-b of the Social Welfare Law provides an abatement of rent for welfare tenants who live in buildings that contain a "violation of law * * * which is dangerous, hazardous or detrimental to life or health". In these summary proceedings for nonpayment of rent against three welfare recipients whose rent had been paid by the Welfare Department, the Welfare Department's defense that a violation existed in the apartment of a fourth tenant who was not a welfare recipient and that there thus existed a dangerous condition which rendered the building unsafe for all tenants was upheld by the trial court who held the section constitutional and dismissed the proceedings. The orders are affirmed.

2. Although the landlord does not have standing to complain that the statute discriminates in favor of tenants who are welfare recipients, she is entitled to urge that it denies equal protection of the laws by providing a sanction only against landlords of welfare recipients. Such legislation is not unconstitutional as long as a reasonable basis exists for differentiating among the members of the same class. Here it is the landlords of welfare recipients who, the Legislature found, "conspicuously offend". While they are not the only landlords who fail to make repairs in slum dwellings, welfare recipients have even less freedom than other tenants of deteriorated buildings in selecting a place to live. And landlords of welfare recipients, secure in their receipt of rents directly from public funds, have even less incentive than other landlords to make repairs. Under circumstances such as these, if the Legislature chooses to impose a special sanction against those landlords, the equal protection clause does not forbid it.

3. The State may, in the exercise of its police power, provide for the curtailment of rent payments to landlords as a means of inducing them to eliminate dangerous housing conditions. The total abatement of rent provided by the statute does not deprive landlord of her property without due process of law.

4. Section 143-b is not unconstitutional because it does not, in so many words, call for notice and hearing to landlord. Both are implicit in the statutory scheme. By providing that a violation of the building laws is "a valid defense" -- to be pleaded as such -- in a summary eviction proceeding (subd. 5, par. [a]), the section affords landlord ample notice of the violation and full opportunity to refute the claim and litigate the issue. Landlord here received a full hearing.

5. Section 143-b does not unconstitutionally impair landlord's contractual rights. This remedial legislation is reasonably aimed at correcting the evil of substandard housing and may not be stricken as unconstitutional even though the means devised to accomplish that result may, to some extent, impair the obligation of landlord's contract.

 

292 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Epton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 496; 227 N.E.2d 829; 281 N.Y.S.2d 9; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1514


February 27, 1967, Argued  
May 16, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) order affirming his convictions for conspiracy to riot, conspiracy to commit the crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy, and advocacy of criminal anarchy, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 508, 2090, 160, 161. He alleged that §§ 160, 161 were unconstitutional, that his punishment was directed solely at speech, and merger of conspiracy to riot with riot.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of conspiracy to riot, conspiracy to commit advocacy of criminal anarchy, and advocacy of criminal anarchy under N.Y. Penal Law §§ 508, 2090, 160, 161. The appellate court affirmed his convictions and he appealed, alleging that §§ 160, 161 unconstitutionally restrained free speech, that the conviction for conspiracy to riot amounted to punishment for free speech, and that the conspiracy to riot charge merged with the dismissed charge of riot. The court affirmed. By an interim decision, the criminal anarchy statute, §§ 160, 161, had been found unconstitutional as interpreted. The court reinterpreted the statute in light of modern day constitutional standards and ruled that defendant's words and actions created a "clear and present danger" that the riots then rocking Harlem would be intensified. It ruled that although speech may have been the means by which defendant sought to incite a riot to further the ends of conspiracy, speech was not in and of itself the object of a prosecution for conspiracy to riot and sufficient evidence supported the conviction for conspiracy. Conspiracy to incite riot was an independently indictable offense from the crime of riot.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate judgment affirming defendant's conviction for the crimes of conspiracy to riot, conspiracy to commit the crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy, and advocacy of criminal anarchy.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- conspiracy to commit crime of criminal anarchy -- freedom of speech -- sections 160 and 161 of Penal Law dealing with conspiracy to commit crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy and advocacy of criminal anarchy were held constitutional in People v. Gitlow (234 N. Y. 132); decision affirmed by Supreme Court of United States (268 U.S. 652) over dissent insisting on showing clear danger before freedom of speech might be abridged; dissent now represents law; Court of Appeals re-examines constitutionality of sections in light thereof -- advocacy by speech of overthrow of Government by force must be accompanied by intent to accomplish overthrow and by clear danger that overthrow may be attempted or accomplished; so construed sections are constitutional -- reinterpretation of statute in accordance with modern constitutional standards does not violate defendant's rights to procedural due process -- State can prosecute seditious activity directed against State or local Government -- speech itself is not object of prosecution for conspiracy to incite riot -- crime of conspiracy to incite riot does not merge in crime of riot -- judgment convicting defendant of crimes of conspiracy to riot, conspiracy to commit crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy and advocacy of criminal anarchy affirmed.

1. Defendant was convicted on three counts, two of the counts charging him with conspiracy to commit the crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy (Penal Law, §§ 160, 161, 580) and advocacy of criminal anarchy (Penal Law, §§ 160, 161). In People v. Gitlow (234 N. Y. 132), decided in 1922, sections 160 and 161 were held constitutional. The United States Supreme Court affirmed (268 U.S. 652) over a dissent which insisted upon a showing of "clear and present danger" before one's freedom of speech, guaranteed by the First Amendment, might be abridged. The United States Supreme Court has since held that the dissent represents the law today ( Dennis v. United States, 341 U.S. 494). As interpreted in People v. Gitlow, the sections are unconstitutional. Such decision does not, however, reflect the Legislature's intent to make unlawful the mere advocacy of violent revolution, and the court is not bound thereby but the sections must be re-examined and construed in accordance with sound constitutional principles so as to preserve their constitutionality.

2. The proscription of mere advocacy of the violent overthrow of the Government would be an unconstitutional infringement upon free speech. The advocacy of the overthrow of the Government by force and violence must be accompanied by an intent to accomplish the overthrow and there must be a "clear and present danger" that the advocated overthrow may be attempted or accomplished. So interpreted the sections are constitutional.

3. During the Summer of 1964, riots broke out in Harlem. During the ensuing weeks, the evidence shows, defendant intended to inflame the already intense passions of the people of Harlem and to incite them to greater violence. Defendant's exhortations calling for organized resistance to the police and the destruction of the State, in the setting of Harlem during the week of July 18th, formed a sufficient basis for the trial court and jury to conclude that his words and actions created a "clear and present danger" that the riots in Harlem would be intensified or, if they subsided, rekindled.

4. Reinterpretation of the criminal anarchy statute in accordance with modern day constitutional standards does not violate defendant's right to procedural due process on the theory that he was not given fair notice of what acts would be punished. Aside from the fact that defendant can be charged with knowledge of the scope of subsequent interpretation by the Court of Appeals, he had the benefit of the Supreme Court's interpretation in Dennis v. United States (341 U.S. 494) of the Smith Act, the language of which is identical to sections 160 and 161.

5. A State can validly prosecute seditious activity directed against a State or local Government even though Congress has pre-empted the field of sedition which is directed against the Federal Government.

6. The gravaman of the charge of conspiracy to riot against defendant is that he entered into an unlawful agreement with others to incite and instigate riot and committed certain overt acts in furtherance of this end. Though speech may have been a means by which defendant sought to further this end of the conspiracy, speech in and of itself is not the object of a prosecution for conspiracy to incite riot.

7. The crime of conspiracy to incite riot does not merge in the crime of riot. Conspiracy is an independent crime for which a defendant can be indicted in addition to being indicted for the substantive crime he allegedly conspired to commit. A conspiracy to riot is separable from the riot itself.

8. The judgment convicting defendant of the crimes of conspiracy to riot, conspiracy to commit the crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy, and advocacy of criminal anarchy is affirmed.

Di Maggio v. Brown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 283; 225 N.E.2d 871; 279 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1624; 65 L.R.R.M. 2518


January 17, 1967, Argued  
April 6, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, which unanimously confirmed the determination of respondent municipal agency which found that the employees were in violation of the Condon-Wadlin Act (Act), N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 108, and which imposed certain penalties.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that there was more than substantial evidence to support the findings of the hearing officer, adopted by municipal agency, that the employees were indeed engaged in a "strike" as that term is defined in N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 108(1). The court further concluded that the sanctions imposed by the municipal agency, such as loss of pay, loss of previously accrued vacation pay, demotion in title and part-time re-employment, were authorized by or permissible under the statute and were imposed in compliance with the necessary notice and hearing required by § 108. The court rejected the employees' contention that § 108 was unconstitutional on its face; § 108 did not constitute a bill of attainder, impose excessive fines or inflict cruel and unusual punishment. However, the court found that the appellate division dismissed the petition without affording the employees an opportunity to factually prove in a court of law the discrimination they alleged. The court found that such an opportunity was required to properly determine the scope of the employees' constitutional rights and any violation thereof.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division that unanimously confirmed the determination of the municipal agency without affording the employees an opportunity to prove discrimination. Such an inquiry was not within the province of the hearing officer, but had to be submitted to a judicial tribunal.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- strikes by public employees -- Condon-Wadlin Act is not unconstitutional on its face -- it does not constitute bill of attainder -- stipulation that statute was not enforced against other striking public employees does not prove existence of purposeful plan of discrimination against petitioners depriving them of equal protection -- petitioners should be afforded opportunity to prove in court their allegation of purposeful discrimination.

1. Petitioners, over 100 ferryboat officers employed under New York State Civil Service Law on ferries operated by the Department of Marine and Aviation of the City of New York, instituted a proceeding to review a determination by that municipal agency which found that they violated section 108 of the Civil Service Law (Condon-Wadlin Act) and which imposed certain penalties in accordance with that act. There was substantial evidence to support the findings that petitioners were engaged in a "strike", as defined in section 108 (subd. 1) of the Civil Service Law. The sanctions were authorized or permissible under the statute.

2. The Condon-Wadlin Act is not unconstitutional on its face as a bill of attainder or otherwise. There is no constitutional provision barring the imposition of reasonable and necessary limitations and conditions on government employment.

3. The stipulation by the parties that the penalty provisions of Condon-Wadlin have not been used, with but two exceptions, over an almost 20-year period of numerous strikes by other public employees does not meet the burden of proof required to sustain petitioners' allegation that they were purposefully discriminated against in the application of Condon-Wadlin as against them, thereby depriving them of equal protection. It must be shown that the provisions of Condon-Wadlin were enforced pursuant to an intentional and invidious plan of discrimination.

4. Petitioners have had no forum at which they could demonstrate such purposeful discrimination, and they are entitled to such an opportunity. The matter is remitted to the Appellate Division.

296 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Thomas v. Melbert Foods, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 216; 225 N.E.2d 534; 278 N.Y.S.2d 836; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1659


January 11, 1967, Argued  
March 2, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order denying defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint for failure to prosecute and granted the motion.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff alleged she was injured and brought an action against defendant. Defendant served on plaintiff a notice for a physical examination. Plaintiff failed to submit to the examination. Defendant moved to dismiss on the grounds of general delay. While the motion was initially denied, that denial was reversed on appeal and the motion was granted. Plaintiff appealed. The court found the facts of the case clearly indicated a general delay by plaintiff. The court's interpretation of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 was that it left untouched the general power of the courts to dismiss actions in their discretion for general delay, but mandated the 45-day demand requirement under the second paragraph of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 where the only basis for the motion was a delay in the filing of a note of issue not amounting to general delay. Since this was a general delay case, the 45-day notice was not required and dismissal was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed dismissal of the action for failure to prosecute.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and nonsuit -- general delay -- CPLR 3216 providing for dismissal of action for failure to file note of issue after 45-day notice is served demanding such filing does not apply where motion based on general delay; action commenced in June, 1962 properly dismissed on motion made in February, 1965 where, after issue joined, plaintiff took no further action -- on motion to dismiss characterized as one for general delay, court can find that no general delay is involved.

1. The second paragraph of CPLR 3216, which provides for dismissal of an action for failure to file a note of issue after a 45-day notice demanding such filing is served, does not apply where defendant makes his motion based on general delay. Accordingly where an action was commenced and issue joined in June, 1962, no other steps were taken in the action by plaintiff and defendant moved in February, 1965 to dismiss because of general delay even though no note of issue had been filed, the motion was properly granted (Salama [Tomich] v. Cohen, 16 N Y 2d 1058, insofar as to the contrary not followed).

2. Where defendant moves to dismiss, characterizing the motion as one for general delay, the court can find that there has been no general delay, that the basis of the motion is a delay in filing a note of issue and require defendant to comply with the 45-day notice provision.

 

297 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cromwell v. Ferrier


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 263; 225 N.E.2d 749; 279 N.Y.S.2d 22; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1666; 21 A.L.R.3d 1212


January 3, 1967, Argued  
March 2, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner commenced proceedings to review the determinations made by defendant municipal zoning board that two billboards on the owner's land violated a town zoning ordinance. The owner appealed the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed the trial court's denial of the owner's application for review.


OVERVIEW: The challenged ordinance prohibited any billboards within the township that were not located on the same lot as the establishments they advertised. Dismissal of the application for review was affirmed because the ordinance was a reasonable regulation that protected the town from becoming an eyesore. The owner argued that the blanket prohibition was arbitrary and resulted in an unconstitutional deprivation of his property. The court held that the ordinance was constitutional. The court overruled a prior decision, which held an identical ordinance unconstitutional, because it was predicated on the discarded notion that an aesthetic objective alone did not support a zoning ordinance. Zoning ordinances based primarily on aesthetic concerns were no longer per se invalid in view of the decreased advertising importance of outdoor billboards, the substantial increase in the deleterious effects of outdoor advertising, and the changed perspective of courts in the area of economic regulation. However, only those aesthetic considerations that were substantially related to the economic, social, and cultural patterns of a community or district justified a blanket zoning prohibition.


OUTCOME: The order upholding the dismissal of the owner's application to review the zoning board's decision that two of his billboards violated a local zoning ordinance was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of signboards -- zoning ordinance prohibiting nonaccessory signboards anywhere in township is constitutional ( Matter of Mid-State Adv. Corp. v. Bond, 274 N. Y. 82, overruled).

Petitioner owns land on two sides of a highway, on one side of which he operates a service station and diner and on the other he has been forbidden, because of the town's zoning ordinance, to erect a sign advertising his service station and diner because it is not on the same lot. The zoning ordinance which permits signboards related to an establishment located on the same lot ("accessory" signs) and implicitly prohibits throughout the township nonaccessory signs is reasonable, is related generally to the economic, social and cultural setting of the community, is constitutional and does not deprive petitioner of his property ( Matter of Mid-State Adv. Corp. v. Bond, 274 N. Y. 82, overruled).

People v. Pickett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 170; 225 N.E.2d 509; 278 N.Y.S.2d 802; 1967 N.Y. LEXIS 1704


January 16, 1967, Argued  
February 23, 1967, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant welfare recipient sought review of the decision of the Niagara County Court (New York), which affirmed a city court's judgment convicting defendant of a violation of N.Y. Social Welfare Law § 145 for his refusal to accept available employment. The welfare recipient contended that § 145 as interpreted and applied violated his rights under the Thirteenth Amendment and the Federal Anti-Peonage Act.


OVERVIEW: Under N.Y. Social Welfare Law § 131(4), the welfare recipient was not eligible for welfare payments if he refused to accept a position for which he was fitted and able to accept. The welfare recipient was trained at public expense in a landscape gardening school. However, when the state employment service referred him to a job as a landscape laborer, he told the employment official that it was not enough money and he desired to look for a job on his own. The trial court affirmed the city court's order, which convicted the welfare recipient of a misdemeanor under N.Y. Social Welfare Law § 145 for his commission of a wilful act designed to interfere with the proper administration of public assistance and care. The court reversed the conviction and dismissed the information finding that the penal sanction of § 145 was only intended to attach to a wilful act committed in furtherance of some fraudulent scheme to obtain undeserved welfare payments. Since the welfare recipient was not charged with fraud and no proof of fraud was produced at trial, the court concluded that his conviction had to be reversed and the information dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment affirming the welfare recipient's judgment of conviction, and the court dismissed the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- interfering with administration of public assistance and care -- defendant, who refused to accept employment although receiving welfare assistance, was improperly convicted of violation of section 145 of Social Welfare Law -- fraud not charged or proved.

Defendant, who was receiving welfare assistance and refused to accept employment to which he was referred by the New York State Employment Service, was improperly convicted of a violation of section 145 of the Social Welfare Law which provides that "Any person who * * * does any wilful act designed to interfere with the proper administration of public assistance and care, shall be guilty of a misdemeanor". The Legislature intended to apply this penalty only if the act were committed in furtherance of some fraudulent scheme to obtain undeserved welfare payments. Defendant was not charged with fraud and no proof of fraud was introduced on the trial.

People v. Lally


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 27; 224 N.E.2d 87; 277 N.Y.S.2d 654; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 885


October 24, 1966, Argued  
December 30, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Queens County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein. Question certified, being unnecessary, is not answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which held in favor of the state, affirmed the denial of defendant's motion to vacate his commitment order, and found N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 454 constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and wounded three persons and pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity. He was acquitted on the ground of insanity and committed to the state hospital. Defendant filed a motion to vacate his commitment order and argued that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 454 was unconstitutional in that it required him to prove his sanity and the right to be at large. Defendant did not request a hearing as to his present condition. The trial court denied defendant's motion. While § 454 was constitutional, the court reversed the judgment and remitted the proceeding for a hearing to be held pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 454(2), (3), & (5) so that defendant would not be deprived of his constitutional rights. According to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 74, any person civilly committed who was not a prisoner whose term was about to expire was entitled to a jury trial as to his mental competency. The court held that before there could be any commitment to the state hospital for the insane under § 454 procedures, a person had to be accorded all the protections of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law §§ 74, 85, which included a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remitted the matter for a hearing as to defendant's competency. The court held that before defendant could be committed to the state hospital for the insane, the trial court was required to accord him a hearing, including a jury trial, if requested.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- acquittal by reason of insanity -- detention of defendant in hospital for criminally insane -- order, entitled in criminal action, refusing to vacate prior order committing defendant to State hospital for criminally insane, is appealable as resulting from petition in special civil proceeding -- section 454 of Code of Criminal Procedure providing for commitment of defendant in criminal case, who pleads not guilty by reason of insanity and is acquitted on that ground, to State mental hospital or hospital for criminally insane is constitutional -- defendant detained following acquittal of crime by reason of insanity is remitted for hearing under section 454 to determine if he may be released without danger to himself and others; court reads into subdivision (5) of section 454 provision for jury trial if requested by defendant -- petition seeking release of funds held by Director of hospital brings up exercise of discretion nonreviewable in Court of Appeals.

1. An order, entitled in a criminal action, which refuses to vacate an order committing defendant to a State hospital for the criminally insane under section 454 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, is appealable despite the fact that it is not one of the orders listed as appealable in section 517 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, since the application to vacate constitutes the beginning of a special civil proceeding.

2. Section 454 of the Code of Criminal Procedure (subd. [1]) provides that in a criminal case, if defendant pleads insanity and the jury acquits him on that ground, the court must order defendant committed to the custody of the State Commissioner of Mental Hygiene to be placed in an appropriate institution in the Mental Hygiene or Correction Department. Subdivisions (2) and (3) provide, in substance, that when, after commitment of such a defendant, the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene believes that defendant may be discharged or released on condition without danger to himself or others, he must apply for such discharge or release to the court, whereupon the court appoints psychiatrists to examine him and on receiving the report of the psychiatrists shall order his discharge or release on condition. Subdivision (5) provides that a committed person may make, to the court by which he was committed, an application for his release or discharge following which the court must obtain a report from the Mental Hygiene Department and if the court thinks there is merit in the application shall hold a hearing, etc. Subdivision (6) provides that any person so committed may at any time during his commitment be transferred to an appropriate institution of the Mental Hygiene or Correction Department approved by the heads of both departments. The section is constitutional. A man who has asserted that he was insane at the time he committed a crime and the jury acquits him on that ground should, in his own interest and for the protection of the public, be forthwith committed for detention, examination and report as to his sanity.

3. So that defendant, who pleaded insanity and was acquitted on that ground and then detained pursuant to section 454 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, may not be deprived of any of his rights, the proceeding is remitted to Criminal Term of the Supreme Court for a hearing to be held pursuant to subdivisions (2), (3) and (5) of section 454. The issues there to be tried are whether defendant may be discharged or released without danger to himself or to others, and, if that question be answered in the negative, whether he is so mentally ill as to require hospitalization. To provide defendant with protection equal to that of other persons under statutes relating to adjudications of mental incompetency the court reads into subdivision (5) a provision for a jury trial of these issues, if defendant so requests. If it be determined as a result of the hearing that defendant, while not dangerous to himself or others, is mentally incompetent and in need of hospitalization, the court shall recommit defendant to the State Commissioner of Mental Hygiene for confinement in a State civil hospital for the insane. He shall in that event not be confined in Matteawan State Hospital for the Criminally Insane. Before there can be any commitment to Matteawan State Hospital for the insane under section 454 procedures, a person must be accorded all the protections of sections 74 and 85 of the Mental Hygiene Law including a jury trial, if requested.

4. So much of the petition as seeks release of part of defendant's funds held by the Director of Matteawan State Hospital for the purpose of obtaining independent psychiatrists to examine defendant and report to the court brings up a discretionary question not reviewable in the Court of Appeals.

Keystone Associates v. Moerdler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


19 N.Y.2d 78; 224 N.E.2d 700; 278 N.Y.S.2d 185; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 890


November 22, 1966, Argued  
December 30, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant preservation corporation sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order that declared a statute unconstitutional and compelled respondent Commissioner of the Buildings of the City of New York (commissioner) to issue a permit for demolition of an old opera house.


OVERVIEW: Respondent building association commenced an action against the commissioner and the preservation corporation to compel the issuance of a license to demolish an old opera house. Respondent opera association filed a lawsuit against the city and the preservation corporation to declare a newly enacted statute unconstitutional. The trial court declared the statute to be a taking of the opera association's property during the six-month period, found the amount of the deposit insufficient to cover the damage to be suffered, and further found that by limiting the recovery amount the Legislature illegally invaded the judicial province. The appellate division affirmed the trial court's decision, which was again affirmed on appeal. The court held that the statute was not enacted in furtherance of the police power. It thus constituted an unreasonable interference with property rights for which the State had to provide a sure and certain fund for recovery of the damage that would be suffered. The amount of such fund had to be established through the judicial process, not by the Legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court upholding the order declaring a state statute unconstitutional and directing issuance of a demolition permit.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- stay of demolition of opera house -- chapter 691 of Laws of 1966, which created private corporation for purpose of preserving Metropolitan Opera House, was not enacted in exercise of police power, fails to provide definite fund for recovery of damages, fails to permit judiciary to determine just compensation and is unconstitutional.

Chapter 691 of the Laws of 1966, which created a private corporation to be known as The Old Met Opera House Corporation for the purpose of preserving the Metropolitan Opera House, gave it power to condemn the property, and provided that the Superintendent of Buildings of the City of New York could refuse a demolition permit for 180 days upon deposit of $ 200,000 by the corporation which would serve as security for any damages the owners might suffer, is unconstitutional. It was not intended to protect the public health, safety and welfare and was not enacted in furtherance of the police power. As a condemnation statute, pending the exercise of the power of condemnation, it unreasonably interferes with the rights of the owners to build upon or improve their property. The delay of six months cannot be justified. The fixing of $ 200,000 as a fund for damages is inadequate. Moreover, there is no guarantee that the assetless corporation will be able to pay damages in excess of the posted security. The State must provide a sure and certain fund for the recovery of the damage which will be suffered. The fixing of just compensation is a judicial function and the amount of compensation cannot be established by the Legislature.

302 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Green v. Lang


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 437; 223 N.E.2d 19; 276 N.Y.S.2d 604; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 945


September 21, 1966, Argued  
December 1, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, New York County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, Personnel Director of the Department of Personnel of the City of New York (department) challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment in favor of petitioner employees invalidating employment reclassifications without examination made for intervenor incumbents by the Civil Service Commission of the City of New York (commission).


OVERVIEW: After a series of promotion examinations, the employees had reached the highest civil service clerical position. The commission abolished the old classification and substituted a new grade providing that persons in the old class were eligible for reclassification without examination. The commission promoted the incumbents to the highest grade without examination. A New York court in an unrelated case held that civil service employees could not be assigned to a new grade without examination. Accordingly, the commission restored the original classifications and continued the incumbents in the highest grade. The employees challenged the continuance of the incumbents in the restored grade. The trial court held that the commission had no power to revive the abolished grade. The appellate court affirmed without opinion. On appeal, the court reversed. The commission could repeal or modify a rule that it had made. The commission had the latitude to assign duties and fix salaries within a class broadly achieved by competitive examination. Because transfers from one position to another in the same grade were valid, the reassignment of incumbents within the grade 5 was also valid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order, which had affirmed a trial court's declaration in favor of the employees and against the department that the commission's reclassifications were null and void.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- reclassification -- New York City Civil Service Commission, which in 1954 abolished highest clerical position (Rule X, Clerk, Grade 5) with unlimited salary, and substituted group of new classifications including Administrative Assistant and higher titles, had undoubted power in 1963, following adverse judicial decision, to modify rule it had previously made and to restore Rule X and reclassify thereto employees previously reclassified to Administrative Assistant but later reclassified to higher grades; petitioners, also reclassified to Administrative Assistant but not to higher grades, showed no clear right to invalidate commission's assignments and reclassification; public policy grounds allow administrative officers latitude to assign duties and fix salaries within class; order affirming judgment invalidating commission's reclassification reversed and petition dismissed.

1. The New York City Civil Service Commission, pursuant to a Career and Salary Plan authorized by the Board of Estimate, abolished effective July 1, 1954 the Rule X, Clerk, Grade 5, the highest clerical position in the city competitive civil service and a class without maximum salary, and substituted a new Rule XI clerical administrative group of new classifications, including Administrative Assistant and other higher titles, for which persons in the former Clerk, Grade 5, were declared eligible without further examination and without being made subject to any maximum salary specified for such new positions. However, the basic concept of this allocation was invalidated in Matter of Weber v. Lang (13 A D 2d 345, affd. 11 N Y 2d 997) which, although allowing initial allocations to new grades where substantially the same duties would be performed, required that, after an unlimited grade was abolished and broken down into new grades in actual operation, the former right of unlimited assignment be terminated as to new grades with respect to incumbents of the former unlimited grade who had not actually performed the duties of such new grades. In an effort to preserve to incumbents in the then unlimited grade their right to unlimited salary and to assignment to unlimited duties, the commission, pursuant to a request of the Board of Estimate, adopted on December 30, 1963 a resolution restoring to Rule X the unlimited type of positions existing prior to 1954. The commission then approved the appointment and continuance in unlimited Grade 5 of those employees previously appointed, under the rescinded Rule XI, to grades higher than Administrative Assistant, and under such unlimited classification they were to receive the salary and perform the duties pertaining to the rescinded Rule XI positions. In this proceeding by petitioners, who were reclassified effective January 1, 1956 from Rule X, Clerk, Grade 5, to the grade of Administrative Assistant but were never classified above such grade, Special Term invalidated the commission's 1963 appointment to the restored Rule X, Grade 5, of other employees who had likewise been reclassified from Rule X, Clerk, Grade 5, to Administrative Assistant but whom the commission thereafter reclassified, without examination, to higher grades. The court agreed with petitioners' contention that the former valid method of allocating duties and salaries within an unlimited grade could not be restored after the grade had been broken down into new components and that the commission, which had initially promulgated the allocation, had no power, as a matter of law, to rescind it. However, the power of a public authority, with rule-making capacity, to repeal or modify a rule it has made, in the absence of demonstrable adverse effect on mature rights, must be assumed as a matter of course and be deemed undoubted. The sweeping injunctive interdiction upon the city's reclassification granted at Special Term was unwarranted on this record, and an order affirming such injunction should be reversed and the petition dismissed.

2. Petitioners do not show that they would pass promotion examinations for the abolished competitive grades above Administrative Assistant or that they will not be advanced in responsibilities or salaries under the now restored unlimited grade, but show that they have not yet been advanced and other employees have been. Accordingly, occupying positions to which they have been duly promoted and which are not themselves changed by the new classifications, they show no clear right to invalidate the assignment of duties to other employees and the payment of salaries within the rules as now amended.

3. Furthermore, there are public policy grounds to allow latitude to administrative officers to assign duties and fix salaries within a class broadly achieved by competitive examination and their judgment should not be overridden, unless interdicted by Constitution or statute. If, as has been held, transfers from one position to another in the same salary grade are valid, the reassignment of various administrative duties to incumbents within the re-established Rule X, Grade 5, is also valid and, if the original unlimited grade was established by a valid exercise of administrative power, those city officers who had created the grade and who later had modified it would normally have power to restore it.
