304 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Spahn v. Julian Messner, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 324; 221 N.E.2d 543; 274 N.Y.S.2d 877; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1019


September 22, 1966, Argued  
October 27, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant publisher appealed the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the award of an injunction and damages to plaintiff professional baseball player in his action against the publisher for the unauthorized publication of a fictitious biography of his life.


OVERVIEW: A professional baseball player brought an action against a publisher under N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 51 for the unauthorized publication of a fictitious biography of his life. The biography dramatized and fictionalized events. The trial court awarded the professional baseball player an injunction to stop the publication of the book as well as damages. The publisher appealed. The court affirmed the order of the lower court because fictitious reporting about the professional baseball player was not in the public's interest and not protected by the Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of an injunction as well as damages to a professional baseball player.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- right of privacy -- publication of fictionalized biography of outstanding professional baseball pitcher constitutes unauthorized exploitation of his personality for purposes of trade proscribed by Civil Rights Law ( § 51) -- application of section 51 to fictionalized biography does not violate freedoms of speech and press guaranteed by Constitution ( New York Times Co. v. Sullivan, 376 U.S. 254, distinguished).

1. The publication of a fictionalized biography of plaintiff, an outstanding left-handed pitcher in professional baseball, constitutes an unauthorized exploitation of his personality for purposes of trade proscribed by section 51 of the Civil Rights Law. Plaintiff as a public personality and insofar as his professional career is involved has no right of privacy. His personality, however, may not be fictionalized and then exploited for defendants' commercial benefit through the medium of an unauthorized biography.

2. The application of section 51 to this publication does not violate the freedoms of speech and press guaranteed by the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the Federal Constitution. ( New York Times Co. v. Sullivan, 376 U.S. 254, distinguished.)

 

305 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


240 Scott, Inc. v. State


Claim No. 37065


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 299; 221 N.E.2d 456; 274 N.Y.S.2d 673; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1059


September 26, 1966, Argued  
October 20, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of the order of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed the trial court's judgment that dismissed respondent claimant's claim, and which remitted the matter to the trial court to enter an award in favor of the claimant after a trial.


OVERVIEW: The claimant owned a warehouse at an intersection. The level of one of the streets had been raised by a viaduct constructed in accordance with plans filed in the city's engineer's office in the years 1897, 1898, and 1914. It was level with the second story of the claimant's building, to which it furnished access by means of an aerial ramp. This viaduct had recently been demolished in connection with the construction of a specific section of a thruway. The street was thus lowered to its original grade affording access on that side to the claimant's building to the first floor only. The claimant filed an action against the state, seeking damages for change of grade authorized by Buffalo City, N.Y., Charter § 367 (later amended). The trial court subsequently dismissed the claim. The lower court reversed the judgment dismissing the claim, and remitted the matter to the trial court to enter an award in favor of the claimant after a trial. The court affirmed the order, by holding that the claimant was entitled to damages for the demolition of the viaduct because the change of grade compromised the whole width of the traveled portion of the street outside the claimant's building.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which reversed the trial court's judgment that dismissed the claimant's claim, and which remitted the matter to the trial court to enter an award in favor of the claimant after a trial.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- compensation -- change of grade -- where State eliminated viaduct constituting entire traveled portion of street on one of two streets abutting claimant's property thereby reducing street level to first floor, change of grade resulted -- damages authorized by statute for change of grade properly recoverable by claimant as abutting owner actually sustaining damage -- street elevation based upon actual grading work performed by city is established grade and was "recorded" within meaning of statute rendering city liable for damages for change of grade.

1. Where claimant's property fronted on one street at the first floor level and on the intersecting street at the second floor level because of the construction of a viaduct by the City of Buffalo pursuant to plans filed in the City Engineer's office in 1897, 1898 and 1914, which viaduct constituted the only traveled portion of such street, a change of grade resulted when the viaduct was eliminated in the course of constructing a portion of the Thruway, thereby reducing the level of the entire traveled portion of the street to that of the ground floor of claimant's building.

2. The State has assumed the liability of the city, imposed by the Charter (Buffalo City Charter, former § 367), for damages resulting from the alteration of "the recorded grade of any street". What resulted to claimant's property was not the closing of the street, so that recovery of damages is precluded because suitable access was provided to the property by another street. Damages authorized by statute to be awarded for change of grade can be recovered by an abutting owner who has actually sustained such damage, as did claimant here, in cases where, under the common-law rule, it would have had no cause of action if the street whose grade was changed had been entirely eliminated.

3. The Buffalo City Charter (§ 674) provided that, when the grade of a street is changed, "such alteration shall be recorded as provided in this act for the recording of the original establishment and description of such grade." Section 179 required the City Engineer to "record all streets, * * * now or hereafter established, and record and describe the grades and any alteration of the grades thereof". For purposes of liability for damages for change of grade, the legally authorized street elevation based upon the actual grading work performed by city authorities is the established grade, and the grade of the street abutting claimant's property was "recorded" sufficiently to come within the language of former section 367 of the Buffalo City Charter. The technical oversight of the City Engineer does not exonerate the State from payment of damages.

 

306 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tannenbaum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 268; 220 N.E.2d 783; 274 N.Y.S.2d 131; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1100


May 2, 1966, Argued  
September 29, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the court of appeals (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted him of the crime of selling obscene material to an infant 17 years of age, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 484-i.


OVERVIEW: At the instigation of a police operation, the 17-year-old infant approached defendant's cigar store, examined an outside stand containing "girlie" magazines, selected 2, and entered the store. Defendant looked at them, priced them, and sold them to the infant, who thereafter departed. Once outside, the infant wrote his name and the time and place of purchase on an inside cover without further examining the magazines' contents. The magazines sold to the infant were devoted purely to sex, told pictorially by nudes and by tales of sex orgies in picture and prose. Defendant was charged and convicted of the crime of selling obscene material to an infant 17 years of age. The trial court found that he had knowledge of the magazines' contents and sold them to a person under the age of 18 years. The court of appeals affirmed the conviction. The court affirmed the judgment, by holding that defendant's right to due process of law was not violated because § 484-i was not unconstitutionally vague, where it neither unduly restricted dissemination of protected matter nor unduly inhibited receipt by those who were constitutionally entitled to receipt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, which affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendant of the crime of selling obscene material to an infant 17 years of age, in violation of the relevant statute.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- magazine sold by defendant, of whose contents he had knowledge, to 17-year-old boy, violated section 484-i of Penal Law forbidding dissemination of obscene material to those younger than 18; defendant's conviction properly affirmed -- section provides standard of obscenity and is not vague -- proof of seller's knowledge of age of purchaser is not predicate to conviction.

1. A magazine, sold by defendant to a 17-year-old boy, fell within the ban of section 484-i of the Penal Law forbidding the dissemination of material obscene as to those under 18, though not necessarily obscene as to the adult community, and defendant had knowledge of its contents. Accordingly, a judgment convicting defendant of violating the statute was properly affirmed.

2. Section 484-i divides the material to which it applies into three categories defined with precision and clarity. The "obscenity" standard is the same. The material must be "posed or presented in such a manner as to exploit lust for commercial gain and which would appeal to the lust of persons under the age of eighteen years or to their curiosity as to sex or to the anatomical differences between the sexes". The thrust of the statute is against pandering the obscene to minors. Material which appeals to the minor's "curiosity as to sex or to the anatomical differences between the sexes" without more does not fall within the ban of the statute. The words "posed or presented in such a manner as to exploit lust for commercial gain" exclude legitimate works of art, educational texts and literature with redeeming social value, and give clear and unequivocal warning of the conduct to be avoided. There is nothing vague about the statutory prohibition and the statute provides reasonably ascertainable standards of guilt readily determinable by men of reasonable intelligence.

3. Despite the fact that the statute forbids dissemination of the proscribed materials to those under 18, and affords full First Amendment protection of the same material to those 18 and older, proof of the seller's knowledge of the age of the purchaser is not a predicate to a conviction. The statute neither unduly restricts dissemination of protected matter nor unduly inhibits receipt by those who are constitutionally entitled to receipt.

In re City of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 741; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1110


September 19, 1966, Submitted  
September 29, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Motion by appellants-respondents to amend remittitur granted in a memorandum.

Motion by respondent-appellant for reargument denied.

Motion by respondent-appellant to amend remittitur denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners instituted an action against respondent city for an alleged taking. The landowners made a motion to amend the court's remittitur of the trial court's decision in the landowners' favor.


OVERVIEW: By its opinion, the court made it clear that the landowners were entitled to immediate payment of the award made below for the tangible property taken and that there was to be a further hearing with respect to the additional award for "going concern" value, to which the court held the landowners were also entitled. The court held that in view of the city's contention that N.Y.C. Admin. Code § B15-21.0 prevented the entry of a separate partial decree directing payment of the award already made, the court granted the landowners' motion to amend the remittitur and make even more explicit the court's meaning. The court concluded that the landowners should not be denied a separate partial judgment in the present proceeding, for to do so would unnecessarily delay payment of an award already held to be due and owing and would thereby impose upon the landowners an undue hardship. Accordingly, the court held that a judgment reflecting the award for the tangible assets should now be entered and payment therefor made, subject, of course, to any lawful claims existing against such award.


OUTCOME: The court modified its decision and as so modified affirmed, with costs to the landowners, and remitted the case for the entry of final judgments for the amounts theretofore awarded for the tangible assets and for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- separate partial judgment -- amendment of Court of Appeals remittitur -- Court of Appeals remittitur in case of Matter of City of New York (Fifth Ave. Coach Lines) (18 N Y 2d 212) amended to provide for entry of separate partial judgment directing payment of award already made below for tangible property taken -- in opinion in said case, Court of Appeals made it clear that claimants were entitled to said payment immediately and that there was to be further hearing with respect to additional award for "going concern" value to which, it held, claimants were also entitled -- section B15-21.0 of Administrative Code of City of New York does not bar entry of such separate partial judgment -- denial thereof would unnecessarily delay payment of award already held to be due and owing and would impose undue hardship.

310 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bookcase, Inc. v. Broderick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 71; 218 N.E.2d 668; 271 N.Y.S.2d 947; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1169


February 24, 1966, Submitted  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bookseller brought an action against respondents, police commissioner and others, challenging the power of the state to prohibit sales to persons below the age of 18 of literature not "obscene" within existing judicial standards for suppression of sales to the general public. The booksellers sought review from a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which held that N.Y. Penal Law §§ 484-i, 484-h were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The bookseller was prosecuted for selling the book "Fanny Hill" to a person under the age of 18 in violation of former § 484-h. The bookseller was found guilty, but the judgment was reversed and the section was declared unconstitutional. The legislature re-wrote the section. The bookseller did not allege that the new section was vague, but that the state did not have the power to pass such a statute. In affirming the decision of the trial court and rejecting the bookseller's argument that the distinction of age was an unconstitutional infringement upon the freedom of the press, the court noted that the Supreme Court had stated that a concept of variable obscenity for the protection of children, in a properly drawn statute, was not only within the power of the legislature but was a desirable and even necessary provision. The court held that, since material was not inherently "obscene" or unprotected, but its obscenity varied with the circumstances of its dissemination, the fact that "Fanny Hill" was deemed not obscene for sale to the general public in New York did not preclude a determination that it was harmful to minors or "obscene" as regards the community of those below 18.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which held that the legislature had the power to pass the statute.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- Legislature had constitutional power to adopt statutes (Penal Law, §§ 484-h, 484-i), which prohibit exposing minors to harmful materials and prohibit sale of pornographic material to minors.

The Legislature had the constitutional power to adopt section 484-h of the Penal Law which prohibits exposing minors under the age of 17 years to harmful materials and section 484-i which prohibits the sale or delivery of pornographic material to minors under the age of 18. The State can exercise its power to protect the health, safety, welfare and morals of its community by barring the distribution to children of books recognized to be suitable for adults.

 

311 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jenad, Inc. v. Scarsdale


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 78; 218 N.E.2d 673; 271 N.Y.S.2d 955; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1170


March 29, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of Appellate Division reversed and that of Special Term reinstated, with costs in this court and in the Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant village sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order denying a motion by plaintiff developer for summary judgment and granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint in plaintiff's action challenging the validity and constitutionality of N.Y. Village Law § 179-1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, pursuant to N.Y. Village Law, §179-k, gave its planning commission the authority to approve proposed plats for subdividing lands in the village including the authority to require, as a condition precedent to the approval of subdivision plats which show new streets or highways, that the subdivider allot some land within the subdivision for park purposes or, at the option of the village planning board, pay the village a fee in lieu of such allotment. A trial court denied plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and dismissed. The appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court reversed and held that N.Y. Village Law § 179-l was valid and enforceable and not in violation of any constitutional or statutory ban. The court found that the language of the statute was not vague and that the provision was not a tax, but a reasonable form of village planning for the general community good.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and granted defendant's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- subdivision of lands -- parks and playgrounds -- Village of Scarsdale, in giving its Planning Commission power to approve subdivision of lands (Village Law, § 179-k), had power to authorize board to require subdivider to allot some land within subdivision for park purposes or to pay village fee in lieu of such allotment -- statute (Village Law, § 179-l) contains sufficient grant of power to permit developers to pay cash in lieu of setting aside land.

1. The Village of Scarsdale, in giving its Planning Commission power to approve proposed plats for subdividing lands in the village (Village Law, § 179-k), had the power to authorize the planning board to require, as a condition precedent to the approval of subdivision plats which show new streets or highways, that the subdivider allot some land within the subdivision for park purposes or, at the option of the village planning board, pay the village a fee in lieu of such allotment.

2. There is no constitutional or statutory ban against section 2 of article 12 of the Rules and Regulations of the Planning Commission as approved by the village trustees, which gives the commission power to direct that, in lieu of such dedication of land, a charge or fee of $ 250 per lot be collected by the village "and credited to a separate fund to be used for park, playground and recreational purposes in such manner as may be determined by the Village Board of Trustees from time to time."

3. There is a sufficient grant of power in section 179-1 of the Village Law to permit developers to pay cash in lieu of setting aside areas for parks, playgrounds and similar purposes and it is valid and enforcible. While the statute does not specifically make such provision, it does provide that a village planning board may waive the provision for providing park plans "subject to appropriate conditions and guarantees". The phrase "appropriate conditions and guarantees" reasonably includes the provision for the payment of cash in lieu of setting aside the lands -- the fund "to be used for park, playground and recreational purposes".

4. The rules and regulations provide that the moneys collected be put into "a separate fund to be used for park, playground and recreational purposes" and that expenditures from such fund are to be made only for "acquisition and improvement of recreation and park lands" in the village. These provisions are not unconstitutional for vagueness ( Gulest Assoc. v. Town of Newburgh, 25 Misc 2d 1004, affd. 15 A D 2d 815, distinguished and disapproved).

5. The provisions with regard to parks do not constitute an unconstitutional and unauthorized "tax".

 

312 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nappi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 136; 219 N.E.2d 176; 272 N.Y.S.2d 347; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1177


June 1, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and a new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed the decision of the Schenectady County Court (New York), which reversed a trial court decision convicting defendant of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving at an excessive speed in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(c). The appeals court reversed the conviction and held that § 1180(c) was unconstitutionally vague. The court reversed and ordered a new trial. The court held that the provision of § 1180(c), that the driver of every vehicle had to, consistent with the requirements of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(a), drive at an appropriate reduced speed, applied the standard of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(a) to the specific situations enumerated in N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(c). The court ruled that the law was not unconstitutional. The court held that a question of fact was presented concerning defendant's guilt under § 1180. The court ruled that since the reversal was on the facts as well as the law, it could not reinstate the judgment of conviction rendered by the trial court, and the decision had to be reversed and a new trial ordered.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- speeding -- section 1180 (subds. [a], [c]) of Vehicle and Traffic Law, providing rule for speed of motor vehicles on public highway, when read together, not unconstitutionally vague and indefinite -- statute valid and prima facie case established -- County Court on appeal could not dismiss information -- new trial ordered.

1. Section 1180 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law provides as follows: "Basic rule and maximum limits. (a) No person shall drive a motor vehicle on a public highway at a speed greater than is reasonable and prudent under the conditions and having regard to the actual and potential hazards then existing. * * * (c) The driver of every vehicle shall, consistent with the requirements of subdivision (a) of this section, drive at an appropriate reduced speed when approaching and crossing an intersection or railway grade crossing, when approaching and going around a curve, when approaching a hill crest, when traveling upon any narrow or winding roadway, and when any special hazard exists with respect to pedestrians, or other traffic by reason of weather or highway conditions." The language of subdivision (c) that "The driver of every vehicle shall, consistent with the requirements of subdivision (a) of this section, drive at an appropriate reduced speed" applies subdivision (a) to the specific situations enumerated in subdivision (c). When so read section 1180 (subd. c) is not unconstitutionally vague and indefinite.

2. The County Court stated that, even assuming the validity of the statute, the People had failed to prove the case and the information should have been dismissed. A question of fact was presented concerning the defendant's guilt under subdivision (c) of section 1180 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law. A new trial must be ordered instead of a dismissal if the reversal be on the facts rather than on the ground that the People failed to prove defendant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt as a matter of law. Here the statute is valid and a prima facie case was established. The Court of Appeals has the power to review since the County Court dismissed the information which it could only do, and here did erroneously, in the determination of a question of law. Inasmuch as the reversal of the County Court was, therefore, also on the facts, the County Court could not dismiss but was obliged to grant a new trial, and in this situation the Court of Appeals cannot reinstate the judgment of conviction rendered by the Justice of the Peace but must order a new trial.

 

313 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Klose


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 141; 219 N.E.2d 180; 272 N.Y.S.2d 352; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1178


June 7, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of an order from the Dutchess County Court (New York) reversing a judgment convicting defendant of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1124 and dismissing the information. The issues were whether § 1124 was unconstitutionally vague and indefinite and whether the evidence was sufficient to make out a question of fact for the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a violation of § 1124. The county court reversed the conviction and dismissed the information finding that § 1124 was unconstitutionally vague and indefinite. On further review, the court reversed the order from the county court and ordered a new trial. The court held that the statute was sufficiently clear and positive to give the average citizen unequivocal warning of the rule to be obeyed. The statute dealt with a standard for passing other vehicles in circumstances too numerous or unpredictable for particular enumeration. Aside from hills and curves, "clearly visible" was plain language that might have encompassed such circumstances as weather conditions, hour of the day, and artificial light conditions. It informed drivers, in no uncertain terms, of what was expected of them. The same was equally true of "free of oncoming traffic." A "sufficient distance" depended on circumstances prevailing at the time when judgment must have been exercised. That requirement did not make the statute vague or uncertain. The court found that the evidence was clearly sufficient to create a question of fact as to defendant's compliance with the statutory language.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order from the county court which had reversed defendant's conviction for violating a vehicle and traffic law dealing with overtaking and passing another vehicle. The court ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- passing motor vehicles -- section 1124 of Vehicle and Traffic Law, providing for manner of overtaking and passing motor vehicles, not unconstitutional for vagueness or indefiniteness -- question of fact was presented to trial court and County Court could not dismiss -- new trial ordered.

1. Section 1124 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law provides as follows: "No vehicle shall be driven to the left side of the center of the roadway in overtaking and passing another vehicle proceeding in the same direction unless such left side is clearly visible and is free of oncoming traffic for a sufficient distance ahead to permit such overtaking and passing to be completely made without interfering with the safe operation of any vehicle approaching from the opposite direction or any vehicle overtaken. In every event the overtaking vehicle must return to the right-hand side of the roadway before coming within one hundred feet of any vehicle approaching from the opposite direction." The statute is not unconstitutional for vagueness or indefiniteness.

2. The County Court reversed the judgment of conviction and remitted the fine and dismissed the information. Whether it had the jurisdiction to reverse on the facts and dismiss the information is a question of law. If a question of fact was presented to the trial court, the County Court could not reverse and dismiss on the facts. At most, it could order a new trial. The evidence was sufficient to create a question of fact as to the defendant's compliance with the statutory language. Accordingly, a new trial is ordered.

 

314 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Willcox v. Stern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 195; 219 N.E.2d 401; 273 N.Y.S.2d 38; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1184


April 25, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant stockholder challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which struck from the trial court's order the leave to replead granted to the stockholder. The trial court granted respondent New York State Superintendent of the Insurance Department's (Superintendent) a motion to dismiss the stockholder's N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The stockholder petitioned for an article 78 proceeding to review and annul the action of the Superintendent approving a merger of insurance companies. The stockholder alleged that his constitutional rights were violated by the approval of the merger that "froze" him out and deprived him of his status as a stockholder. The trial court granted the Superintendent's motion to dismiss the petition for failure to sufficiently plead a cause of action, but it allowed the stockholder leave to amend the petition. The appellate court struck the stockholder's leave to amend, and the stockholder appealed. The court held the appellate court correctly struck the leave granted by the trial court to replead the allegations of fraud because there was no factual support for the allegations. The court found the Superintendent acted reasonably and rationally, and the record evidence warranted his approval of the merger. The stockholder erroneously used the article 78 proceeding to challenge the fairness of the stock valuation. The court also found that the stockholder had no constitutionally protected right to continue as a stockholder where he received fair and just compensation for his interest.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court striking the stockholder's leave to replead was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- merger -- Superintendent of Insurance properly approved merger where one company owned over 97% of other company's stock (Insurance Law, § 486; cf. Stock Corporation Law, § 85) -- petitioner, who owns less than 1/10 of 1% of stock of merged corporation, entitled to payment in cash -- remedy of appraisal and payment sufficient protection -- payment of $ 25.74 per share was fair and equitable -- no showing of fraud -- petitioner properly required to apply to Special Term for leave to replead.

1. The Superintendent of Insurance approved a merger of American Surety Company of New York (American) and Transamerica Insurance Company (Insurance) which owned over 97% of American stock. Petitioner owns less than 1/10 of 1% of the issued and outstanding stock of American. The proposed merger agreement provided that minority stockholders of American would receive $ 25.74 per share for their stock. Section 486 of the Insurance Law provides that the Superintendent shall approve a merger if he is satisfied that it "is fair and equitable, and is not inconsistent with the laws and the constitution of this state and of the United States and that no reasonable objection exists thereto." Section 85 of the Stock Corporation Law permits freeze-outs where one of the merging corporations owned 95% of the stock of the other. A minority stockholder has no constitutional right to preserve his status as a stockholder in the surviving corporation. Section 85 anticipates a "cash payout" by which minority stockholders may be frozen out of continued participation in the merged corporation. The validity of section 85 has previously been upheld ( Beloff v. Consolidated Edison Co., 300 N. Y. 11). The remedy of an appraisal and payment for one's shares affords fair and just compensation to dissenting stockholders while allowing the overwhelming majority to proceed with the merger. So long as the value of petitioner's interest is compensable, he has no constitutionally protected right to continue as a stockholder.

2. The Insurance Law had its own provisions regarding merger, but contained no merger statute such as section 85 of the Stock Corporation Law. Nor, at the time of the merger, did the Insurance Law contain any provision tying it into the Stock Corporation Law. It is clear, however, that under the present statutes, if the present case had arisen after September 1, 1964, there would be direct authority for the Superintendent's approval of this type of merger with a cash payout. Since mergers of stock insurance companies were authorized (Insurance Law, § 481), it was not unreasonable, arbitrary, or capricious for the Superintendent, when considering proposed mergers, to adopt as a standard that set by the Legislature in respect of other types of stock companies.

3. The determination of an administrative agency will be accepted by the courts if it has warrant in the record and a reasonable basis in law.

4. The payment of $ 25.74 per share was fair and equitable and the Superintendent acted reasonably and rationally. His approval was warranted by the record and has a reasonable basis in law.

5. The inquiry at the present time in this proceeding is limited to the reasonableness and rationality of the Superintendent's act (cf. Insurance Law, § 486). This proceeding under CPLR article 78 is not the proper vehicle for determining the actual value of the stock. On the other hand, equity will act despite the existence of an appraisal remedy, where there is fraud or illegality. Petitioner has made no showing of fraud, and the Appellate Division correctly struck the leave granted by the trial court to replead the allegations of fraud and required petitioner to apply to Special Term for leave to replead.

 

315 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mary Chess, Inc. v. Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 205; 219 N.E.2d 406; 273 N.Y.S.2d 46; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1185


June 6, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: On the appeal of Catco and Rorech: Order reversed, with costs, and judgment of Special Term reinstated.

On the appeal of Chess, Ralsan, Baymore, Nylacore, Cove and Universal: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision of the trial court, which declared certain portions of the building zone ordinance of defendant city to be unconstitutional as applied to the property owners.


OVERVIEW: One set of property owners owned the interior parcel of land in question and another set of owners owned the perimeter parcels. The interior tract was used for industrial purposes. When the tract was sold in 1942, it was zoned industrial and remained so until 1960 when the building zone ordinance upzoned the properties to single family residence. All of the property owners claimed the ordinance was confiscatory in that none of the properties were adaptable to the uses permitted. The trial court found the ordinance confiscatory and unconstitutional as to the vacant interior parcels but upheld as to the improved perimeter parcels. The court of appeal affirmed with regard to the perimeter parcels but reversed as to the interior lands. On appeal, the court affirmed with regard to the perimeter parcels and reversed as to the interior parcels. The court held that the ordinance deprived the interior owners of the substantial use of their property for an unknown and uncertain number of years, and as to those owners, the ordinance was confiscatory and void. However, the improved perimeter was properly zoned as residential.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in the property owner's action against the city concerning a building code ordinance with regard to the perimeter parcels but reversed as to the interior parcels.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- with regard to owners of property on perimeter of oval area, who utilize their property under valid nonconforming uses for industrial purposes, amendment of zoning ordinance not unconstitutional -- ordinance unconstitutional as to interior parcels -- permitted uses there not feasible.

1. Four of the plaintiffs are owners of all the land on the perimeter, while two of the plaintiffs own all but one acre of the land in the interior of a tract which consists of an oval-shaped dirt road enclosing four acres of undeveloped land, surrounded by five buildings of heavy mill construction, each of which face inward. The uses permitted by an amendment to the zoning ordinance are single-family dwellings, municipal buildings, schools, colleges, etc., churches, scientific or research laboratories subject to the restriction that they "may not offer any goods or services for sale", and florists' greenhouses, country estates, and nurseries. The perimeter plaintiffs, who have continuously utilized their improved parcels for industrial purposes, have valid nonconforming uses. The unimproved interior parcels have no nonconforming use. With regard to the perimeter parcels, the amended ordinance is constitutional.

2. A nonconforming use may be considered in determining the effect of the ordinance upon the property. In comparing the value of the land before and after the amendment of the ordinance to see if there is a confiscation, the continuing value as a nonconforming use must be considered. Six years after the passage of the amendment the perimeter businesses are apparently flourishing and the property seems to be in profitable use. The value of the parcels as nonconforming uses was not shown to be so disproportionate to their value as permitted uses as to amount to an economic taking of the property.

3. Proof based on the unrealistic assumption that valuable buildings would be demolished and vacant lands sold whereas the statute, far from requiring such demolition, creates nonconforming uses which may continue indefinitely did not constitute proof of confiscation.

4. Moreover, the evidence supports the finding of the trial court that the perimeter plaintiffs failed to show that their buildings could not be reasonably and economically converted to the permitted scientific or laboratory use.

5. The interior parcels are virtually surrounded by 15 acres of industrial buildings and the surrounding nonconforming uses will apparently continue into the foreseeable future. As the trial court found, it would offend common sense to hold that the permitted uses would be feasible. In addition the permitted uses of a research laboratory or greenhouse, while perhaps technically feasible on one of the two parcels, are not economically feasible. One interior parcel was neither functionally nor economically adaptable for any of the permitted uses. The ordinance deprives the interior owners of the substantial use of their property for an unknown and uncertain number of years, and as to those owners the ordinance is confiscatory and void.

6. While the result reached is anomalous and perhaps self-defeating, it is hoped that the legislative body will review the zoning regulation in light of the ruling that the present amendment is unconstitutional as regards the interior.

 

316 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Peters


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 238; 219 N.E.2d 595; 273 N.Y.S.2d 217; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1188


May 5, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction for unlawful possession of burglar's instruments from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for unlawful possession of burglar's instruments. He argued that the tools were discovered as a result of an illegal search and seizure and should have been suppressed. The court affirmed the conviction. An off-duty police officer heard suspicious activity in his apartment building. After phoning the local police, he chased down one of the two suspects and frisked him. Feeling what he thought could have been a knife on defendant's person, the police officer confiscated an envelope containing burglar's tools. The tools were used in defendant's prosecution and he sought review. The court held that the police officer had a reasonable suspicion to stop defendant, which fulfilled constitutional requirements and the requirements of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 180-a. Once the stop was justified, the police officer was allowed to frisk defendant for weapons. When a possible weapon was found, the police officer permissibly seized it, although it was not a weapon, but the instruments of a crime.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for unlawful possession of burglar's instruments was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- New York City policeman living in apartment house in City of Mount Vernon, while not in uniform, apprehended, questioned and frisked defendant who was tiptoeing about hallway and who fled at his approach; in defendant's pocket was plastic envelope which felt like weapon, and upon examination of envelope it was found to contain burglar's tools; policeman delivered defendant and tools to Mount Vernon police; following defendant's indictment for possession of burglar's tools, his motions to suppress such seized evidence were properly denied and, on his plea of guilty, he was properly convicted -- section 180-a of Code of Criminal Procedure and prior case law authorize police to stop, question and frisk person, upon reasonable suspicion; then, if frisk and questioning reveal evidence and probable cause, police may arrest such person and seize such evidence.

1. A New York City policeman, who lived in the City of Mount Vernon on the sixth floor of an apartment house which was served by an elevator, heard a noise at his door one afternoon, looked through a peephole in the door and saw two men tiptoeing about the hallway. He telephoned the Mount Vernon police, got his gun and returned to the door where he observed the men tiptoeing toward the stairway. He slammed his door and followed them. He apprehended one of them, the defendant whom he did not recognize as a tenant, on the stairs. The policeman asked him what he was doing there and defendant claimed he was looking for a girl friend, but refused to identify her because, he said, she was married. Thereupon the policeman, who was not in uniform, frisked defendant for a weapon by tapping his groin pockets and under his arms. He felt something hard which could have been a knife and withdrew from the right pants pocket an unsealed opaque plastic envelope which upon examination was found to contain six picks, two Allen wrenches with the short leg of each filed down to a screwdriver edge and a tension bar. He recognized these as burglar's tools. He took defendant down to the manager's office and turned him and the envelope over to the Mount Vernon police. Following defendant's indictment for possession of burglar's tools (Penal Law, § 408), his motions to suppress the seized evidence and dismiss the indictment were properly denied, and then, on his plea of guilty, he was properly convicted.

2. The stopping, inquiring and frisking and the resultant seizure were lawful, both under case law ( People v. Rivera, 14 N Y 2d 441, followed) and under section 180-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure.

3. Under section 180-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, such stopping, inquiring and frisking do not constitute an arrest. The statutory standard -- reasonable suspicion -- may be based on facts and circumstances of which a policeman has reasonably trustworthy information and upon his experienced appraisal of the appearance of criminal activity. Finally, before he may arrest the suspect and seize evidence from his person, he must have reasonable or probable cause. On the scale of absolute knowledge, reasonable suspicion is somewhat below probable cause and is equally objective.

4. Section 180-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure does not authorize "unreasonable searches and seizures" (U. S. Const., 4th Amdt.); indeed, there is not much difference between the statutory standard and the standard used prior to the statute in People v. Rivera (supra).

5. Hallways and stairways of multiple dwellings are public places within the intendment of section 180-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure.

People v. Sibron


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 603; 219 N.E.2d 196; 272 N.Y.S.2d 374; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1195


June 7, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- defendant was convicted, upon his plea of guilty, of violating section 3305 of Public Health Law (unlawful possession of narcotics) -- at pretrial hearing on motion to suppress evidence as alleged product of unlawful search and seizure, testimony by police officer that, after observing defendant talking to known narcotics addicts, he approached him, said "You know what I am looking for" and, when defendant reached into his pocket, put his hand into defendant's pocket, intercepting defendant's hand and finding therein packets in metal tinfoil wrapper -- motion to suppress was denied -- contentions by People that section 180-a of Code of Criminal Procedure was constitutional, and that, under said section and on facts of case, officer's conduct in stopping defendant and reaching into his pocket was justified, contraband found in process was legitimately obtained and subsequent arrest was authorized -- judgment of conviction was properly affirmed. 

 

318 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Saffore


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 101; 218 N.E.2d 686; 271 N.Y.S.2d 972; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1172


June 9, 1966, Argued  
July 7, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed his conviction, upon his plea of guilty, for the misdemeanor crime of assault in the third degree. Defendant was imprisoned for one year, the maximum for a misdemeanor, and had to pay a $ 500 fine. If the fine was not paid, defendant was ordered imprisoned an additional day for each dollar remaining unpaid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, an indigent, argued that the trial court knew of his indigency and it was a denial of due process and of equal protection to let his lack of money determine how long he was to be imprisoned. The maximum term of imprisonment for a misdemeanor was one year (eight months with time off for good behavior). The sentence of imprisonment for defendant's nonpayment of the fine was reversed. The court found that defendant's sentence meant that he would have been confined for 8 months plus 500 days. Essentially, defendant would have been a prisoner for 26 months for a crime that the maximum term was 1 year. The court ruled that a fine of $ 500 for a misdemeanor, levied on defendant who had no money at all, was necessarily excessive when it meant in reality that he had to be jailed for a period far longer than the normal period for the crime. The court held that when payment of a fine was impossible and known by the trial court to be impossible, imprisonment to work out the fine, if it resulted in a total imprisonment of more than one year for a misdemeanor was unauthorized and violated defendant's right to equal protection and the constitutional ban against excessive fines.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the sentence of imprisonment for nonpayment of a fine and ordered defendant discharged.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- imprisonment for failure to pay fine -- sentencing court knew that defendant, who pleaded guilty to misdemeanor, had no funds and was unable to pay fine -- sentence of one year and fine of $ 500, with provision that fine, if not paid, be served out at rate of one day's imprisonment for each dollar unpaid, was illegal -- defendant discharged from custody -- defendant's right to equal protection of law and constitutional ban against excessive fines violated.

1. Defendant, having pleaded guilty to a misdemeanor, was given a sentence of one year and required also to pay a fine of $ 500, the fine, if not paid, to be served out at the rate of one day's imprisonment for each dollar remaining unpaid ( Penal Law, § 245; Code Crim. Pro., §§ 484, 718). The sentencing court knew that defendant had no funds or property and was unable to pay the fine, with the result that, whereas the maximum term of imprisonment for a misdemeanor is one year, defendant was to be confined for eight months (with time off for good behavior) plus 500 days. The sentence was illegal and defendant should be discharged from custody.

2. The direction that defendant shall go to jail for nonpayment of the fine is not part of the punishment but a means of collecting the fine. It, therefore, is contrary to the meaning and intent of section 484 of the Code of Criminal Procedure to order a defendant to stay in prison a total of more than a year until he pays a fine, when the court knows that he cannot possibly pay it.

3. Under the circumstances, defendant's right to equal protection of the law and the constitutional ban against excessive fines are violated.

 

319 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Donohue v. Cornelius


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 390; 218 N.E.2d 285; 271 N.Y.S.2d 231; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1278


March 23, 1966, Argued  
June 9, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sergeant in state police sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which entered an order modifying the law and facts and affirmed the judgment of the supreme court at a special term which determined that respondent superintendent of the division of state police had failed to show any necessity for a regulation authorizing a promotion examination.


OVERVIEW: The sergeant was required to take an examination under Article 10 of the State Police Department Rules and Regulations. A challenge was brought against the regulation under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78. The special term set aside the article. The superintendent appealed to the appellate division and the sergeant was allowed to intervene. The appellate division sustained that part of the order of the special term that annulled the examination for sergeant but refused to pass on the examination for lieutenant since the police superintendent had not had the opportunity to justify the degree of subjectivity in the examination. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that because the sergeant had passed the examination that was the basis for the challenge, he did not have standing to intervene in the action. The court reasoned that the examination against which the sergeant sought relief had not injured him in any way. The court held that for misbehavior of an officer, in his office, either for misfeasance or nonfeasance, no one could maintain an action against him, unless he could show a special and particular damage to himself, and that it had damaged his private rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the special term, which had sustained the sergeant's right to intervene in the appeal challenging the superintendent's regulation requiring the promotion examination. The court remitted the matter to the special term for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- State Police -- parties aggrieved -- petitioner, who sought order annulling regulations of New York State Police and promotion examination for Sergeant based upon said regulations, is not party aggrieved and has no right to maintain proceeding where he took examination and passed it -- petitioner does not have right as private citizen and taxpayer to maintain proceeding.

1. After the court at Special Term had dismissed the original petition to annul article 10 of the Regulations of the New York State Police and a promotion examination announcement for Sergeant based upon said article 10, the promotion examination was held and was taken and passed by petitioner. He thus became eligible for promotion to Sergeant. Therefore, the examination against which he seeks relief herein has not injured him. Moreover, after he became eligible for promotion, charges were instituted against him for failure to obey a lawful command of a superior officer and, as a result of these charges, he was dismissed from the State Police. Petitioner is not a party aggrieved and has no right to maintain this proceeding to set aside the rules or the examination.

2. He does not have a right as a private citizen and taxpayer to maintain this proceeding (cf. St. Clair v. Yonkers Raceway, 13 N Y 2d 72). The courts are not given jurisdiction as guardians of the rights of the people generally against illegal acts by the executive or legislature but, when a controversy arises between litigants, the court must then follow the Constitution and may incidentally pass upon an act of the executive or legislative branches while determining the individual rights of the parties.

3. Petitioner has made no showing to bring his case within the exception to the rule under which the Court of Appeals, on occasion, decides questions which, even though moot as between the parties, affect the entire State or transactions of a similar character which are numerous and likely to arise with frequency.

 

320 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jones


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 404; 218 N.E.2d 291; 271 N.Y.S.2d 240; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1280


May 5, 1966, Argued  
June 9, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order denying, without hearing, an application by defendant for an order vacating a judgment that sentenced defendant as a second felony offender following his conviction on a guilty plea of third-degree robbery, resentenced him as a first felony offender, and remitted the application to the Westchester County Court.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Penal Law § 1943 was amended, effective 1964, to provide that a previous conviction should not be utilized as a predicate for multiple offender treatment if the earlier conviction was obtained in violation of the United States Constitution. The county court concluded that the new 1964 language operated only when the prior conviction sought to be challenged was had in another state. However, the new language specified "a previous conviction in this or any other state." The appellate division reversed, holding that the 1964 amendment had retroactive effect and that the statute by clear and unambiguous language permitted a defendant to challenge for constitutionality a previous conviction in the State of New York. Conceding that the principal purpose of the amendment was to challenge convictions obtained in other states, the court found no way to avoid the statutory language "previous conviction in this or any other state." As to the retrospective application of the 1964 amendment to multiple offender sentences prior to the effective date of the amendment, it was settled by prior case law that the amendment was generally retroactive.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division vacating defendant's conviction as a multiple offender was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- section 1943 of Penal Law, dealing with multiple offender punishment, as amended in 1964, creates new right to review constitutionality of prior conviction within State of New York or outside State -- amendment is retroactive, applying to multiple offender sentences prior to effective date of amendment.

1. The language of the 1964 amendment to section 1943 of the Penal Law, dealing with multiple offender punishment, that a previous conviction "in this or any other state" shall not be utilized as a predicate for multiple offender treatment if the earlier conviction was obtained in violation of the United States Constitution shows that it creates a new right and remedy applicable to convictions both "in this * * * state" and in any other State. It is immaterial that a remedy by way of coram nobis motion already exists with respect to a conviction within the State and that the remedy of section 1943 of the Penal Law, because it is available in any county in which a defendant is charged as a multiple offender and not necessarily in the county where the prior conviction was had, will cause difficulty, inconvenience and expense.

2. The 1964 amendment is retroactive and applies to multiple offender sentences prior to the effective date of the amendment.
