People v. Sprowal


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 884; 218 N.E.2d 343; 271 N.Y.S.2d 310; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1290


May 3, 1966, Argued  
June 9, 1966, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- defendant was convicted of violation of section 722-b  [*3] of Penal Law, which renders one guilty of disorderly conduct who loiters in or about school buildings or grounds without permission from principal or custodian or in violation of posted rules -- evidence that defendant, handing out leaflets at school, refused to stop blocking way of students, sat down on street, refused to move and continued handing out leaflets -- contentions that statute was unconstitutional as imposing ban on distribution of literature on public street, that requirement of prior approval by principal or custodian was unconstitutional prior restraint on free speech and publication, that statute was void for vagueness, and that court abused discretion in imposing 60-day sentence -- judgment of conviction properly affirmed. 

 

322 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bergerson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 398; 218 N.E.2d 288; 271 N.Y.S.2d 236; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1279


May 4, 1966, Argued  
June 9, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court rendered upon a verdict convicting defendant of endangering the life or health of a child in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 483(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, an adult male, bought beer and hosted a party for several teenaged boys, all of whom were minors. One boy was killed on his way home after being struck by an automobile, and several others became drunk and sick. On appeal, defendant contended that the indictment should have been dismissed because the Grand Jury was prejudiced by the introduction of irrelevant evidence regarding the boy's death and that the introduction at trial of the same evidence denied him a fair trial. He argued that, because the bill of particulars did not set forth what acts or conduct of defendant were claimed to have caused the boy's death, nor was there any proof on the trial as to the circumstances of his death, the evidence relating to the death was immaterial, irrelevant, and highly prejudicial. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment, finding that because defendant was on trial for endangering the life and limb and injuring the health of the boy, the fact of his death would seem to be highly relevant. The evidence presented was clearly material to the issue whether defendant's conduct caused or permitted the life or limb of the deceased child actually to become endangered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division affirming defendant's conviction for endangering the life and health of children.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- endangering life or health of child -- Penal Law ( § 483) protects physical health of child and was properly applied to defendant who organized beer party for boys -- immaterial that defendant was indicted under subdivisions 1 and 2 of section 483 rather than subdivision 3 of section 484 which makes it misdemeanor to sell alcoholic beverages to children under 18 -- evidence that boy covered by first count of indictment died was relevant before Grand Jury -- and on defendant's trial -- Penal Law ( § 483) is constitutional.

1. Section 483 of the Penal Law (endangering life or health of child) is not confined to sexual offenses or morals cases but is intended to protect the physical health, morals and well-being of children and was properly applied to defendant who organized a beer party for boys 16 years of age and under.

2. That defendant was indicted under subdivisions 1 and 2 of section 483 and not under subdivision 3 of section 484 which makes it a misdemeanor to sell, give away or procure to be sold or given away alcoholic beverages to children under the age of 18 is of no consequence. The same act may constitute a different crime and be punishable in different ways, and the State may proceed under a general statute even though a more specific one is available.

3. Since the first count of the indictment charged that defendant wrongfully and knowingly caused the life of a named boy to be endangered and his health impaired, and the other three counts charged that defendant caused or permitted each of three other boys to be placed in a situation that his life or limb was endangered or his morals likely to be impaired, mention of the death of the boy covered by the first count of the indictment was neither incompetent nor immaterial. Such evidence was not illegal when presented to the Grand Jury. In order to obtain an indictment or conviction under the first count, the People had to allege and prove that the boy's life, limb or health did in fact become endangered, as distinguished from the fact that such eventualities only need likely to have transpired under the other three counts. In order to show the actual danger to which the boy was exposed, it was relevant to show his death. The trial court's conclusion that none of the evidence was illegal is supported by the record, as well as fortified by the presumption that an indictment is found upon legal evidence.

4. On the trial also the fact of the boy's death was relevant to the issue whether defendant's conduct caused or permitted the life or limb of the deceased child actually to become endangered.

5. Section 483 of the Penal Law is not unconstitutional as vague. This statute informed defendant that the offense prohibited was the endangering of the life, limb, health or morals of a child and what was required of him was that he refrain from willfully causing or permitting such danger.

 

323 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Roosevelt Raceway, Inc. v. County of Nassau


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


18 N.Y.2d 30; 218 N.E.2d 539; 271 N.Y.S.2d 662; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1275


March 29, 1966, Argued  
June 9, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs in this court and in the Appellate Division, and matter remitted to the Comptroller of Nassau County for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, county and its comptroller, challenged the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which annulled a determination of the comptroller that assessed a deficiency against petitioner racetrack on its county admission taxes, and granted the racetrack's petition for a judgment that declared unconstitutional 1956 N.Y. Laws 837 and Nassau County, N.Y., Loc. Law 1 (1956).


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to 1956 N.Y. Laws 837 and Nassau County, N.Y., Loc. Law 1 (1956), the county imposed a 30 percent tax on admissions to harness horse race meetings that were held at racetracks located in cities or counties that were adjacent to a certain metropolitan area. The racetrack challenged the tax's validity because only a 15 percent tax was imposed on flat racetracks. The appellate division ordered that the racetrack's tax deficiency was annulled and declared that 1956 N.Y. Laws 837 and Nassau County, N.Y., Loc. Law 1 (1956) were unconstitutional because they lacked a rational basis for a distinction between a tax on flat and harness racetracks. The court reversed the appellate division's order and held that the geographical classification was proper and that the government's power to single out classes of objects for taxes was unquestionable. The court held that it could not say that the classification between flat and harness racetracks was arbitrary and without a reasonable justification because the local governmental legislation and management of harness racetracks adjacent to the metropolitan area were sufficiently different from the flat tracks that were located elsewhere.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division that annulled the comptroller's determination and that granted the racetrack's petition, and the court remitted the matter to the comptroller for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- admissions tax -- horse races -- chapter 837 of Laws of 1956 and Nassau County Local Law No. 1 of 1956, pursuant to which county was authorized to impose 30% tax on admissions to harness horse races held at petitioner's racetrack in Nassau County, retaining limit of 15% on admissions at "flat tracks", are constitutional.

1. Chapter 837 of the Laws of 1956 and Nassau County Local Law No. 1 of 1956, pursuant to which the county, by authorization of the State statute, imposed a 30% tax on admissions to the harness horse races held at petitioner's racetrack in Nassau County, are constitutional.

2. The statute, permitting an increase from 15% admission tax to 30% at harness tracks in cities or counties adjacent to a city having a population in excess of 2,000,000, but retaining the limit to 15% on "flat tracks", limited the increase to petitioner's track in Nassau County and a similar harness racing track in Westchester County. Such geographical classification is valid and did not deny equal protection of the laws to petitioner.

3. The law was not local and did not require, under (former) article IX (§ 1, subd. [b]) of the New York State Constitution, a request from the local Board of Supervisors for a certificate of necessity transmitted by the Governor to the Legislature.

4. The Nassau County Local Law, passed three days before the State enabling act was signed by the Governor, was not void. The local law provided that it should take effect immediately if the Governor meanwhile should have approved the State act, but that, if the Governor should not have attached his signature to the State act on or before the approval of the local law, the latter should take effect on the day on which the Governor should approve the State act. Such arrangements for a statute taking effect on the happening of a future event do not result in invalidity.

5. Section 9 of article I of the State Constitution permitted pari-mutuel betting on horse races "as may be prescribed by the legislature and from which the state shall derive a reasonable revenue for the support of government". This does not mean that the county, as distinguished from the State itself, may not be authorized to collect a tax on admissions to a racetrack where there is pari-mutuel betting. The tax here concerned is not a tax on betting but on admissions fees charged for entering the racetrack.

6. There is a rational basis for different treatment of "flat tracks" and "harness tracks".

7. Petitioner, being required by law to collect and pay over this tax, is a party aggrieved and has standing to maintain this proceeding.

8. The constitutionality of the statute and local law may be tested in a proceeding under article 78 CPLR.

 

324 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Grossman v. Baumgartner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 345; 218 N.E.2d 259; 271 N.Y.S.2d 195; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1317


March 31, 1966, Submitted  
June 2, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, former tattoo parlor operators, challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's judgment for plaintiffs. Plaintiffs' action against defendants, the Commissioner of Health of the City of New York and other parties, had sought to enjoin the enforcement of New York City, N.Y., Health Code § 181.15 and to have it declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The former tattoo parlor operators, who were put out of business by § 181.15, which outlawed tattooing except by a doctor, brought an action to declare § 181.15 unconstitutional and to enjoin defendants from enforcing it. The trial court declared § 181.15 unconstitutional and the intermediate court reversed, holding that the record showed that the prohibition of lay tattooing was an advisable procedure for the security of life and health. Defendants argued that there was a direct causal connection between tattooing and hepatitis, and that the threat was too dangerous and deadly to depend on regulatory measures to assure proper sterilization techniques in tattooing. The court on appeal affirmed the intermediate court's decision. The court held that there was no warrant for the charge that defendant board of health acted arbitrarily or capriciously or that the regulation under attack was unreasonable. The court further held that § 181.15 was not an unconstitutional exercise of legislative power by the board of health in violation of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 1. The court also found that the state legislature had not preempted the field in enacting provisions pertaining to tattooing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate court's decision that the city health code regulation, which prohibited tattooing except by a doctor, was not unconstitutional and that rejected the former tattoo parlor operators' request for injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

Health -- tattooing -- provision of section 181.15 of New York City Health Code, which prohibits tattooing by person not licensed to practice medicine or osteopathy, is constitutional -- evidence demonstrates compelling medical necessity for said provision -- its enactment was constitutional exercise of power vested in Board of Health -- State has not evidenced any desire or design to pre-empt field -- plaintiffs seeking declaration of unconstitutionality, not being doctors or prospective tattoo customers, lack requisite standing to raise questions as to portion of section which prohibits tattooing even by physicians except for medical purposes.

1. The provision of section 181.15 of the New York City Health Code, which prohibits the tattooing of a human being by a person not licensed to practice medicine or osteopathy, is constitutional. It has been established that there is a connection between tattooing and hepatitis, that rigorous regulation of the tattoo industry, with the aim of permitting tattooing without danger to the public, would be ineffective, and that tattooing is a health hazard which can be controlled only by the prohibition of lay tattooing. Since the evidence demonstrates compelling medical necessity for the provision, it is not unreasonable and the Board of Health did not act arbitrarily or capriciously in enacting it.

2. The enactment was a constitutional exercise by the Board of Health of the power vested in it by the New York City Charter (§ 558, subds. b, f).

3. The State Legislature clearly intended the Board of Health to be the sole legislative authority within New York City in the field of health regulations so long as its regulations are not inconsistent with, or contrary to, State law dealing with the same subject matter (N. Y. City Charter, § 558; Public Health Law, § 228). The State has not purported to regulate those engaged in the business of tattooing nor has it evidenced any desire or design to pre-empt that field. Accordingly, the provision is not unconstitutional by reason of pre-emption.

4. Plaintiffs are neither doctors nor prospective tattoo customers and they therefore lack the requisite standing to question, on constitutional grounds, that portion of the section which prohibits tattooing even by physicians except for medical purposes.

 

325 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Heyert v. Orange & Rockland Utilities, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 352; 218 N.E.2d 263; 271 N.Y.S.2d 201; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1318


March 29, 1966, Argued  
June 2, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner brought an action against defendant utility for removal of a gas main. The Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York) granted the utility's motion for summary judgment for an order of inverse condemnation and appointed commissioners to fix the property owner's compensation. The Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York) affirmed, as modified to have the trial court fix the compensation. The utility appealed.


OVERVIEW: The utility's gas main was installed under a highway to the center of which the property owner had a fee interest. The town had an easement for highway purposes and granted the utility the right to lay the gas main. The utility argued that the main's installation was within the scope of the town's easement. The court concluded that the town's easement included only the right of passage over the property owner's land. The court considered the principle of stare decisis and found no reason to disturb a rule of property that affected many land titles. The property owner was entitled to recover compensation for the value of the land appropriated by the utility for the gas main.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the utility's motion for summary judgment for an order of inverse condemnation.


HEADNOTES

Easements -- easement for public highway purposes -- scope of easement -- easement acquired by town to use land for public highway purposes does not include right to install gas main beneath it or to authorize utility corporation to do so -- such installation is, in essence, partial taking which may result in consequential damage to adjacent land -- fee owner is entitled to opportunity to prove damages.

1. An easement acquired by a town to use land for public highway purposes does not include the right to install a gas main beneath it or the right to authorize a utility corporation to do so. This is so whether the roadway involved is a rural or more populous highway.

2. Such an installation is, in essence, a partial taking which may cause only nominal damages but, in some instances, may result in serious consequential damage to the adjacent land.

3. Accordingly, where plaintiff owns the fee to one half of a street and the town, which has an easement for public highway purposes, has permitted a utility corporation to install a gas main beneath it, plaintiff is entitled to an opportunity to prove damages at a hearing.

 

326 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Commercial Credit Corp. v. Lafayette Lincoln-Mercury, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 367; 218 N.E.2d 272; 271 N.Y.S.2d 212; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1319


April 25, 1966, Argued  
June 2, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff filed an appeal from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed an order of the trial court dismissing the complaint under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 as against one defendant due to plaintiff's unreasonable neglect to proceed in the action as against that defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued defendants, alleging liability on guarantees in commercial transactions. Three years after the complaint was filed and answered, plaintiff served a note of issue and statement of readiness on all defendants, but immediately withdrew these documents so that one defendant could complete pretrial procedures. Four years then elapsed with no activity in the case. Plaintiff then served and filed another note of issue and one defendant moved to dismiss, failing, however, to comply with the second paragraph of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216. The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint as against that defendant. Although the legislature had set forth specific steps for dismissal of suits for lack of prosecution in the second paragraph of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216, trial courts could still dismiss suits for general delay under the first paragraph. Issue had been joined six and one half years earlier, but plaintiff let the action lie dormant, taking no affirmative steps whatever for four years, except to file the note of issue at the last minute, and plaintiff offered no excuse for this inordinate delay.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing plaintiff's complaint, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and nonsuit -- dismissal for general delay -- courts still have power to dismiss actions for general delay under first paragraph of CPLR 3216, notwithstanding addition of second paragraph thereto which relates to dismissal motion based upon failure to serve and file note of issue and which provides for service of demand prior to making of motion -- where dismissal motion is made after service and filing of note of issue, said second paragraph has no application.

1. Prior to September 1, 1964, CPLR 3216 provided that "Where a party unreasonably neglects to proceed in the action against any party who may be liable to a separate judgment, the court, on its own initiative or upon motion, may dismiss the party's pleading on terms." Effective September 1, 1964, a second paragraph was added to CPLR 3216 which provided that no motion to dismiss "based upon the failure of the plaintiff to serve and file a note of issue" shall be made or granted until at least six months after the joinder of issue and unless the defendant shall have served a written demand requiring the plaintiff to serve and file a note of issue and, in effect, giving the plaintiff 45 days in which to do so. The addition of this second paragraph was not intended to have the effect of eliminating the first paragraph. The courts still have the power to dismiss actions for general delay. This is confirmed by the history of an unsuccessful attempt made in 1965 to amend CPLR 3216.

2. In the present case, defendant-respondent made a motion to dismiss without complying with the second paragraph of CPLR 3216 and it was granted. Plaintiff relies upon the said second paragraph but it has no application since the dismissal motion was made after plaintiff had served and filed its note of issue.

 

327 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Havemeyer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 216; 217 N.E.2d 26; 270 N.Y.S.2d 197; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1408


February 16, 1966, Argued  
April 28, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to the Surrogate's Court, Suffolk County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state tax commission sought review of a decision by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, New York, which affirmed an order determining that the value of respondent decedent's interest in certain real estate be excluded from the gross estate subject to tax.


OVERVIEW: Respondent decedent and his son were partners with a partnership agreement made in New York State. Upon respondent's death, the lower court issued an order excluding from the gross estate certain real property located in Connecticut owned by respondent and his son, which has been taxed in Connecticut. Appellant state tax commission sought review of that order. The court reversed, holding that the partnership agreement was made in New York State subject to the New York State Partnership Law contained express provisions under N.Y. Partnership Law §§ 12, 51, converting partnership real estate into personalty and providing that on death it shall pass to the surviving partner or partners as tenants in partnership.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order determining that the value of respondent decedent's interest in certain real estate be excluded from the gross estate subject to tax because the real property although located in another state, was owed by respondent's partnership and, therefore, was subject to the partnership laws applicable in the state in which the partnership was established.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- estate tax -- New York Partnership Law becomes part of partnership agreement made in New York between New York residents -- real property in Connecticut, owned by partnership, is converted by Partnership Law into personalty and must be included in gross estate of deceased partner for computation of New York estate tax.

1. A partnership agreement made in New York between decedent, a resident of New York, and his son, also a resident of New York, is, in accord with the grouping of contracts criteria ( Auten v. Auten, 308 N. Y. 155), governed by New York law and therefore is subject to the New York Partnership Law, which becomes part of the contract.

2. Real property located in Connecticut and owned by decedent and his son as partners, the property having been expressly deeded to the partnership and having been purchased with partnership capital, is converted, by virtue of the Partnership Law ( §§ 12, 51), into personalty which passes on death to the surviving partner as tenant in partnership. Accordingly, it was error to exclude such real property from the gross estate of decedent and to fail to compute the New York estate tax thereon.

330 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Byron


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 64; 215 N.E.2d 345; 268 N.Y.S.2d 24; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1543


January 10, 1966, Argued  
February 24, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and matter remitted to the County Court for further proceedings not inconsistent with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision of the County Court of Chautauqua County (New York), which reversed a judgment that convicted defendant of driving a vehicle that had an inadequate muffler, in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Laws § 375(31), and dismissed the information.


OVERVIEW: The county court held that § 375(31) was unconstitutional for vagueness. The court reversed. The court held that a criminal statute had to be sufficiently definite, clear, and positive to give unequivocal warning to citizens of the rule that was to be obeyed. The court also held that all presumptions and intendments favored the validity of a statute, and that mere doubt did not afford sufficient reason for a judicial declaration of invalidity. According to the court, the test was whether a reasonable man, who was subject to the statute, would have been informed of the nature of the offense that was prohibited and what was required of him. The court also held that such a warning had to be unequivocal, but the requirement did not preclude the use of ordinary terms to express ideas that found adequate interpretation in common usage and understanding. The court thought that § 375(31) sufficiently described the evil to be prevented and informed the motorist of his duty, not to eliminate all noise, but to have a properly maintained muffler to prevent unusual noise. The court also held that when the noise exceeded the usual level, the motorist had violated the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- mufflers -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 375, subd. 31) requiring motor vehicle to be equipped with muffler properly maintained to prevent excessive or unusual noise is constitutional.

Subdivision 31 of section 375 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law providing that "Every motor vehicle, operated or driven upon the highways of the state, shall * * * be equipped with an adequate muffler * * * and properly maintained to prevent any excessive or unusual noise" states with sufficient clarity the rule to be obeyed and is constitutional. The evil sought to be prevented is "excessive or unusual noise" and the statute sets up standards and definitions covering prevention of excessive noises emanating from mufflers. What is usual noise in the operation of an automobile has become common knowledge and anything in excess of that is excessive or unusual and any ordinary motorist should have no difficulty in ascertaining whether or not excessive or unusual noise accompanied the operation of his vehicle. That a truck may make more noise than defendant's vehicle and not violate the statute is not vital. The vital point is that the duty upon all motorists to provide a muffler which will prevent noise greater than the usual noise from the exhaust system of their particular vehicle is exactly the same and the statute applies equally to all. The enactment in 1965 of section 386 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, limiting the amount of noise produced by any vehicle, does not amend subdivision 31 of section 375. The two statutes stand side by side.

332 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kramer v. Vogl


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 27; 215 N.E.2d 159; 267 N.Y.S.2d 900; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1566


January 5, 1966, Argued  
February 17, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff resident appealed a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the order granting the motion of defendant non-residents to dismiss the fraud action for lack of personal jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: A resident of the state served a fraud action on non-residents in Austria where they lived and carried on the business of producing and marketing leather. The resident alleged that the non-residents appointed him as their exclusive agent in the United States and made intentionally false representations that they would not supply the leather to anyone else in the country in competition with the resident. The contract was arranged in Paris and the non-residents made the shipments to the resident f.o.b. European ports. The resident appealed the dismissal of the action. The court refused to exercise personal jurisdiction over the non-residents under N.Y.C.P.L.R. 302(a)(2) or (1). The court held that the resident failed to make a sufficient showing that the non-residents' tortious acts were committed within the state because they did everything in Europe. The court that the cause of action did not arise out of any transaction of business by the non-residents in the state because they carried on no sales, advertising, or promotion activities in the state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, with costs, the dismissal of the residents' fraud claim against the non-residents.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service on nonresident -- plaintiff alleged that defendants, carrying on business in Austria, falsely represented that their goods would not be supplied in this country in competition with plaintiff but sold goods to codefendants -- motion to dismiss complaint properly granted -- no sufficient showing of commission of tortious act in this State ( CPLR 302, subd. [a], par. 2) -- no adequate showing of cause of action arising from transaction by defendants of any business in this State ( CPLR 302, subd. [a], par. 1).

1. Plaintiff is a resident of this State and does business in New York City as a dealer in imported leather. Defendants live in Austria and carry on the business of producing and marketing leathers. Plaintiff alleges that he suffered damage because of the willful fraud and deceit of defendants in that the latter, when appointing plaintiff as defendants' exclusive United States agent to import and distribute defendants' leather products, made intentionally false representations to plaintiff that their leather would not be supplied to anyone else in this country in competition with plaintiff, and that defendants never intended to keep their promises but, in fact, sold their leather goods to codefendants. All shipments from defendants to plaintiff were, as agreed, f. o. b. European ports. The original contract between plaintiff and defendants was arranged at a meeting in Paris and, when plaintiff returned to New York, defendants wrote from Austria to plaintiff in New York a letter confirming the conversation in which plaintiff had been granted the sole American agency for one year. Plaintiff was not paid on a salary or commission basis but bought the leather and paid for it. A motion by defendants to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the defendants had never transacted any business in the State of New York and that the court had not acquired personal jurisdiction as to them was properly granted.

2. There is no sufficient showing under paragraph 2 of subdivision (a) of CPLR 302 of the commission by defendants of a tortious act within this State. The statutory phrase is not synonymous with "commits a tortious act without the state which causes injury within the state". The statute requires that a defendant's act of omission occur within the State. Here, everything defendants did was done in Europe.

3. There is no adequate showing of a cause of action arising from the transaction by defendants of any business in this State under paragraph 1 of subdivision (a) of CPLR 302. Defendants never came into New York State but sold and sent goods into the State pursuant to an order sent from within the State. These defendants, in Austria, made arrangements to sell merchandise f. o. b. to the local distributor. They carried on no sales, promotion or advertising activities in this State. The cause of action here sued upon cannot be said to have arisen out of any transaction of business in this State.

Entwistle v. Murtaugh


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


17 N.Y.2d 6; 214 N.E.2d 153; 266 N.Y.S.2d 969; 1966 N.Y. LEXIS 1638


January 6, 1966, Argued  
January 13, 1966, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


HEADNOTES

Public officers -- oath of office -- possession by Town Clerk of oaths of office of town officers, following his taking of their oaths which he was authorized by law to do, constituted filing with him enough to qualify such officers -- filing, within time limited by law, of oath of office either with County Clerk's office or Town Clerk's office is sufficient.

1. The oaths of office of appellants, duly elected town officials of the Town of Frankfort, whose oaths were taken on December 31, 1963 and January 2, 1964 before the Town Clerk who was authorized by law to take them and who signed the jurat, were in possession of the Clerk at that time and such possession, in law, was sufficient to constitute filing with him enough to qualify appellants. The subsequent delivery of possession of the oaths to the Supervisor was a mere irregularity which did not impair the title of appellants to their offices.

2. The filing, within the time limited by paragraph h of subdivision 1 of section 30 of the Public Officers Law, of the oath of a town officer in either the County Clerk's office or the Town Clerk's office is sufficient (Public Officers Law, § 10; Town Law, § 25).

Fieldston Property Owners' Ass'n v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 267; 213 N.E.2d 436; 266 N.Y.S.2d 97; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 907


December 1, 1965, Argued  
December 30, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Bronx County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff street owner appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the supreme court denying its motion for summary judgment and granting defendant city's cross-motion for summary judgment. The owner's complaint alleging an unconstitutional taking of property was dismissed.


OVERVIEW: The street owner obligated itself, by contracts with abutting homeowners, to maintain and repair the streets until title to the streets was acquired by the city. Although the owner let the public use the streets for years, the city never took title or made repairs. The court reversed an order of the appellate court affirming the supreme court's order denying the owner summary judgment and granting the city summary dismissal in the owner's action claiming an unconstitutional taking of property. The court then remitted to the supreme court, holding that the owner was entitled to a declaration that municipal regulation of parking on the streets did not prevent it from barring parking altogether. The court reasoned that the police could enforce only the city parking regulations, but the owner could assure compliance with its rules by using traditional common-law remedies. The court also reasoned that there was no constitutional defect in N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1642 to the extent that the municipality forbid parking on the streets because that was a proper objective for the exercise of police power and the power to regulate traffic included the power to prohibit parking.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision denying the street owner's motion for summary judgment and granting the city's cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the owner's complaint for a declaration that the city unconstitutionally took its property.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- prohibition of parking on private streets -- statutes empowering City of New York to prohibit parking by public on private streets owned and maintained by plaintiff over which plaintiff has permitted public to drive are constitutional -- however, plaintiff is entitled to declaration that municipal regulation of parking does not prevent plaintiff from barring parking altogether.

1. Statutes which empower the City of New York to "prohibit, restrict or regulate traffic" on the highways -- including a "private road open to public motor vehicle traffic" (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1642) -- "for the facilitation of traffic and the convenience of the public as well as the proper protection of human life and health" (New York City Charter, § 435) are constitutional to the extent that the municipality prohibits on parking private streets owned and maintained by plaintiff but on and over which plaintiff has permitted the public to drive. Such objectives are appropriate for the exercise of state police power. The power to "regulate traffic" includes the power to prohibit parking.

2. Plaintiff is entitled to a declaration, however, that municipal regulation of parking on these privately owned streets does not prevent plaintiff, as the fee owner, from barring parking altogether.

 

336 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Letterio


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 307; 213 N.E.2d 670; 266 N.Y.S.2d 368; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 914


October 21, 1965, Argued  
December 30, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: As to Kohler: Order of the Appellate Term reversed and the judgment of the Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County, reinstated.

As to Letterio: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from judgments of the appellate divisions of the Supreme Court, First Judicial Department (New York), and the Supreme Court, Second Judicial Department (New York), which respectively affirmed a conviction for speeding in spite of the trial court's failure to inform defendant of his right to counsel, and reversed a conviction for speeding because of the trial court's failure to inform defendant of his right to counsel.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that those charged with traffic infractions were expressly excluded from a new statutory scheme for providing counsel to indigent defendants. The court therefore concluded that the new statutory scheme was a clear indication that the legislature had not departed from existing case authority that the court in the City of New York having jurisdiction of traffic infractions was under no statutory duty to inform a defendant of his right to counsel in a trial for a traffic infraction. The court further held that neither the state nor federal Constitution required the court having jurisdiction of a petty offense such as a traffic infraction to advise defendant of the right to counsel. The court noted that New York had long deemed traffic infractions as a form of misconduct distinguishable from more serious breaches of law, and concluded that a traffic court needed only to assure that defendant had a fair forum in which to be heard. The court accordingly reversed the judgment of the appellate court that had reversed defendant's speeding conviction and affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that had affirmed the other defendant's speeding conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court reversing defendant's conviction for speeding was reversed, and the trial court's conviction was reinstated. The judgment of the appellate court affirming the other defendant's conviction for speeding was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- traffic infractions -- right to counsel -- there is no statutory or constitutional requirement that defendant charged with traffic infraction be apprised of right to counsel and to assignment of counsel.

There is no statutory or constitutional requirement that a defendant charged with a traffic infraction be apprised of his right to counsel and to an assignment of counsel. The general language of section 41 of the New York City Criminal Court Act is not the corrective legislation suggested in People v. Felberbaum (9 N Y 2d 213). In enacting section 41 the Legislature has not manifested an intention to overrule People v. Felberbaum.

 

337 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Federated Dep't Stores, Inc. v. Gerosa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 320; 213 N.E.2d 677; 266 N.Y.S.2d 378; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 915


October 19, 1965, Argued  
December 30, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner department store sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which confirmed a determination of respondent city comptroller that petitioner owed additional sales taxes.


OVERVIEW: The department store was a Delaware corporation that had its principal office in Cincinnati, Ohio, and that owned three large department stores in New York city. The city comptroller had issued a determination against the department store that it owed additional sales taxes, which was confirmed by the lower court. On appeal by the department store, the court affirmed the lower court's order. The department store vigorously attacked as both unfair and unconstitutional the formula that the city comptroller had used in apportioning the taxes against it. The court rejected the argument that the tax tended to increase the department store's out of state and wholly non-taxable sales receipts. Although the department store owned many stores in other states, these stores did not contribute much to the sales of the New York city stores. The court did not find the department store had demonstrated unfairness in the actual impact on it of the tax assessments of which it complained.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order that confirmed a determination of the city comptroller that the department store owed additional sales taxes.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- general business and financial tax -- City of New York imposes excise tax for privilege of carrying on business therein -- formula of Comptroller for allocation of that portion of interstate transactions which for local tax purposes are properly attributable to New York is fair and reasonable as applied to petitioner's three retail department stores within City of New York.

Under the authority of section 24-a of the General City Law, the City of New York imposes an excise tax for the privilege of carrying on business in the city. The Comptroller prescribed a formula for allocation of that portion of the interstate transactions which for local tax purposes are properly attributable to New York (Administrative Code, § B46-2.0, subd. b). The formula weighs into consideration the total property and total wages within and without the city deemed to have produced the interstate business; the amount of wholly taxable business (that done in intrastate transactions); the amount of wholly untaxed business (that done outside the State), and the allocable business (that done in interstate commerce emanating from the city). The formula creates a percentage to be applied to allocable receipts and a limit of 66 2/3% is provided. Petitioner owns three large department stores within the city, some of whose sales are made in New York for delivery in New Jersey and Connecticut. The formula resulted in taxation of 45% to 48% of the receipts from allocable interstate business emanating from New York City in the calendar years from 1953 to 1958. The impact of the formula on petitioner is fair and reasonable. The formula offers a rough approximation of a just allocation. Stores owned by petitioner in other States contribute nothing to the sales of the New York City stores of merchandise delivered to New Jersey and Connecticut, and the part played by petitioner's central headquarters in Cincinnati, Ohio, is not shown to have large significance in a fair allocation of the basic activity which produced the sales. The business of retail sales is a thing closely related to the place where a store is. When sales are made, even to a customer who comes, calls or writes in from New Jersey or Connecticut, an activity in large part local exists.

Siegel v. People


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 330; 213 N.E.2d 682; 266 N.Y.S.2d 386; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 916


November 24, 1965, Argued  
December 30, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of this court dated October 1, 1964 granting leave to appeal vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizen appealed from a resettled order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which denied the citizen's application (1) to vacate an ex parte order granted to respondent state permitting (a) wire tapping of the citizen's telephone and (b) electronic eavesdropping of his office premises, and (2) to require disclosure of the order and affidavits.


OVERVIEW: The state filed an ex parte application for an order for eavesdropping. After obtaining the ex parte order, the state began wire tapping the citizen's telephone and electronic eavesdropping of his office premises. The citizen filed an application under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5704(a) to vacate the ex parte order and disclose it and the affidavits. The appellate court denied the application. After initially granting the citizen leave to appeal, the court vacated that order and held that the appellate court should have dismissed the proceeding rather than have denied relief. The court found that the appellate court did not have jurisdiction to entertain the citizen's application because the ex parte order was criminal in nature. Therefore, the relief sought was not authorized pursuant to Rule 5704(a). Furthermore, there was no provision in the New York Code of Criminal Procedure permitting the citizen's application. Therefore, no review could be had.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed its earlier order granting the citizen leave to appeal the appellate court's denial of his application to vacate the ex parte order permitting the state to wiretap the citizen's telephone and electronically eavesdrop on the citizen and to require disclosure of the ex parte order and affidavits.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Court of Appeals -- order granting leave to appeal -- order of Court of Appeals granting leave to appeal from order of Appellate Division which denied application to vacate ex parte order permitting tapping of telephone and electronic eavesdropping of premises is vacated.

On a petition under CPLR 5704 (subd. [a]) by an attorney who alleged that his telephone was wire tapped and electronic eavesdropping devices were installed in his office premises, pursuant to an ex parte order, to vacate said order and to require disclosure of the order and affidavits, the Appellate Division denied the motion. An order of the Court of Appeals granting leave to appeal from the order of the Appellate Division is vacated.

 

339 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Martell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 245; 212 N.E.2d 433; 264 N.Y.S.2d 913; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 992


October 27, 1965, Argued  
November 24, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged that part of the judgment of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment, rendered upon a verdict convicting her upon charges that she delivered and gave away alcoholic beverages to four named minors in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 484(3).


OVERVIEW: The evidence established that defendant allowed four children under the age of 18 years to congregate in her home where she gave them alcoholic beverages to drink. Defendant was convicted of delivering and giving away alcoholic beverages to the four minors in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 484(3). On appeal, defendant claimed it was not the legislative intent to make criminal the service of alcoholic beverages to youngsters in the home of the person who served them. The court agreed with defendant, reversed the conviction, and dismissed the indictment. In reaching its decision, the court construed § 484(3) and found that it could not reasonably be read as making criminal such dispensing of alcoholic beverages in the home. According to the court, § 484(3) was intended to prohibit certain activities as to children when carried on in certain resorts or by people in certain kinds of business.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by striking that part of the judgment which found defendant guilty of delivering and giving away alcoholic beverages to four named minors and, as modified, affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- giving alcoholic beverages to minor -- provision of Penal Law ( § 484, subd. 3), prohibiting selling or giving away alcoholic beverages to minor, was not intended to apply to service of alcoholic beverages in home.

Subdivision 3 of section 484 of the Penal Law, which makes guilty of a misdemeanor a person who "Sells or gives away, or causes or permits or procures to be sold or given away to any child actually or apparently under the age of eighteen years any alcoholic beverages as defined by the alcoholic beverage control law", was not intended to apply to the service of alcoholic beverages to infants in the home of the person who served them.

Battista v. Power


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 198; 211 N.E.2d 821; 264 N.Y.S.2d 370; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1039


October 27, 1965, Argued  
October 28, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of Appellate Division reversed and that of Special Term reinstated, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Board of Elections of the City of New York, sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which modified and as modified affirmed a judgment in a N.Y. Elec. Law § 330 proceeding. The modification provided that the ballot contain eight columns, which included a line for an independent citizens party.


OVERVIEW: The independent party candidates were also those candidates that were listed on the lines allocated to the republican and liberal parties. On review the court found that a reversal was warranted and that the supreme court's judgment should be reinstated. The court noted that N.Y. Elec. Law § 248 denied an additional independent column on the ballot to a candidate who already appeared thereon as the candidate of two major parties. That statute expressed a continuing legislative policy of preventing the major party candidates from pre-empting the whole ballot through the device of setting up independent political bodies. The court did not doubt the constitutionality of § 248, but noted that in some particular situations it could not constitutionally be enforced because in those instances application would be unfair and prejudicial to a particular class of voters. There was no unconstitutional unfairness because the candidate who was demanding a third line would still have two lines and the other two major candidates would have two lines and one line respectively.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's order and reinstated the special term's order.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- ballots -- order of Special Term which denied to political body column on voting machines in behalf of candidates who had two lines on voting machines as candidates of two political parties is reinstated; Election Law ( § 248) denies such additional line.

An order of Special Term which denied to the Independent Citizens party, a political body, the fifth column (column E) on the voting machines in behalf of candidates who had two lines on the voting machines as the candidates of the Republican and Liberal parties is reinstated. Section 248 of the Election Law denies an additional "independent" column on the ballot to a candidate who already appears thereon as the candidate of two major parties. Under the Special Term order there can be no unconstitutional unfairness since the candidate who is demanding a third line will still have two lines and the other two major candidates will have two lines and one line respectively. ( Matter of Belford v. Board of Elections, 306 N. Y. 70, distinguished.)

Glinski v. Lomenzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 27; 209 N.E.2d 277; 261 N.Y.S.2d 281; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1180


July 8, 1965, Argued  
July 9, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, by reinstating so much of the order of Special Term as contained an injunction against the holding of an election on November 2, 1965 under plan A, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a senator and voters, sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department, which modified an order of the supreme court and dismissed the first cause of action brought by the senator and the voters that sought an injunction to restrain respondent Secretary of State from proceeding with an election of members of the legislature.


OVERVIEW: The proposed election was pursuant to a plan for apportioning and districting the state senate assembly. The supreme court granted judgment in favor of the senator and voters, and the appellate division modified by dismissing that cause of action. On review the court found that the injunction should have been granted and a constitutional convention should be held. The court ordered that, until an election of legislators under a valid districting plan, the presently sitting legislators should continue to sit, and to vote under an appropriate system of weighted voting. The court had to perform its sworn duty by preventing the holding of an election that violated the state constitution. The court held that neither the federal supremacy clause, U.S. Const. art. VI, nor the rules against interference with federal courts by state courts had any application, noting that, as to state questions the state courts were still empowered to control state elections. The proposed election would have violated the positive provisions of the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court modified the appellate division's order by granting the injunction.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- election of legislators -- injunction granted by Special Term against 1965 election of legislators under plan which Court of Appeals has held to be violative of New York State Constitution is reinstated, in absence of controlling Federal decision commanding that election under said plan be held.

1. An injunction granted by Special Term against a 1965 election of legislators under a plan which the Court of Appeals has held to be violative of the New York State Constitution (see Matter of Orans, 15 N Y 2d 339) is reinstated.

2. The Court of Appeals would be obliged to follow a final and binding Federal court order for an election under this plan if such an order had been made, but it has not found or seen such an order. All previous Federal decisions were based upon the assumption that the 1964 or the 1965 Legislature would pass a valid legislative districting statute, but neither did so. In the absence of a controlling decision, commanding that an election under this plan be held, the Court of Appeals must perform its sworn duty by preventing the holding of an election which violates the New York State Constitution.

Van Berkel v. Power


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 37; 209 N.E.2d 539; 261 N.Y.S.2d 876; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1182


May 20, 1965, Argued  
July 9, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the New York City Commissioners of Election (Commissioners), sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court in New York County at Special Term (New York), which declared unconstitutional, null, and void N.Y. Const. art. II, § 1 and N.Y. Elec. Law § 150 insofar as they established an additional 90-day waiting period for naturalized citizens, such as petitioner naturalized citizen, to vote.


OVERVIEW: After the naturalized citizen obtained his status as a naturalized citizen of the United States, he attempted to register to vote for an upcoming election in New York City. The Commissioners refused to register him because he had not acquired citizenship at least 90 days prior to the election, as required by N.Y. Const. art. II, § 1 and N.Y. Elec. Law § 150. In an action filed by the naturalized citizen challenging the 90-day requirement, the trial court found in favor of the naturalized citizen. However, on appeal, the court reversed and found that the requirement was reasonable, was in the power of the state to set voting qualifications, and did not infringe on a pre-empted field of federal legislation. The requirement also did not violate the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which found unconstitutional a requirement that naturalized citizens who wished to vote had to acquire citizenship at least 90 days prior to the election. The court ordered the dismissal of the naturalized citizen's petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- elections -- naturalized citizens -- Election Law ( § 150) and New York State Constitution (art. II, § 1), which provide, as qualification for voting, that naturalized citizen must have been naturalized at least 90 days prior to day of election, are not unconstitutional.

1. Section 150 of the Election Law and article II (§ 1) of the New York State Constitution, which provide, as a qualification for voting, that a naturalized citizen must have been naturalized at least 90 days prior to the day of election, are not unconstitutional.

2. Plaintiff has failed to show that no reasonable basis existed for the 90-day time limitation.

3. The classification of newly naturalized citizens and others is not arbitrary.

4. There is no serious conflict between Federal and State law. The New York State Constitution and statute have not violated any of the prohibitions of the Federal Constitution nor entered into a field pre-empted by the Federal Government.

 

346 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Joseph E. Seagram & Sons, Inc. v. Hostetter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 47; 209 N.E.2d 701; 262 N.Y.S.2d 75; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1184


May 27, 1965, Argued  
July 9, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff liquor wholesalers appealed a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment in favor of defendant liquor authority, declaring constitutional and valid 1964 N.Y. Laws ch. 531, §§ 7(3)(a), 9.


OVERVIEW: A suit was brought by 62 liquor distillers and wholesalers, as well as some importers, seeking to overturn New York's restrictions on pricing for the industry. The court, however, affirmed the judgments below, finding no constitutional violations. The court noted first that the liquor industry had a long history of regulation, control, price fixing, place of time and sale setting, and outright extinction, and that it was too late to suggest that the liquor wholesalers' rights were the same as for those engaged in other industries. Thus, the court found no Equal Protection violations. The court then held that U.S. Const. amend. XXI gave the state the power to enact such price regulations and that the restrictions under § 9 did not unconstitutionally burden interstate commerce. The court stated that, because the wholesalers could not sell in New York at a price below their lowest price elsewhere, the wholesalers could increase their prices elsewhere to compensate. The court further found that the schedule requirements contained in § 7(3)(a), while broad, could be waived by the liquor authority in its discretion and, consequently, the statute did not have to be invalidated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the liquor authority, finding the statutes to be constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- price setting -- statute (L. 1964, ch. 531, § 9) which provided that, on filing schedules of brand owners' prices to wholesalers, brand owner must file affirmation that bottle and case price to wholesalers in New York is no higher than lowest price at which such item of liquor was sold to any wholesaler elsewhere in country or any State or State agency operating public liquor enterprise, not unconstitutional.

1. Section 9 of chapter 531 of the Laws of 1964 provided that, on filing the schedules of brand owners' prices to wholesalers, the brand owner or his designee must file an affirmation "that the bottle and case price" to wholesalers in New York "is no higher than the lowest price at which such item of liquor" was sold the previous month to any wholesaler elsewhere in the country or any State or State agency operating a public liquor enterprise. This statute was adopted to take the place of section 101-c of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, which had authorized a manufacturer who was a "brand" owner to fix the minimum retail prices for that brand. The new statute is not unconstitutional.

2. The State, particularly since the addition of the Twenty-first Amendment to the United States Constitution, could eliminate as well as regulate the liquor traffic within its borders. Therefore, if the statute makes it economically difficult for the liquor industry, it is within the competence of the State to make it that way.

3. A long history of regulation, control and outright extinction lies behind the liquor business in this country, and it is too late today to suggest that the rights of those who choose to engage in it are on a constitutional or legal parity with the rights of people who trade in other commodities.

4. The conditions set down by the Legislature are not economically impossible for the liquor industry. The effect of the statute will be to reduce liquor profits and pass the benefit of some of them on to New York consumers.

5. In effect, the dependence of the New York price on the maximum price of the distiller for his brand elsewhere is to tie the price in this State to a national price. This is not unreasonable nor an interference with interstate commerce.

6. If the effect of the new statute is that the price in New York is too low, the distillers have it within their power to raise the lowest price elsewhere and thus control the base price. The incidental effect on prices in another State does not invalidate the New York statute.

7. The statute is not in violation of the Sherman Anti-Trust Act or the Robinson-Patman Act.

8. Section 7 of the statute requires that filed price schedules show the bottle and case price paid by a retailer and prohibits sale or purchase by a wholesaler unless schedules are filed by brand owners "irrespective of the place of sale or delivery". The statute is concerned with New York practices and, if the sales in other States have no relevancy to New York enforcement, the statute permits the Liquor Authority, for good cause, to waive the general prohibition against sales to wholesalers in the absence of such schedules. It would be reasonable to expect that the statute would be administered consistently with its sole purpose to regulate the intrastate sale of liquor.

 

347 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Seaman v. Fedourich


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 94; 209 N.E.2d 778; 262 N.Y.S.2d 444; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1187


May 26, 1965, Argued  
July 9, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city council appealed by permission of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) from a summary judgment order that unanimously affirmed an order declaring Local Law No. 1 of 1965 of the city to be unconstitutional, as it was found that the existing districting plan of the 13-member body offended the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11.


OVERVIEW: The city council was made up of 13 members at the time of the suit. Each was elected by the inhabitants of one of the city's 13 wards and had the power to cast one vote, of equal weight with the others, on matters coming before the council. The city council enacted a Local Law that proposed the council would be reduced from 13 to seven members, each to be elected from one of seven districts, with certain of the less populous wards simply being incorporated into the new and larger districts. The population estimates employed by the council were obtained by up-dating the 1960 census figures, and by excluding the 3,217 persons who were patients in the state hospital. The court found that the approximations of increase and decrease of population by reason of population movement and other factors were at best estimates and were not to be accepted in lieu of the federal census. Even accepting the council's own population figures, significant population discrepancies existed between certain of the seven councilmanic districts, and no special considerations were called to the court's attention to rationally explain the patent discrepancies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order finding that the redistricting plan was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- municipal districts -- new districting plan adopted by Binghamton Common Council does not meet constitutional requirements -- "one person, one vote" principle applicable to municipal legislative bodies -- New York courts have jurisdiction -- latest official census must be employed.

1. Following a determination that the existing districting plan for the City of Binghamton providing for a 13-member Common Council was unconstitutional, the Binghamton Common Council by Local Law No. 1 of 1965 adopted a new districting plan by which it was proposed that the Common Council be reduced from 13 to 7 members, each to be elected from one of seven districts. The population of each of the new districts, as reflected in the 1960 Federal census, varied from 7,863 to 15,808. The court at Special Term properly determined that this plan did not meet constitutional requirements.

2. Even accepting defendants' own population figures arrived at by "up-dating" the 1960 census and excluding the patients at the State Hospital located within one of the districts, the population discrepancies are so great that the districting does not meet the constitutional requirements.

3. The principle of "one person, one vote", mandated by the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution, is applicable to elective legislative bodies exercising general governmental powers at the municipal level.

4. The courts of New York are vested with jurisdiction of actions brought to vindicate the right to equal representation.

5. The latest official census must be employed in determining population for districting purposes. The New York State Constitution (art. III, § 4) makes the latest Federal census "controlling as to the number of inhabitants in the State or any part thereof" for purposes of State legislative apportionment. Numerous statutes also make that census the determinant whenever "population" is a factor to be taken into account.

 

348 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dusablon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 9; 209 N.E.2d 90; 261 N.Y.S.2d 38; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1269


April 20, 1965, Argued  
June 10, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments of conviction modified to the extent of directing a hearing on the issue of the voluntariness of the confessions introduced as a part of the People's case at the trial and, as so modified, affirmed. Case remitted to the Supreme Court, New York County, for such hearing ( People v. Huntley, 15 N Y 2d 72).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the judgments of the Supreme Court in New York County (New York), which were entered upon the jury's verdicts that convicted defendants of two counts of murder in the first degree and sentenced defendants to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendants shot and killed two people. The court held that the convictions were proper. The court found that the change in N.Y. Penal Law § 1045-a was only procedural in that the current § 1045-a allowed the jury to consider defendants' former crimes in determining defendants' sentences, whereas the former § 1045-a only permitted the judge to consider such information. Thus, § 1045-a was not ex post facto. The court also determined that relevant evidence, including the probation report, was admissible before the sentencing jury. The court also held that the trial court properly performed its obligation to charge the jury as to the law that related to the possible release on parole of a person sentenced to life imprisonment. The confessions of defendants were not required to be excluded because defendants were not previously informed of their right to counsel or that their statements could be used against them, because there was no request for representation by an attorney when defendants were interrogated by the police and there was no attorney who was refused admittance. However, the court held that a hearing on the voluntariness of defendants' confessions was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the trial court subject to hearings by the trial court on the voluntariness of defendants' confessions.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder, first degree -- jury proceeding to determine sentence -- after defendants have been found guilty of murder in first degree, proceeding may be conducted before jury to determine sentence, and nonlegal evidence may thereupon be presented as to circumstances and defendants' history (Penal Law, § 1045-a, subd. 3), including their prior homicides and their other crimes; no burden of proof on either side; such procedural change not ex post facto -- court correctly instructed such sentencing jury that person who is sentenced to life imprisonment may be paroled after 26 years and 8 months (Penal Law, § 1045-a, subd. 4; § 1945, subd. 6; Correction Law, § 230, subd. 2) -- in murder trial proper, hearing should be held by Judge on voluntariness of defendants' confessions -- accordingly, judgments which were rendered on verdicts of death are modified to extent of directing hearing by Judge on voluntariness of defendants' confessions.

1. After a trial has been completed and a verdict has been rendered of guilty of murder in the first degree, a proceeding may be conducted for a determination by the jury as to whether the sentence should be life imprisonment or death (Penal Law, § 1045-a). In such a proceeding, "evidence may be presented by either party on any matter relevant to sentence including, but not limited to, the nature and circumstances of the crime, defendant's background and history, and any aggravating or mitigating circumstances. Any relevant evidence, not legally privileged, shall be received regardless of its admissibility under the exclusionary rules of evidence." (Penal Law, § 1045-a, subd. 3.) This new statute merely changes the procedure. Whatever evidence would have been relevant in the case of a probation report, or could be considered by a sentencing Judge, is properly admissible before the sentencing jury, including nonlegal evidence of prior homicides and other crimes of the defendants; and there is no burden of proof on either the prosecution or the defense. This procedural change does not make this an unconstitutional ex post facto statute.

2. Subdivision 4 of section 1045-a of the Penal Law requires the court to charge the sentencing jury on "the law relating to the possible release on parole of a person sentenced to life imprisonment." In this case, each of the two defendants had been found guilty on two counts of felony murder, since two victims had been killed. The court (a) correctly instructed the sentencing jurors that, if they saw fit, they could direct life sentences on each count; (b) correctly refused to charge that they could recommend that such life sentences run successively rather than concurrently (cf. Penal Law, § 2188); and (c) correctly instructed the jurors that it is possible for a defendant who is sentenced to life imprisonment to be paroled after serving 26 years and 8 months (Penal Law, § 1945, subd. 6; Correction Law, § 230, subd. 2).

3. In the murder trial proper, it was not necessary to exclude defendants' confessions on the ground that the police had not previously informed the defendants of their right to counsel or that their statements could be used against them. There was no request for representation by attorney when the defendants were interrogated by the police, nor was there an attorney in attendance who was refused admittance. However, a hearing should be held by the Judge on the voluntariness of the confessions, under the holding of the United States Supreme Court in Jackson v. Denno (378 U.S. 368).

4. Accordingly, the judgments of conviction, which were rendered on the verdicts of death, are modified to the extent of directing a hearing on the issue of the voluntariness of the confessions which were introduced as a part of the People's case at the trial ( People v. Huntley, 15 N Y 2d 72), and, as so modified, affirmed.

350 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Atlantic Gulf & Pacific Co. v. Gerosa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


16 N.Y.2d 1; 209 N.E.2d 86; 261 N.Y.S.2d 32; 1965 N.Y. LEXIS 1726


April 12, 1965, Argued  
June 10, 1965, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which confirmed a determination of respondent Comptroller of the City of New York (comptroller) determining the tax imposed on the taxpayer under the New York City Compensating Use Tax Law.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that this was a proceeding to review the determination of the comptroller imposing a compensating use tax upon a dredge and some pipe lines belonging to the taxpayer, a West Virginia corporation having its principal place of business in New York City. The taxpayer appealed on constitutional grounds. Despite the broad wording of the statute, the taxpayer contended that, because the tax was, in terms, based upon the "consideration given" for personal property, its sole purpose was to eliminate sales tax avoidance by imposing a use tax identical with the sales tax but only on property purchased outside the city and brought into the city within a reasonable period after purchase. However, the court held that in the light of other provisions of the use tax statute, it was clear that the tax was not based only upon the retail selling price to the taxpayer. The court further rejected the taxpayer's contention that the use tax was an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that a use tax did not hamper the transactions of interstate commerce or discriminate against them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which confirmed the determination of the comptroller in imposing a tax on the taxpayer under the New York City Compensating Use Tax Law.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- New York City compensating use tax -- foreign corporation with principal place of business in New York City bought personal property in another state and used it in other states for eight years, and then used it in New York City for six weeks, and then used it in another state; New York City Comptroller assessed compensating use tax on value, not on purchase price, of property thus used in city; order of Appellate Division confirming such determination affirmed -- since no other jurisdiction has imposed sales tax or use tax on these items, there is no problem of burden of multiple taxation on interstate commerce -- tax for six weeks' use not unfair.

1. A West Virginia corporation, which was engaged in the business of dredging rivers and harbors, had its principal place of business in New York City. The corporation bought a certain dredge in Maryland, and used it for about eight years in South Carolina, Florida, and Virginia, then used it in New York City for about six weeks, and then moved it to Connecticut. Its home port was designated at New York, N. Y., in its registration with the United States Coast Guard. The corporation also employed within the city, in order to haul the dredged material, certain used pipe and pontoon line equipment which it had originally bought outside of the city. The New York City Comptroller subsequently assessed against the corporation a compensating use tax of about $ 33,000, plus penalties and interest, based not on the original purchase price but on the value of the dredge and equipment which were used "for any length of time" within the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § M46-15.0, subd. 1; § M46-28.0, subds. 1, 5; N. Y. City Sales and Use Tax Reg., art. 2 [F]; see, also, Administrative Code, §§ M46-18.0, M46-19.0, M46-21.0). An order of the Appellate Division confirming the City Comptroller's determination is affirmed. The compensating use tax statute is intended not only to eliminate avoidance of the sales tax statute; it is also intended that the tax may be imposed on the use within the city of property purchased and long used elsewhere. Furthermore, one of the purposes of a city use tax is to enable city retail sellers to compete on terms of equality with retail sellers in other states or cities who are exempt from a sales tax or any corresponding tax burden.

2. Appellant corporation acknowledges that there is no evidence that any other jurisdiction has imposed a sales tax or a use tax on these items. Hence this case presents no constitutional problem of the burden of multiple taxation on interstate commerce (cf. U.S. Const., art. I, § 8). Nor is a tax for six weeks' use unfair. And, since such tax is not based solely on the purchase price, the corporation's arguments concerning legislative powers and equal protection of the laws are not relevant.
