378 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Balaban v. Rubin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


14 N.Y.2d 193; 199 N.E.2d 375; 250 N.Y.S.2d 281; 1964 N.Y. LEXIS 1146


April 2, 1964, Argued  
May 7, 1964, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimants appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's order nullifying a zoning plan developed by respondent school board for a new school for violating N.Y. Educ. Law § 3201.


OVERVIEW: When a new school was completed, the school board zoned the district to provide for racial balance. The trial court held that the zoning plan violated § 3201 because, had the new school not been built, the claimants would have attended a different school in their own "neighborhood." The appellate court reversed, finding that § 3201 was on its face and from its history and plain purpose an anti-segregation statute only, which was .not to be construed so as to invalidate a zoning plan because the plan accomplishes integration. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court's judgment that the zoning plan did not violate § 3201. The zoning plan excluded no one from any school and had no tendency to foster or produce racial segregation. Also, the school board had express statutory power to select a site for a new school and to determine the school where each pupil was to attend, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 2556, 2503(4)(d). There were no oppressive results and no child had to travel farther to the new school than he would have to go to get to his "neighborhood" school.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order reversing the trial court's nullification of a zoning plan for a new school on the basis that the school board unlawfully zoned the district to provide racial balance.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- segregation -- plan for establishment of new junior high school, which resulted in school population of approximately one-third negro, one-third Puerto Rican and one-third non-Puerto Rican white, did not violate statute (Education Law, § 3201), which provides that no one shall be refused admission into or excluded from any public school on account of race, creed, color or national origin -- zoning was not arbitrary, capricious or unreasonable.

1. Section 3201 of the Education Law provides that: "No person shall be refused admission into or be excluded from any public school in the state of New York on account of race, creed, color or national origin." Junior High School 275, located in the borough of Brooklyn, was authorized by the Board of Estimate of the City of New York for the purpose of relieving overcrowding in several existing junior high schools. The first zoning map for the new school was rejected by the Board of Education because of the failure so to draw the zone as to prevent any measure of de facto segregation of negro and Puerto Rican students. Under that plan the enrollment would have been 52% negro, 34% Puerto Rican and 14% non-Puerto Rican white. A new proposal was then formulated with the result that the school population would be approximately one-third negro, one-third Puerto Rican and one-third non-Puerto Rican white. The statute is in no way violated by this plan, nor was there any other legal impediment to its adoption. The plan excludes no one from any school and has no tendency to foster or produce racial segregation.

2. The question whether there is an affirmative constitutional obligation to take action to reduce de facto segregation is not in this case.

3. The zoning is not arbitrary, capricious or unreasonable. The Board of Education has express statutory power to select a site for a new school and to "determine the school where each pupil shall attend" (Education Law, §§ 2556, 2503, subd. 4, par. d). There are no oppressive results of the choice here made by the board.

 

379 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Claim of George


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


14 N.Y.2d 234; 199 N.E.2d 503; 250 N.Y.S.2d 421; 1964 N.Y. LEXIS 1153


March 23, 1964, Argued  
May 7, 1964, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division, Third Judicial Department (New York), which (1) reversed so much of a decision of the Unemployment Insurance Appeal Board (board) as was appealed from by respondent employer, a car manufacturer, and (2) dismissed the cross appeal of the employees.


OVERVIEW: The employees were a part of a national strike against the employer. The national strike ended, but local strikes continued until dates when each local negotiation concluded. The employees sought unemployment benefits from the time when their strike ended until they were recalled. The court modified the lower court's order that reversed the determination of the board allowing the employees to collect such unemployment. The court found that delays caused by lack of parts and supplies from other idle plants were no part of the termination of a controversy in an establishment that had settled its own controversy. Denial of benefits was strictly limited to the geographic location of the employment even if the industry was highly integrated nationwide. The court upheld the lower court's affirmance of the board's determination that three plants who shared a tract of land should be considered one for purposes of the controversy on the principle that great weight should be given to the board's application of law to facts. Finally, the court rejected the employer's proposal that a strike in one plant should constitute a strike in any affected unit as contrary to the language of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the intermediate appellate court by reversing so much of the order as reversed the decision of the board and reinstated the board determination with appellate costs to the board against the employer. As so modified, the court affirmed the decision of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Unemployment insurance -- benefits -- strike -- suspension of benefits -- Labor Law ( § 592, subd. 1) suspends unemployment insurance benefits to claimant unemployed because of industrial controversy in "establishment in which he was employed"; several local plants of General Motors did not constitute single establishment and employees therein qualified for benefits for unemployment continuing due to lack of essential parts from other General Motors plants resulting from industrial controversy despite settlements of strike in local plants ( Matter of Ferrara [Catherwood], 10 N Y 2d 1, followed) -- Unemployment Insurance Appeal Board properly determined that motor plant, foundry, and forge occupying single enclosed tract constituted one establishment ( Matter of Ferrara [Catherwood], supra, held controlling) -- provision in national collective bargaining agreement that strike in one bargaining unit causing interruption of material flow to another unit be considered strike in affected unit did not override statute's policy and provisions -- statute not unconstitutional impairment of contracts or regulation of interstate commerce.

1. A nationwide strike against General Motors Corporation called on October 2, 1958 was ended within 24 hours by a national settlement which, however, allowed local unions to continue strikes as to local issues. Thereafter, on various days between October 5 and October 28, settlements were reached and ratified at five General Motors plants in New York State, but full employment therein was not resumed until some time thereafter due to a lack of parts from other plants still on strike. Claimants, employees at the five plants, were entitled to unemployment insurance benefits from the date of the local settlement in each claimant's plant to the date of his recall to employment. The Labor Law ( § 592, subd. 1) suspends benefits to a claimant losing employment because of an industrial controversy "in the establishment in which he was employed". However, it cannot be said that the several plants involved in this case constituted a single establishment (Matter of Ferrara [Catherwood], 10 N Y 2d 1, followed) or that claimants' unemployment was not involuntary because they should have foreseen the consequences of idleness in some of the 124 plants of this integrated industrial enterprise. Accordingly, if the controversy in one establishment was terminated, unemployment continuing therein due to lack of essential supplies or parts from another establishment idle because of an industrial controversy qualified for benefits under subdivision 1 of section 592.

2. The Unemployment Insurance Appeal Board determined that the motor plant, the forge, and the foundry at Tonawanda constituted one establishment. The three divisions, although represented by different local unions under different bargaining agreements, occupied a single 150-acre tract within a single fence. Although their administrative structure seems to tend toward separateness, the controlling decision is the holding in Matter of Ferrara ( Catherwood) (supra) that geographic unity is the primary and ordinarily controlling factor in determining the existence of an "establishment" under the statute, and, therefore, the board's determination must be left undisturbed.

3. The national collective bargaining agreement between General Motors and the international union stated that an authorized strike in one bargaining unit which interrupted the flow of material or service to any other unit should be considered an authorized strike in the affected unit. Such provision cannot override the policy of the statute and the statutory definition of the class entitled to benefits and of the conditions under which they are due means an actual settlement within a given establishment regardless of what the national agreement considers to be an authorized strike. The sustenance due the unemployed is not a fit subject of private waiver.

4. Such statute, pre-existing the formation of the collective bargaining agreement and limiting its effectiveness, did not unconstitutionally impair contract obligations. Likewise, the statute did not violate the supremacy clause by invalidly regulating interstate commerce.

Trans-Lux Distributing Corp. v. Board of Regents


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


14 N.Y.2d 88; 198 N.E.2d 242; 248 N.Y.S.2d 857; 1964 N.Y. LEXIS 1261


January 22, 1964, Argued; March 23, 1964, Reargued  
March 26, 1964, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Regents sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which annulled a determination of the Board in a motion picture licensing dispute arising under N.Y. Educ. Law § 122 and brought against respondent corporation.


OVERVIEW: The appellate division annulled a determination of the Board that directed the elimination of two scenes from a film as a condition for granting a license for the exhibition of the film. The grounds for the Board's action rested on the obscenity of two sequences in the picture. The court concluded that (1) a filmed presentation of sexual intercourse, whether real or simulated, was just as subject to state prohibition as similar conduct if engaged in on the street; (2) the nature of films was sufficiently different from books to justify the conclusion that the critical difference between advocacy and actual performance of the forbidden act was reached when simulated sexual intercourse was portrayed on the screen; (3) unlike speech cases, obscenity did not have to be the dominant theme of the work as a whole; and (4) artists were not such favorites of the law that they could ply their craft in the teeth of a declared overriding public policy against pornographic displays.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's judgment in the motion picture licensing dispute and reinstated the determination of the Board.


HEADNOTES

Theatres and shows -- motion pictures -- obscenity -- Board of Regents properly directed elimination of two scenes from picture as condition for granting license for exhibition on ground that they were obscene (Education Law, § 122).

The Board of Regents properly directed the elimination of two scenes from the film "A Stranger Knocks" as a condition for granting a license for the exhibition of the film on the ground that they were obscene (Education Law, § 122).

Hudson Land Corp. v. Temporary State Housing Rent Com.

383 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Essenfeld Bros., Inc. v. Hostetter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


14 N.Y.2d 47; 197 N.E.2d 535; 248 N.Y.S.2d 45; 1964 N.Y. LEXIS 1318


January 7, 1964, Argued  
February 27, 1964, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff transporters appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a decision that had granted summary judgment in favor of the transporters. The transporters' complaint was also dismissed, and a finding was made that defendant State Liquor Authority of the State of New York (Authority) conformed with N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 102(1)(c).


OVERVIEW: The transporters held permits issued by the Authority for carrying alcohol within the state and delivered alcohol to residents who purchased it for their own personal use. The Authority determined such deliveries violated § 102(1)(c). The transporters sought a declaration that they were entitled to such deliveries and an injunction to restrain the Authority from interfering in the deliveries. The Authority acknowledged that the transporters could deliver the alcohol once it reached the state but alleged that the transporters could not deliver the alcohol into the state. The court found that § 102(1)(c) applied to those who were engaged in the liquor business, not to those who purchased liquor for their own use. The court determined that the activity the Authority sought to proscribe was specifically authorized by N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 116(c). The court indicated that the Authority's interpretation had little, if any, impact on the promotion of temperance. The court concluded that residents who purchased alcohol for their own use were entitled to enlist the services of the transporters to deliver the alcohol to them.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order, which was in favor of the Authority. The court reinstated the trial court's judgement, which was in favor of the transporters.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- liquor purchased by travelers abroad -- Alcoholic Beverage Control Law ( § 102, subd. 1, pars. [c], [d]), providing that no alcoholic beverages shall be shipped into State unless consigned to person licensed to traffic in alcoholic beverages, is directed at commercial shipments and does not prohibit plaintiffs from engaging in business of delivering to New York residents alcoholic beverages purchased by residents, for personal use, outside United States and shipped to themselves here.

Subdivision 1 (par. [c]) of section 102 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law provides that "No alcoholic beverages shall be shipped into the state unless the same shall be consigned to a person duly licensed hereunder to traffic in alcoholic beverages", and paragraph (d) of subdivision 1 declares that no common carrier or other person may bring or carry such beverages into the State unless consigned to one so licensed. These prohibitions are directed not at a shipment to a person who has purchased liquor for his own personal use but solely at shipments to those who are engaged in the liquor business and have purchased the alcoholic beverages outside the State for resale. They are not intended to prevent tourists from hiring carriers to transport their liquor for them. The activities of plaintiffs, who are engaged in the business of delivering to New York residents alcoholic beverages which such residents purchased for their own personal use while outside the United States and shipped as consignors to themselves as consignees, do not violate the provisions of section 102 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law.

 

384 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ampco Printing-Advertisers' Offset Corp. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


14 N.Y.2d 11; 197 N.E.2d 285; 247 N.Y.S.2d 865; 1964 N.Y. LEXIS 1352


January 22, 1964, Argued  
February 20, 1964, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers brought actions against appellee city seeking a declaratory judgment to determine the constitutionality of the New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax Law, N.Y. City Admin. Code, § L46-2.0(a). The Supreme Court at Special Term (New York) determined that the law was constitutional. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers brought actions against the city seeking a declaratory judgment to determine the constitutionality of the New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Law, N.Y. City Admin. Code, § L46-2.0(a). The trial court determined that the law was constitutional. On appeal, the taxpayers contended that the tax was a tax on real estate in the sense of a state constitutional provision that limited the amount to be raised by tax on real estate. The court held (1) the tax was not a tax on real estate within the sense of the constitutional provision because it was imposed not on real estate but on lessees of real estate or on those who acquired lesser rights to use someone else's real estate for business or commercial activity, (2) the Due Process Clause of the U.S. Constitution could not be availed of to condemn the statute because the imposition of the tax was not motivated by any other purpose than the raising of revenue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which had determined that the challenged law was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax Law -- New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax Law (L. 1963, ch. 257; Local Laws, 1963, No. 38 of City of New York) is constitutional -- said tax is not tax on real estate -- consequently no violation of New York State Constitution (art. VIII, § 10) -- tax is not ad valorem tax nor imposed on "intangible personal property" nor imposed "because of ownership or possession" of intangible personal property (N. Y. Const., art. XVI, § 3) -- no violation of due process or equal protection.

1. The New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax Law (L. 1963, ch. 257; Local Laws, 1963, No. 38 of City of New York) is constitutional.

2. The enabling act (L. 1963, ch. 257) authorizes the City of New York to impose a tax, such as the State Legislature could impose, "on persons occupying premises in such city for the purpose of carrying on or exercising any trade, business, profession, vocation or commercial activity, measured by the rent paid for the use or occupancy of such premises". The city enacted Local Law No. 38 for the year 1963 imposing the authorized tax, and defining "taxable premises" (§ L46-1.0, subd. 5) as "Any premises in the city occupied, used, or intended to be occupied or used for the purpose of carrying on or exercising any trade, business, profession, vocation or commercial activity, including any premises so used even though it is used solely for the purpose of renting, or granting the rights to occupy or use, the same premises in whole or in part to tenants". Section 10 of article VIII of the State Constitution applying to a "tax on real estate" limits the amount to be raised by such a tax to a percentage "of the average full valuation of taxable real estate" of the city. If the tax under consideration is one imposed on real estate, adding it to the tax already imposed on assessed valuation would admittedly result in a total tax which would exceed the limit permitted by the Constitution. It is not, however, a tax on real estate within the sense of the constitutional provision. It is not imposed on real estate nor on owners of real estate. It is imposed on lessees of real estate or on those who acquire lesser rights to use someone else's real estate for business or other commercial activity. A leasehold is personal property.

3. Section 3 of article XVI of the State Constitution provides that "Intangible personal property shall not be taxed ad valorem nor shall any excise tax be levied solely because of the ownership or possession thereof". The tax here involved is not an ad valorem tax but, even if it were, it would be one on a leasehold which is not "intangible personal property" within the sense of the constitutional provision.

4. Similarly, there is no violation of that part of section 3 of article XVI which provides that no excise tax "shall * * * be levied solely because of the ownership or possession" of intangible personal property. The tax does not apply to mere ownership or possession. It applies only when the leased premises are used for commercial purposes.

5. It is not a violation of due process or equal protection to levy a tax on tenants engaged in commercial activities without levying it on owners who engage in such activities on their own premises, nor is it violative of the constitutional provisions to distinguish between tenants paying an annual rent of $ 2,500 or less and those paying more.

 

385 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wilson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 277; 196 N.E.2d 251; 246 N.Y.S.2d 608; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 809


November 20, 1963, Argued  
December 30, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the lower court denying defendant's motion for a writ of error coram nobis setting aside his conviction for attempted murder in the second degree and his sentence as a second felony offender.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced to prison for a term of 25 to 50 years as a second felony offender. The previous felony judgment was a Florida conviction for murder in the first degree. Defendant petitioned for a writ of error coram nobis, claiming that he had not been advised that he had a right to counsel and was not represented by counsel in the Florida proceeding. The lower court denied his petition. On appeal, the order denying defendant's petition was affirmed. On further appeal, the court affirmed the order. The court held that by their very nature, coram nobis proceedings were required to be brought in the court that entered the judgment under attack. The court concluded that the State of New York was under no duty to provide a forum for defendant to attack his foreign conviction. Moreover, the State could use the Florida conviction at its face value as an effective predicate for multiple offender punishment under N.Y. Penal Law §§ 1941 or 1942.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- coram nobis -- where prior conviction in Florida was used as one of bases for sentencing defendant as multiple offender in this State, he may not, by coram nobis proceeding, question validity of Florida conviction ( People v. McCullough, 300 N. Y. 107, re-examined and followed).

Where a prior conviction in Florida was used as one of the bases for sentencing defendant as a multiple offender in this State, defendant may not, by a coram nobis proceeding, question the validity of the Florida conviction. ( People v. McCullough, 300 N. Y. 107, re-examined and followed.) If defendant's Florida sentence was imposed in violation of the Federal Constitution, he must be afforded some remedy for setting it aside but it is not necessary for New York State to make such a remedy available.

Hartley Holding Corp. v. Gabel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 306; 196 N.E.2d 537; 247 N.Y.S.2d 97; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 814


November 21, 1963, Argued  
December 30, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord appealed the order of Special Term, Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied its application, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, for review and annulment of a determination of respondent City Rent and Rehabilitation Administrator that had denied the corporation a requested rent increase, dismissing the petition for lack of a substantial constitutional question.


OVERVIEW: The landlord argued that its constitutional rights were infringed when it had not been permitted to use as the valuation base the assessed value of its property as equalized and adjusted upward to or toward "actual" value under the New York City Local Rent Control Law, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y41-5.0(g) (1962). The Administrator argued that there was no substantial constitutional question. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that dismissal was proper because while the assessment of the landlord's property may not have been equal to its true value, the same may have been said with equal force of the assessments outside New York City which, were validly adjusted for rent control purposes by use of an equalization. The court ruled that an equalization rate had not purported to measure the ratio of assessed valuation to full value of any individual property. Section Y41-5.0(g) provided for a rent increase if a landlord was earning less than 6 percent of the value of his property, with "value" having been defined as assessed value in effect at the time the application was filed. The court concluded that state rent control laws used equalized assessment values.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in favor of the Administrator in landlord's action for a rent increase.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- New York City Local Rent Control Law -- constitutionality -- that part of New York City Local Rent Control Law (Local Laws, 1962, No. 20 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y41-5.0, subd. g) which provides for rent increase if landlord is earning less than 6% of value of his property and defining "value" as current assessed value in effect at time application filed, not unconstitutional -- no denial of constitutional rights results from use of unequalized assessments in New York City and equalized assessments in other parts of State.

That part of the New York City Local Rent Control Law (Local Laws, 1962, No. 20 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y41-5.0, subd. g) which provides for a rent increase if a landlord is earning less than 6% of the value of his property, "value" being defined as current assessed value in effect at the time the application is filed, is not unconstitutional. The landlord's constitutional rights were not infringed when it was not permitted to use as the valuation base the assessed value of its property as equalized. There was no denial of equal protection of the laws resulting from the use of unequalized assessments in New York City and equalized assessments in other parts of the State, where State rent control is in effect.

 

387 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lewis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 180; 194 N.E.2d 831; 245 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 867; 6 A.L.R.3d 1321


October 8, 1963, Submitted  
November 21, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the County Court of Chautauqua County (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court, which convicted him of the offense of violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180, speeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating the offense of violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180, speeding. The appellate court affirmed the conviction, and defendant sought review. The court held that recklessness was not an element of the offense of speeding. The court held that defendant was guilty under the statute if, in view of existing conditions or actual and potential hazards reasonably to be anticipated, defendant was guilty of ordinary negligence with respect to speed alone. The court held that an information was sufficient to charge a violation of the statute without stating the speed in miles per hour, provided that enough facts and circumstances were set forth in detail so that the trier of fact could have determined that the speed was not reasonable and prudent. The court affirmed the judgments of the lower courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the lower courts, which convicted defendant of speeding. The court held that recklessness was not an element of the offense and that defendant was guilty of ordinary negligence with respect to speed alone if he drove with reasonably anticipated potential hazards. Information was sufficient to charge a violation without stating speed if trier of fact could have determined that speed was not prudent.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- speeding -- constitutional law -- section 1180 (subd. [a]) of Vehicle and Traffic Law, which provides that no person shall drive motor vehicle on public highway at speed greater than is reasonable and prudent under conditions and having regard to actual and potential hazards then existing, not too vague or indefinite and not unconstitutional -- evidence sufficient to show violation of statute.

1. Subdivision (a) of section 1180 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law which provides that "No person shall drive a motor vehicle on a public highway at a speed greater than is reasonable and prudent under the conditions and having regard to the actual and potential hazards then existing" is not too vague or indefinite and is not unconstitutional.

2. Defendant on a slippery, icy street, with knowledge of its slippery nature, started his automobile from a stop on three separate occasions, each time spinning his car around and ending by sliding off the street into a snowbank. He repeatedly operated his automobile with knowledge of the dangerous conditions at a speed which could be found to be greater than reasonable and prudent. The evidence was sufficient to convict him of driving at a speed greater than is reasonable and prudent under the circumstances in violation of the statute.

 

388 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Post Standard Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 185; 195 N.E.2d 48; 245 N.Y.S.2d 377; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 868; 99 A.L.R.2d 434


September 30, 1963, Argued  
November 21, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, newspaper company and employees, challenged an order form the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed an order of the county court that sustained a demurrer to the indictment of contempt of court against the newspaper.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper published a false account of court proceedings. The newspaper and its employees were indicted for the crime of contempt of court. The county court sustained a demurrer to the indictment and the appellate division reversed and directed a reinstatement of the indictment. The newspaper and its employees appealed. The court reversed the lower court's order and dismissed the indictment. The court reasoned that the intent to defy the dignity and authority of a court was a necessary element of a criminal contempt. The court did not see how the false publication, on its face as charged in the indictment, in any way affronted the dignity and authority of the court or impeded its proceedings in anyway. The court stated that it was merely an error in reporting testimony of an accused. The court held that mere errors in reporting, where no willfulness was alleged, were not usually considered a sound basis for contempt proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision which reversed the trial court's order that sustained a demurrer to the indictment against the newspaper for contempt of court.


HEADNOTES

Contempt -- criminal contempt -- indictment charging defendants with contempt of court (Penal Law, § 600, subd. 7) for publishing allegedly false statement made by person in judicial proceeding should be dismissed in absence of allegation of intent to deny dignity and authority of court.

1. An indictment which charged defendants with the crime of contempt of court (Penal Law, § 600, subd. 7) for publishing an allegedly false statement made by a person in a judicial proceeding that a named police officer had beaten him and knocked him to the ground should be dismissed. Under the statute, the false or grossly inaccurate publication must intentionally assail the dignity and authority of the court. The indictment does not allege an intent to make a false or grossly inaccurate publication or to deny the dignity and authority of the court.

2. While ordinarily an indictment in the language of the statute is not susceptible to demurrer on its face, the rule is not fixed and invariable where circumstances dictate otherwise.

Blaikie v. Power


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 134; 193 N.E.2d 55; 243 N.Y.S.2d 185; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1019


August 22, 1963, Argued  
August 22, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voter sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), in favor of respondent New York City Board of Elections, which (1) reversed the trial court's order declaring unconstitutional parts of New York City, N.Y., Charter § 22(c), (d), issuing injunctive relief; and (2) dismissed the proceeding under N.Y. Elec. Law § 330.


OVERVIEW: The voter challenged New York City, N.Y., Charter § 22(c), (d), the provisions relating to the election of council members at large, which provided that a voter could only cast one vote even though two at large council members were to have been elected from the voter's borough. The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court that found the election system constitutional and dismissed the petition. The court determined that this plan, designed to increase minority representation, was similar to the proportional voting plan that was upheld in the past. The plan treated all voters alike and was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court, which reversed the trial court's finding that the city election laws were unconstitutional, was affirmed without costs.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- councilmen at large -- constitutional law -- section 22 of New York City Charter which, in providing for election of 10 additional councilmen at large to City Council, permits each voter to vote only for single candidate of two councilmen to be elected from each borough, not unconstitutional ( Johnson v. City of New York, 274 N. Y. 411, controlling).

Section 22 of the New York City Charter provides for the election to the City Council of one councilman from each senate district in the city and 10 additional councilmen at large, two to be elected from each of the five boroughs, and limits each voter to vote for a single borough-wide candidate. It also provides that a party (or independent body) may nominate only one candidate for councilmen at large in each borough. Section 1 of article II of the New York State Constitution reads in part as follows: "Every citizen of the age of twenty-one years, who shall have been a citizen for ninety days, and an inhabitant of this state for one year next preceding an election, and for the last four months a resident of the county, city, or village and for the last thirty days a resident of the election district in which he or she may offer his or her vote, shall be entitled to vote at such election in the election district of which he or she shall at the time be a resident, and not elsewhere, for all officers that now are or hereafter may be elective by the people, and upon all questions which may be submitted to the vote of the people." Section 22 of the Charter does not violate this constitutional provision. ( Johnson v. City of New York, 274 N. Y. 411, controlling.) Nor does it offend against any provision of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution.

St. Clair v. Yonkers Raceway, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 72; 192 N.E.2d 15; 242 N.Y.S.2d 43; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1024


May 6, 1963, Argued  
July 10, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the taxpayer's class action suit in which he sought to find the Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law unconstitutional where it resulted in granting $ 42,000,000 to respondent racetrack.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer sought to obtain a judgment declaring that the Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law was an unconstitutional misappropriation of taxpayers' funds where its effect was to grant an additional $ 42,000,000 to a racetrack. The trial court dismissed the taxpayer's complaint for lack of standing, and the lower court affirmed. On further appeal, the court adhered to the rule of law that held that New York state courts denied the right of a taxpayer to bring before a court for review the acts of another department of government simply because that taxpayer was one of many such citizens and taxpayers. The only other state to adhere to that rule of law was New Mexico. The taxpayer had argued that the constitution was a people's document, and the hypothesis that a taxpayer held no "interest" in state expenditures was little more than a legal fiction. In affirming, the court concluded that it could not be squared with the generally accepted doctrine that a taxpayer on the municipal level, where the issues were rarely of comparable importance, did possess the requisite direct and immediate interest to warrant his bringing an action to challenge the expenditure of municipal funds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's affirmance of the trial court's dismissal of the taxpayer's complaint to determine that the Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law was an unconstitutional misappropriation of taxpayers' funds.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- right to challenge constitutionality -- citizen-taxpayer who is not aggrieved is without standing to challenge constitutionality of section 45-a of Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law.

A citizen-taxpayer who is not aggrieved is without standing to challenge a statute's validity and, accordingly, the complaint seeking judgment declaring that section 45-a of the Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law (L. 1956, ch. 837) violates the constitutional prohibition against the expenditure of public funds to private parties (N. Y. Const., art. VII, § 8) was properly dismissed on the ground that plaintiff lacked capacity to sue.

2. Since, in reversing the judgment of conviction, the County Court stated that the jury's finding on the issue of scienter was against the weight of the evidence, a new trial is necessary.

397 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stover


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 462; 191 N.E.2d 272; 240 N.Y.S.2d 734; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1184


February 26, 1963, Argued  
May 9, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendants appealed judgments of the County Court of Westchester County (New York), which affirmed their convictions for violating local ordinances, and denied defendants' claims that their nonverbal protest was protected under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Defendant property owners constructed a clothesline in their front yard as a nonverbal protest of taxes. Plaintiff city enacted an ordinance prohibiting clotheslines or other devices for hanging clothes in a front or side yard abutting a street; providing for the issuance of a permit when practical difficulty or unnecessary hardship in drying clothes elsewhere on the premises existed; and granting a right of appeal if a permit was denied. Defendants were denied a permit, but neither removed the clothesline nor appealed the permit denial. Defendants were charged and convicted of the ordinance violation. Defendants claimed that the ordinance, as applied to them, was an unconstitutional interference with U.S. Const. amend. I and V. The court held that the ordinance was a proper exercise of police power because it did not impose an arbitrary standard of beauty or conformity on the community, nor did it cause undue hardship to defendants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions, holding that, as the ordinance did not impose arbitrary or capricious standards on the community and caused no undue hardship to defendants, it was a constitutional exercise of plaintiff's police power.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- ordinance prohibiting clotheslines -- constitutional law -- defendants properly convicted of violation of ordinance prohibiting erection and maintenance of clothesline in front or side yard abutting street -- ordinance valid as attempt to preserve residential appearance of city and property values by banning unsightly clotheslines -- ordinance not unconstitutional infringement of freedom of speech.

1. In 1956 defendants placed a clothesline, filled with old clothes and rags, in the front yard of their home as a form of "peaceful protest" against the high taxes imposed by the city. During each of the five succeeding years, defendants added another clothesline. The city enacted an ordinance prohibiting the erection and maintenance of clotheslines or other devices for hanging clothes or other fabrics in a front or side yard abutting a street, but provided for the issuance of a permit for the use of such clotheslines if there is "a practical difficulty or unnecessary hardship in drying clothes elsewhere on the premises". An application by the owner of the property for such a permit was denied. Judgments convicting defendants of violation of the ordinance were properly affirmed.

2. The ordinance may be sustained as an attempt to preserve the residential appearance of the city and its property values by banning, insofar as practicable, unsightly clotheslines from yards abutting a public street. Although based on what may be termed aesthetic considerations, it prescribes conduct which offends sensibilities and tends to debase the community and reduce real estate values.

3. The ordinance is not invalid as applied to defendants as an unconstitutional infringement of their freedom of speech. Assuming that their action constituted a form of speech within the meaning of the First Amendment, the right is subject to such reasonable regulation as is provided by this ordinance. The city may proscribe conduct which incites to violence or works an injury on property, and that such prohibition has an impact on speech or expression, otherwise permissible, does not necessarily invalidate the legislation.

 

398 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Babcock v. Jackson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 473; 191 N.E.2d 279; 240 N.Y.S.2d 743; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1185; 95 A.L.R.2d 1


January 23, 1963, Argued  
May 9, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs in all courts, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), affirming an order granting defendant's motion to dismiss the underlying complaint, in a negligence action arising from an automobile accident.


OVERVIEW: In appellant's underlying negligence action for injuries arising from an automobile accident which occurred in Ontario, defendant moved to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the law of the place where the accident occurred governed, and asserted that Ontario's guest statute barred recovery. Appellant sought review of the judgment affirming the order granting defendant's motion to dismiss. The reviewing court reversed its prior choice of law rule for torts, which was based on the law of the place of the tort, and held that the applicable choice of law rule should also reflect a consideration of other factors relevant to the purposes served by the enforcement or denial of the remedy. Comparison of the relative "contacts" and "interests" of New York and Ontario in the action made it clear that the concern of New York was unquestionably greater and more direct, and the interest of Ontario was at best minimal.


OUTCOME: The judgment appealed from was reversed, and the motion to dismiss was denied, as the court discarded its prior steadfast application of the choice of law rule based on the place of the tort.


HEADNOTES

Conflict of laws -- law governing tort actions -- traditional choice of law rule that law of place of tort invariably governs is discarded and "center of gravity" or "grouping of contacts" doctrine applied to permit action by passenger against owner and driver of automobile to recover for injuries sustained in accident in Ontario, Canada, despite Ontario guest statute barring such recovery, inasmuch as automobile was garaged, licensed and insured in New York, all parties were New York residents, and Ontario's only relation to occurrence was that accident occurred there.

1. Plaintiff and defendant, residents of Rochester, left that city in defendant's automobile, plaintiff as guest, for a week-end trip to Canada. While driving in the Province of Ontario, there was an accident allegedly owing to the negligence of the defendant who was driving. Plaintiff may bring an action in this State for damages for injuries sustained despite Ontario's statute providing that owner or driver of a motor vehicle is not liable for damages resulting from injury to the guest.

2. The inflexible rule that substantive rights and liabilities are invariably determinable by the law of the place of the tort should be discarded and the "center of gravity" or "grouping of contacts" doctrine adopted. Justice, fairness and best practical results may best be achieved by giving controlling effect to the law of the jurisdiction which, because of its relation or contact with the occurrence or the parties, has the greatest concern with the specific issue raised in the litigation.

3. The action involves injuries sustained by a New York guest as the result of the negligence of a New York host in the operation of an automobile, garaged, licensed and insured in New York, in the course of a journey which began and was to end in New York. The sole relationship of Ontario is that the accident occurred there. The issue involved is not whether defendant offended against a rule of the road prescribed by Ontario for motorists generally or violated a standard of conduct imposed by that jurisdiction, which then would usually have a predominant, if not exclusive, concern, but rather whether the guest, solely because of his status as guest, is barred from recovering damages for a conceded wrong. The determination of that issue, as well as other issues, depends on the law of the jurisdiction, in this instance New York, which has the strongest interest therein.

 

399 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Spatt v. New York


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 618; 191 N.E.2d 91; 240 N.Y.S.2d 605; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1225


Argued April 2, 1963  
May 2, 1963. - Decided


HEADNOTES

Taxation - New York City sales and use taxes - summary judgment dismissing complaint was properly granted in action to declare unconstitutional article 42 of Regulations of City Comptroller of City of New York insofar as it provided that sales tax, imposed by city on personalty, automobile in present action, purchased by its residents within its corporate limits, was to be computed upon net receipts of sale after deduction of trade-in allowance made by vendor, whereas compensating use tax, imposed by city on personalty purchased by its residents outside of its corporate limits, was to be computed upon total selling price, without deduction of trade-in allowance - there was sufficient basis for classification in different tax treatment of trade-in automobiles in transactions taking place outside of City of New York by reason of greater cost of enforcing compensating use tax and greater likelihood of resale of used automobile within City of New York - no other question passed upon.

Noxon Operating Corp. v. Herman


Court of Appeals of New York


13 N.Y.2d 604; 190 N.E.2d 648; 240 N.Y.S.2d 361; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1216


Argued March 27, 1963  
May 2, 1963. - Decided


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant - rent regulation - Special Term found that determination of State Rent Administrator that petitioner's establishment was not hotel within meaning of Emergency Housing Rent Control Law (L. 1946, ch. 274, as amd.) had ample support in record, that prior opinion letter of Local Rent Administrator granting hotel status thereto, being based on assumption of accuracy of facts supplied by petitioner, was subject to reversal after investigation, and that 1961 amendment to above statute (§ 2, subd. 2, par. [b] , as amd. by L. 1961, ch. 337), attacked as unconstitutional by petitioner, was enacted to clarify existing law and made no substantive change in emergency rent policies of state - determination was properly affirmed.
